The Third Beit HaMikdash
צורת בית המקדש
The Third Beis HaMikdash, trans. by Rabbi Eliyahu Touger, Moznaim Pub Corp. 2016
https://www.amazon.com/dp/1885220782/ref=cm_sw_r_cp_ep_dp_8pvHAbJWBG77V

The Third Beit HaMikdash

Introduction

God told the prophet Yechez­kel:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:10-11.</i>
You, son of man, describe the House to the House of Israel so that they be ashamed of their sins; let them measure its outline. If they are ashamed of all that they have done, make known to them the design of the House, and its form, its exits, its entrances, its entire design, and all its ordinances, and all its shapes, and all its laws; and write it before their eyes so that they will preserve its entire design and all its ordinances, and carry them out.
God commanded the prophet to tell the Jewish people to measure out the entire plan and reckoning for the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> so that they would remember its design in their hearts lest <i>Mashiach</i> come on this day and there will be no one to build it – no one aware of the dimensions and details of how to build the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. <i>Rashi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>ibid.:</i>11.</i> interprets the charge in the same manner, commenting on the words, “they will preserve”: “They should study the details of the measures so they will know how to apply them in the Ultimate Future.”
The verse repeats the phrase, וכל צורתיו, “its entire design.” The second time that phrase is mentioned, <i>Rashi</i> writes, “this is not explained.” It is possible that the intent is to refer to a design of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that could actually be pictured, and from which a blueprint could be drawn according to its dimensions. Such a picture would be a preface to understanding the subject matter.
Similarly, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Abarbanel, ibid.:</i>11.</i> appears to say that the prophet was commanded to make a clearly apparent design. Abarbanel explaines that the verse relates that the prophet was commanded to describe four aspects of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>: “the design of the House, and its form, its exits, and its entrances.” These four features parallel four ways that all concepts can be understood. The first approach he mentions is to understand the subject matter in a manner that is almost tangible. This mode of understanding it alluded to with the words, “the design of the House.” Abarbanel also explains that the phrase, “its entire design,” is stated twice to reflect two different ways of understanding. The first time the phrase is used refers to comprehension in an apparent manner, and the second time refers to the basic understanding of the material. Both are forms that are easily comprehensible without deep analysis. From his words as a whole, it can be understood that the prophet was commanded to explain the <i>Beis HaMikdash’s</i> layout in a way that can be visualized.
<i>Radak</i> offers a similar explanation on the words “the design of the House,”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak, ibid.:10</i>.</i> stating: “They should measure the design of the form of the House, preparing it as a sign, for it will be constructed in this manner in the Ultimate Future at the time of the coming of the Redeemer<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mashiach.</i></i> and the Resurrection of the Dead. It should be prepared as a sign, for those who visualize this design will live in this House in the Ultimate Future.” And in his commentary on the words,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 43:11.</i> “and carry them out,” <i>Radak</i> writes that this vision is “a great proof of the Resurrection of the Dead.”
This promise was not only given to Yechez­kel’s generation. After all, why should we of the future generations be denied? Rather, it is a promise of a general nature to anyone who preserves and measures the design as detailed, and who does not live to see the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> rebuilt, that he will merit to be resurrected and to apply his studies in practice in the Ultimate Future. For even though the prophets’ promises were addressed to the people of their time, their messages were also intended for all the future generations who would follow them and who would continue to relate the message until the final generation. This prophecy was stated in the present tense, to promise God’s goodness at an earlier time, thus hastening its realization were the people to merit. Moreover, that tense is appropriate because it is as if God’s words and His promise were already realized and applied from the time they were uttered. <i>Rashi</i> explains this in several places,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shmos</i> 15:1, <i>Yirmeyahu</i> 6:19.</i> when commenting on the fact that prophets often use the past tense rather than the future when relating their prophecies.
Similarly, the <i>Midrash Yilamdeinu</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Another name for <i>Midrash Tanchuma.</i> A teaching similar to that cited above is found in <i>Midrash Tanchuma, Parshas Tzav,</i> sec. 14 and also in <i>Yalkut Shimoni, Yechezkel,</i> sec. 382.</i> comments on the verse, “Son of man, describe the House to the House of Israel,” saying: When the Holy One, blessed be He, revealed the vision to Yechez­kel and showed him the design of the House, its form, and its exits, He commanded him to relate this vision to the House of Israel.
Yechez­kel protested, saying: “My Master, can they build it now as [implied by] Your words, ‘They will preserve its entire design… and carry them out’?”
God answered him, “No, [they cannot build it at present]. Nevertheless, even though they do not build it now, let them study the design of the House and I will consider it as if they were occupied in its construction.”
And the <i>Pesikta</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesikta Rabesi,</i> sec. 16.</i> “Shmuel<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">One of the leading Sages of the Talmud.</i> comments on the verse: ‘If they are ashamed of all that they have done, make known to them the design of the House’: Is there a design of the House at present? The intent is that the Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘since you are involved in the study of its construction, it is as if you are involved in its construction.’ ”
The expression, “you are involved in its construction,” warrants an explanation. God did not say, “You are building it,”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, the standard published text of the <i>Pesikta</i> states, “you are building it.” Nevertheless, the word cited by the <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is found in <i>Yalkut Shimoni, loc. cit.</i> See <i>Likkutei Sichos,</i> Vol. 18, pp. 412, 419 which discusses this point.</i> because that would imply building the structure in its entirety. “Involved in its construction,” by contrast, implies building a portion of it, for there are aspects of this building that are enigmatic, and not explicitly explained. Thus <i>Radak,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 40:13.</i> when describing the measure of the cells around the gate and the chambers of the Sanctuary, quotes our Sages’ statement, “Certain aspects of this prophecy will be interpreted by the prophet Eliyahu in the future,” implying that we are unable to interpret these details at present.
It is true that this quote was stated in the Talmud<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachos</i> 45a.</i> regarding the sacrifices mentioned in Yechez­kel’s prophecies, and was not made regarding the structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Nevertheless, <i>Radak</i> associated this statement with other enigmatic passages in Yechez­kel’s prophecies because they too are not explicit and could be interpreted in several ways. We are thus forced to say that Eliyahu will also explain these passages in the future.
Similarly, <i>Rambam</i> in the first chapter of his <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishhneh Torah, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 1:4.</i> “[The design of] the building that will be constructed in the Future, though mentioned in <i>Yechez­kel</i>, is not explicit or explained.” Based on these ideas, in the passage cited above Shmuel used the expression, “are involved in its construction,” i.e., building parts of the structure, for it is impossible to understand the entire design. However, by studying Yechez­kel’s prophecy, and understanding those aspects of the structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that are clear, it will be considered as if the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was constructed in its entirety.
Even if one would dispute this interpretation and say that Shmuel used the expression, “are involved in its construction,” because it parallels the initial clause in his statement, “involved in the study of its construction,” the concept stated is true. If it were not said, it would be worthy to say it, for it is a logical conclusion: Only when one understands the structure can it be considered as if it was built, for how can one build if one does not understand? Therefore, I based this concept on the wording of the <i>Midrash.</i>
It can be derived from the above discussion that our study of the design of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> must involve thorough and precise understanding. If not, it would be sufficient to merely read about it. Were that the case, one could read Yechez­kel’s description in its entirety, even those portions that are not understood. Why then would it not be considered as if one built the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in its entirety? Instead, the concept is as explained above.
This can also be understood from the term, “involved in its study,” used in the <i>Pesikta</i> cited above. Shmuel does not say that his listeners should “read about it,” i.e. peruse Yechez­kel’s prophecy regarding the structure of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Rather, Shmuel states that his listeners should be “involved in its study,” implying that they should make a laborious effort to understand the details of the Future structure. This same message is implied by the wording of the blessing recited before Torah study.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bach, Orach Chayim</i> 47:3.</i> Such precise understanding is also implied by the prophet’s words, who speaks of “measuring.”
After these words of truth I, who am young of years, and who laps up the words of the wise, after reading the call which God sounded in the ears of the prophet to “describe the House to the House of Israel so that they be ashamed of their sins [and] let them measure its outline” took this call to heart. Perhaps I have the potential to understand some aspect of the vision. Conscious that “the task is not ours to complete, nor may we neglect it,”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avos</i> 2:16.</i> and after reviewing twice and a third time, the words of <i>Rashi,</i> the most prominent of commentators, I realized that it would be very valuable to make a design with an encompassing picture, that would be “a remembrance between my eyes,”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Shmos</i> 13:9.</i> as we were commanded, as explained above. When some of my noble contemporaries saw my picture, they implored me to engrave it with a pen of iron and lead<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Iyov</i> 19:24; i.e., to commit the work to print.</i> so that it will endure for an extended time,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Yirmeyahu</i> 32:14.</i> until the end of time, acquiring for me the merit of the many. For when ten enter the House of Study, none turn to Yechez­kel’s vision of the House, because of the difficulty of understanding his prophecies.
I said to myself, I will not withhold good from its owners.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Mishlei</i> 3:27.</i> Nor have I entered beyond my limits and positioned myself among the great,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Devarim Rabbah</i> 2:7.</i> for I have not taken upon myself to develop new concepts. I have only attempted to explain the words of the commentaries, since their intent was not to obscure, but to explain. My intent was to communicate their interpretation in a manner that is easy to comprehend. Thus no one can protest that I have sought a place to buttress<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Chulin</i> 7a. This expression is often used in Rabbinic works to imply the development of a <i>chidush,</i> a novel insight.</i> beyond my capacity.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The literal meaning of the words is “the finger of one [who could do such a task] is thicker than my loins” (cf. <i>I Melachim</i> 12:10).</i> Everything is clearly open before you. I know that there is no necessity for what I have done here, my handiwork was only for the youth of the Jewish people. No reader should think that I [undertook this project] for my honor or for the honor of my father’s house.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Taanis</i> 20a.</i> I only undertook this project in response to the sincere requests of the friends who listen<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Shir HaShirim</i> 8:13.</i> to the reader’s voice asking to accept the truth from the one who utters it.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam, Shemoneh Perakim,</i> Commentary on the <i>Mishnah,</i> Introduction to Tractate <i>Avos.</i></i>
With the above words, I have fulfilled my obligations to remove any complaints and to reply by showing the reader my work, and revealing what I have done. I have already faithfully acknowledged that I will structure my commentary according to that of <i>Rashi,</i> the most prominent of the commentators, for his commentary reflects the teachings of the <i>Mishnah</i> that describe the building of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
We have found in the works of our Sages three instances where Yechez­kel’s vision was preserved:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i>, Introduction to Tractate <i>Middos.</i></i> the wicket entrances at the sides of the Gate of the Sanctuary,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:2; <i>Tamid</i> 3:7.</i> the measurements of the Altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:1.</i> and the unroofed courtyards.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 2:5.</i> We find a similar concept in the works of <i>Radak,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 40:5.</i> with regard to the eastern wall of the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> as we will explain in our description of that structure, with God’s help.
Similarly, <i>Rambam</i> writes in the first chapter of <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 1:4.</i> that the people who built the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built it “like the structure of Shlomoh and similar to what was explicit in Yechez­kel’s vision.” They did not build the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> entirely according to Yechez­kel’s vision, because – based on prophetic vision – they knew that this was not the time for that structure to be built. Similar concepts are found in <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary:<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 43:11.</i> It was intended that the second entry into <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> led by Ezra resemble the first entry led by Yehoshua. The Jews should have come with power and with miracles, as our Sages commented<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachos</i> 4a.</i> on the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 15:16.</i> “Until Your nation crosses over.” Yechez­kel’s structure was destined for them when they ascended from the exile and their redemption was intended to be eternal, except that sin had an effect. Their repentance was not befitting.
Thus, God intended for the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to be built according to Yechez­kel’s vision, and for the Jews’ redemption to be eternal, were they to have merited this. When they did not merit this, they were not granted any license to construct the building in Yechez­kel’s vision, because the building in Yechez­kel’s vision is designated and structured for an eternal redemption. When the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be built according to this structure, God’s glory will dwell there forever.
Accordingly, in his description of the structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, Yechez­kel relates the prophecy,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:9.</i> “I will dwell among them forever.” And when the Jewish people did not merit having the Divine presence dwell among them during the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, they did not have the license to build it entirely according to Yechez­kel’s vision. Nevertheless, they included certain aspects of Yechez­kel’s vision in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, since his vision could have been actualized at that time. Furthermore, this enabled them to prepare somewhat for the entire building to be realized, were they to merit to turn to God in proper <i>teshuvah</i>.
For this reason, our Sages mentioned three instances in which the building of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> reflected <i>Yechez­kel’s</i> vision. Significantly, these three instances were each in a separate section of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>: the Outer Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This area was known as the <i>Ezras Nashim</i> in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> the Inner Courtyard, and the building of the Sanctuary. These three areas parallel three sections of the Spiritual Cosmos: what is below the sphere of the moon, the <i>galgalim</i> (the heavenly spheres), and the chariot of the Throne of His Glory. The Final <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will seamlessly connect our world with the spiritual realms so that “one will draw close to another and no [foreign and impure] spirit will pass between them.”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The wording is borrowed from <i>Iyov</i> 41:8. The bracketed words “foreign and impure” are additions not found in that verse.</i> This is not the place to elaborate on this concept. Nevertheless, we see that when they built the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, they incorporated one element that resembles the ultimate and eternal <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> into each of its sections.
In the Outer Courtyard, they built unroofed chambers. In the Inner Courtyard, they built the Altar, and in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> itself, they built the wicket entrances to the gates. All of this was done to forge a connection – and to prepare for –the structure that they would build in the Future. True, other portions of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> were also built according to Yechez­kel’s vision of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> as will be mentioned below. Nevertheless, our Sages singled out these three items to allude to the fact that every area of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> contained one element that resembled the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
We have focused on <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary because it reconciles the text in <i>Yechez­kel</i> with the <i>Mishnah’s</i> description of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to the greatest degree possible. For the [fundamental] difficulty in understanding Yechezkel's description of the structure does not stem from the structure itself, but from the fact that the prophet skips from one matter to another. Therefore, in my description, I go from gate to gate, from corner to corner, and from concept to concept according to the structure of the building. This will allow the verses to be easily understood. To cite a parallel, in his commentary to the <i>parshah, VeAtah Tetzaveh,</i> in his explanation of the charge,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 28:6.</i> “And they shall make the <i>ephod,” Rashi</i> writes: “Were I to explain the manner in which the <i>ephod</i> and the <i>choshen</i> were fashioned according to the order of the verses, the explanation would be fragmented…. Therefore, I will describe the manner in which they were fashioned in a way that a reader can proceed quickly, and afterwards, I will explain the verses in the order they appear in the text.”
Since the order of the verses is explained in <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary, there is no need to repeat those explanations. Everything will be understood from our explanations. Nevertheless, I recorded the sources in parentheses to indicate the chapter in Scripture to which my explanations refer.
I structured this text with alphanumerical markers in order.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In Hebrew, each of the letters has a numerical value. The sections of the text composed by <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> correspond to these markers.</i> Those markers appear in the illustration of the entire structure that we composed.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> made a drawing of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> with the numbers he mentioned here. As mentioned, that design was not published in the original text of <i>Tzuras HaBayis</i>, but rather printed separately and included as an insert to the book. It is reproduced as a foldout at the end of the bookץ</i> Thus, the explanations after the section marker <i>alef</i> in this text will correspond to the marker <i>alef</i> in the illustration. The same is true with regard to the other letters.
I have also recorded whatever corrections have to be made regarding the errors in <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary. I have also shifted from the description of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to the clarification of certain errors found in the description of the buildings constructed by King Shlomoh. For I have seen elements in the building of the House of the Forest of Lebanon<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 7:2 and commentaries there, which discuss this building which was constructed by King Shlomoh.</i> and the bases<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>27.</i> which are difficult to understand, I will write their explanations in short.
And<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The following lines were written in verse in the original text.</i> He who wraps Himself in light like a garment,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Tehillim</i> 104:2.</i>
And gave us this Torah,
Ordered in its entirety and preserved.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>II Shmuel</i> 23:5.</i>
May He who instructs sinners in the path [of repentance]<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Tehillim</i> 25:8.</i>
Direct me and support my heart in both light and severe matters,
Enabling our eyes to see [His glory] rest in the building of His Chosen House.
These are the words of the abominable and offensive one who wrote
About the matter and the form for the honor of the members of the company
For the love of You burns in his heart like a fiery furnace,
Yom Tov, who is called Lipmann, the son of Nassan HaLevi Heller, born in the Community of Wallerstein. 
Afterwards, I settled in the sacred and glorious community of Prague, a large metropolis, the capital city. This is where I composed this work.

Preface

“So says God, the Lord, ‘This is the boundary for which you shall divide the land as an inheritance…. You shall divide it as an inheritance, for each [tribe] like his brother.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 47:13-14.</i> These verses imply that the future division of the land will not resemble the original division,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At the time of the entry into the Land after the Exodus.</i> in which each tribe was given a portion according to its population. In the future time, all the tribes will be given equal portions. The division of the tribes’ portions will resemble the rows of a vineyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The wording <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> uses is borrowed from <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Yechezkel</i> 47:14.</i> with each portion spanning from the eastern border of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to the Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 48:1. Generally, the Mediterranean Sea is recognized as the western border of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Nevertheless, the term <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> uses quoted from <i>Rashi, op. cit., okianus</i> usually refers to the ocean. However, in Greek and Roman thought, <i>okianus</i> is also used to refer to the Mediterranean Sea.</i> on the west, as explained in the narrative. No two tribes will be given a portion in the same strip of land.
The verse states “You shall divide it as an inheritance, each one like his brother, as I swore to give it to their fathers.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 47:14. The literal meaning of the words in the verse is “that I raised My hand,” i.e., raised My hand in an oath.</i> Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explains that this verse is teaching that the tribes should not complain that the land is being divided in a different manner from how it was divided originally. With the words, “as I swore to give it to their fathers,” Yechezkel is explaining that God did not swear to give the land to those who originally inherited it. Rather, God swore to give the land to the “fathers,” i.e., the Patriarchs, Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov. Hence, all the tribes have an equal share in their heritage, and all the tribes will receive an equal share in the Land.
With regard to the length of each strip of land, the <i>Sifri, Parshas Haazinu,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 10.</i> states: “What is the intent of the statements,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel,</i> ch. 48.</i> ‘Yehudah, one,’ ‘Asher, one,’ ‘Dan, one’? They teach that in the Future, <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> will occupy a span from the east to the west. The strip to be given to each tribe being 25,000 rods<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the length of the span will run from east to west and its width from north to south will be equal to 25,000 of the measuring rod held by the angel, as stated in <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:5.</i> wide – this is 75 <i>mil.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A Talmudic measure equivalent to a kilometer.</i> <i></i> From where did they derive this measure? From the portion of land to be offered as a <i>terumah.</i> It was equal to one of these tribal portions, as it is written with regard to the <i>terumah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:8.</i> “as one of the portions.” The portion set aside as <i>terumah</i> was 25,000 rods wide, as explicitly stated in the narrative.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The total length of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> in this future era will thus be 975 km, encompassing an area extending from a point near the border between contemporary Turkey and Syria to below Eilat, as depicted on the map on the preceding page. There is a slight difficulty with this explanation, however, because the borders of the land will extend beyond those mentioned in <i>Yechezkel</i>, ch. 48.</i>
To explain: The measuring rod of the angel in Yechezkel’s vision was six oversized cubits long, as evident from the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid</i>. 40:5.</i> “In the man’s hand, there was a measuring rod six cubits long, each [cubit] a cubit and a handbreadth.” An ordinary cubit is five handbreadths long. Each of the measuring rod’s cubits was an ordinary cubit and an additional handbreadth. Thus, each of the measuring rod’s cubits was six handbreadths long. Thus, a length of 25,000 rods is 150,000 oversized cubits. A <i>mil</i> is 2000 cubits. Thus, the width of every strip of land is 75 <i>mil.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">We are forced to say that these <i>mil</i> are oversized <i>mil.</i> To explain: The length of 25,000 rods is 180,000 ordinary cubits, because the measuring rod was six oversized cubits, i.e., 7.2 ordinary cubits and 7.2x25,000=180,000 ordinary cubits, or 90 ordinary <i>mil.</i> See <i>Rashi</i>’s commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 48:1 which also states that these <i>mil</i> will be larger than ordinary <i>mil.</i></i> See sec. 80.
“These are the names of the tribes. In the northernmost corner….”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 48:1. The portions of the tribes the <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> proceeds to list are mentioned in verses 2-8.</i> Dan, Asher, Naftali, Menashe, Ephraim, Reuven, Yehudah.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 48:8 which explains the future division of the land among the tribes and how it is possible that five tribes will be granted a portion in what was originally the heritage of the tribe of Yehudah.</i> “On the border of Yehudah, from the eastern edge to the western edge shall be the portion that is <i>terumah</i> that you shall offer twenty-five thousand rods in width, and in length as one of the other parts, from the eastern edge to the western edge. The <i>[Beis Ha]Mikdash</i> shall be in the midst of it.”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid</i>. 48:8.</i> The narrative continues, stating<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 48:9.</i> that “the portion to be offered as <i>terumah</i> to God,” i.e., for God’s Sanctuary, will be in this strip that is located near the tribal portion of Yehudah. This portion, as a whole, is called the <i>terumah,</i> as will be explained at the end of this treatise.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 80.</i> In the midst<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">But not in its exact center.</i> of the <i>terumah,</i> as one proceeds from east to west, in its northern portion, there will be a strip the length of 25,000 measuring rods with a width of 10,000 measuring rods from the north to the south.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 45:1. This strip is not at the exact center of the <i>terumah</i>, but is located towards the center, away from the edges.</i> “The <i>[Beis Ha-]Mikdash</i>, the Holy of Holies, will be located within [this area].”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 43:3</i>
“Five hundred by five hundred; its perimeter will be square,”<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 45:2.</i> as measured by the measuring rod. This verse describes the area enclosed by the wall surrounding the Temple Mount, which encompasses the Sanctuary, as will be explained below. The additional space beyond this area on either side will be explained at the end of the treatise,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Sections 76-83.</i> with the help of the Rock [of Israel].
Now “Behold with your eyes, listen with your ears, and focus your heart, on all that I am showing you.”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:4.</i> “Behold a wall outside the House all around.”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:5.</i> This wall encompasses the area 500 measuring rods by 500 measuring rods that was mentioned; this is the wall around the Temple Mount.
“He measured the width of the structure,” i.e., the thickness of the wall. “It was one measuring rod and its height was one measuring rod.” Thus, this was a low wall, as the <i>Mishnah</i> states with regard to the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:4.</i> “All the walls that were there were tall with the exception of the eastern wall [to enable] the <i>kohen</i> who would burn the red heifer standing on the Mount of Olives to see the entrance to the Sanctuary.” <i>Rashi</i> also interprets the above verse in this manner. True, from the simple meaning of the above verse, it is possible to understand that the wall around the entire Temple Mount was low, not only the eastern wall. Nevertheless, since the builders of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built their structure to correspond – at least partially – to this Future Building, as we explained at length in the introduction, it is possible to say that this verse refers only to the eastern wall. Therefore, when explaining the above verse, <i>Rashi</i> quotes this <i>mishnah</i> without making a distinction between one structure and another since it is possible to interpret the verse as corresponding to the building of the Second Temple. And indeed, the angel describing the structure to Yechezkel was standing in the east, as can be derived from the context.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:6 describes the angel ascending through the Eastern Gate. Thus, he would be describing the eastern wall.</i>
<i>Radak</i> interprets the verse in the same manner, stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 40:5.</i> The <i>mishnah</i>’s statement, “with the exception of the eastern wall,” refers to the wall around the Temple Mount. Those who ascended from exile built the structure like the vision of the structure that will be built in the Future and that was shown to Yechezkel in certain ways. True, the simple meaning of the verse is that the entire wall was low, for it did not specifically mention the eastern wall, but said simply “Its height was one measuring rod.”
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, by contrast, maintains that the entire surrounding wall was low, stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, <i>Yechezkel, loc. cit.</i></i> “This was for beauty and for attractiveness so that from the outside one could see the entire building without being blocked by that wall.” It is also possible to explain this interpretation based on the rationale given by the <i>Mishnah</i> that the reason the eastern wall was low in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was so that the <i>kohen</i> who burnt the red heifer could see that entrance to the Sanctuary. This rationale is not relevant in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. To explain: Regarding the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> the <i>Mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> “There were 12 steps leading to the Women’s Courtyard,” and the height of each of the steps was half a cubit. Thus, the Women’s Courtyard was 6 cubits higher than the entrance to the Temple Mount. There were 15 cubits to the Courtyard of the Israelites, which was above the Women’s Courtyard. Thus the Courtyard of the Israelites was 7 1/2 cubits above the Women’s Courtyard, and 13½ cubits higher than the entrance to the Temple Mount. The <i>Duchan</i> was 2½ cubits higher than the Courtyard of the Israelites. Thus, the <i>Duchan</i> was 16 cubits above the entrance to the Temple Mount. There were 12 steps from the Temple Courtyard to the <i>Ulam.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Entrance Hall to the Sanctuary.</i> Thus the <i>Ulam</i> was 6 cubits higher than the <i>Duchan</i> and 22 cubits higher than the entrance to the Temple Mount. Now, the gates to the Temple Mount were only 20 cubits high, and there was a rampart built above them. As mentioned above, the entrance to the Sanctuary was 22 cubits higher than the entrance to the Temple Mount. Thus, it was impossible to see the entrance to the <i>Ulam</i> from the east because it was blocked by the ramparts on top of the gate to the Temple Mount. Therefore the builders of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> made the eastern wall lower.
The Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will have 8 steps leading to the Courtyard of the Israelites instead of 15 steps. This means that the Courtyard of the Israelites will only be 10 cubits above the Temple Mount, and the <i>Ulam</i> will only be 18 1/2 cubits above the Temple Mount. Thus, the entire structure will be 3 1/2 cubits lower, making it unnecessary to reduce the height of the eastern wall of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kohen Gadol</i> would burn the red heifer on the Mount of Olives (see the accompanying diagram). He is required to see the entrance to the Sanctuary when doing so. <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is discussing whether it is necessary to lower the wall around the Outer Courtyard to enable him to do so.</i> Hence, we can posit that the wall around the Temple Mount was not low to enable the <i>kohen</i> burning the red heifer to see the entrance to the Sanctuary, but for another reason, as explained by Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel. According to this approach, there is no difference between the eastern wall and the walls on the other sides.
Nevertheless, this interpretation has certain drawbacks. Firstly, the Altar was positioned entirely before the Sanctuary as will be explained in sec. 43. The Altar was – and will be – 9 cubits high without its horns. As we mentioned above, the <i>Ulam</i> was 6 cubits higher than the <i>Duchan</i>. Thus, the Altar will be 3 cubits higher than the steps of the <i>Ulam,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 54a.</i> and will be 21 ½ cubits above the ground at the base of the Temple Mount.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the Altar will prevent the <i>kohen</i> from seeing the entrance to the Sanctuary.</i> Furthermore, the description of Yechezkel’s vision does not describe how many steps there were leading to the Women’s Courtyard and the <i>Ulam.</i> It is possible that the reduction of the height of the stairs to the Courtyard of the Israelites was made up by an increase in the height of the stairs to the Women’s Courtyard and the <i>Ulam</i> respectively<i>.</i> Thus the rationale mentioned by the <i>Mishnah</i> could also apply, as stated above. Therefore, the opinion of <i>Rashi</i> and <i>Radak</i> – that the verse agrees with the <i>Mishnah</i> – remains the most desirable.
It was stated above that in the Future, the perimeter of the Temple Mount, may it be built speedily in our days, will be greater than it was during the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The <i>mishnah</i> states that during the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> “The Temple Mount was 500 cubits by 500 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:1.</i> In contrast, the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be six times as large.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Although 3000 equals 6 x 500, 3000 square is 36 times 500 square. Accordingly, we have omitted some lines from the translation and made a bracketed addition to the Hebrew text.</i> Thus, the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be 3000 cubits by 3000 cubits. Elazar HaKalir<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A renowned composer of <i>piyutim.</i> The phrase cited is from a <i>piyut</i> recited on the second day of <i>Sukkos.</i></i> alluded to this, writing, “The Beloved One extended a line; 3000 measured cubits.” Indeed, <i>Rashi</i> quotes this <i>piyut,</i> liturgical hymn, in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:20.
God revealed His secret to His prophet, explaining to him why the area of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be expanded, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:7-9.</i> “The House of Israel will no longer defile My Holy name, they and their kings, by their harlotry, nor by the carcasses of their kings in their high places, by placing their threshold by My thresholds, and their doorpost by My doorposts,…<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">During the era of the First <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> the kings’ palace was located next to the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i></i> Now, they will distance their harlotry and the corpses of their kings from Me and I will dwell in their midst forever.”<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This verse is implying that the people will have to reach an elevated plane of moral behavior for the prophecy to be fulfilled.</i> These verses teach us that the moral level of the people during the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> era was not fit for the Divine presence to dwell within it for eternity. Therefore the boundaries of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> were not expanded. Instead, that prophecy was left to be fulfilled in the Ultimate Future, as is explained in the introduction.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">p. 12ff.</i>
“On [all] four sides, he measured it. It had a wall all around, 500 measuring rods long and 500 measuring rods wide to separate between the holy and the mundane.”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:20.</i> “And he came to the gateway that faces toward the east.”<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:6.</i> This verse describes how the angel entered the enclosed area of the Temple Mount and came to the eastern gateway leading to the Women’s Courtyard. That courtyard is called “the outer courtyard” throughout the narrative because it is located before the Courtyard of the Israelites. The Women’s Courtyard surrounds and encompasses the entire complex, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 2, 17, and 28. In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, by contrast, the Women’s Courtyard was located only to the east.</i>
It important to emphasize that this enclosure (i.e., the Women’s Courtyard) is not located precisely in the center of the Temple Mount. Indeed, the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:1.</i> teaches, “The majority of its area<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the free space of the Temple Mount in which the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and its courtyards were located. As explained at the conclusion of the text, in the future, the city of Jerusalem will also be located to the south of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> was in the south. The second largest area was in the east, then in the north, and the smallest area was the west. In the area where there was the most space, there more of its functions were carried out.”





Chapter 1

The wall of the Outer Courtyard wall was<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The past tense is used because the vision was already shown to Yechezkel, although the prophecy will not be fulfilled until the future. See <i>Rashi, Yirmeyahu</i> 6:19; <i>Rabbenu Bachaye, Devarim</i> 34:1.</i> 6 cubits thick, as explained with regard to the threshold of the gateway in sec. 3. The length of the wall surrounding the Outer Courtyard on its northern side,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">We have amended the text according to sec. 8. The original edition reads differently.</i> as it ran from east to west, was 317 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 8 below.</i> We refer to this side as the length, because that is the manner of referring to the longer side of a rectangle, as <i>Rashi</i> explains in several places in his commentary.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:11; <i>I Melachim</i> 6:3; 7:6.</i> The width of the Outer Courtyard, as it runs from north to south, was 312 cubits. This will be explained in sections 28 and 74 below, with God’s help. The size of the Women’s Courtyard differs substantially from that of the Women’s Courtyard in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> because during the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> the Women’s Courtyard did not surround the entire building, as we will explain further on.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 17.</i>

Chapter 2

There was a gateway in the middle of the eastern side of the wall surrounding the Women’s Courtyard, which is called <i>Shaar HaEisun,</i> “the Gateway of the Entrance.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:15.</i> The term “e<i>isun</i>” has the same root as the phrase, “<i>asa boker,</i>” “the morning came.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yeshayahu</i> 21:12.</i> This gate was called “the Gateway of the Entrance” because it was the primary gateway for entering and departing the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. One who entered through this gateway would prostrate himself directly opposite the Holy of Holies.
The narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:6.</i> mentions that the angel “ascended its steps.” In his commentary, <i>Rashi</i> refers to the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> which relates that there were twelve steps leading to the Women’s Courtyard. Each step was half a cubit high and half a cubit wide. Indeed, all of the steps in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> had these dimensions. The only exception was the steps leading to the <i>Ulam.</i> This is <i>Rashi’s</i> version of the <i>mishnah.</i> However, it seems that <i>Rambam’s</i> version of the text did not include the words “except the steps to the <i>Ulam.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See his commentary to the <i>Mishnah.</i></i>
<i>Radak</i> explains that all of the references to the Eastern Gateway refer to the gateway to the Temple Mount, and not to the gateway to the Outer Courtyard. Nevertheless, it is logical to assume that even according to his understanding, the gateway to the Women’s Courtyard was built in this manner. By studying the description of the gateways on the northern and southern sides, we can infer what the gateway on the eastern side was like. <i>Radak</i> explains that the verse mentions the eastern gateway to the Temple Mount, and the northern and southern gateway to the Outer Courtyard. The description of one should be applied to the other and vice versa.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, although <i>Rashi</i> and <i>Radak</i> differ regarding the interpretation of the verse, they agree with regard to the dimensions of the gate in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i>

Chapter 3

The narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i> continues,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:6.</i> “And he measured the threshold of the gateway,” i.e., the width of the doorposts that cover the thickness of the wall. “It was one measuring rod wide and the [other] threshold was one measuring rod wide.” “And he measured the width of the entrance to the gateway; it was 10 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>11.</i>
The narrative continues:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>15.</i> <i>V’al pnei HaShaar HaEisun…chamishim amah. Rashi</i> explains that the words <i>v’al pnei</i> mean “and the height”. Thus, the verse should be translated as, “And the height of the Entrance Gate was 50 cubits.” He states: “From the fact that the other gateways mentioned in the narrative were 50 cubits high, I derived that the words <i>al pnei</i> here refer to a height of 50 cubits.” <i>Rashi</i> also interprets the term <i>al</i> as referring to height in <i>I Melachim</i> 7:6, וְאוּלָם עַל פְּנֵיהֶם, which he understands as meaning, “He made a high vestibule above the entire building.”
Concerning the height of this gateway, <i>Radak</i> writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:15.</i> I am amazed at [<i>Rashi’s</i>] interpretation, for nowhere in the narrative is a measure for the height of the gateways mentioned. Similarly, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there was no gateway that was 50 cubits high. All of the gateways were 10 cubits wide and 20 cubits high, except for the gateway to the <i>Ulam</i> (the Entrance Hall),<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> which was 40 cubits high. [Moreover,] where [in Scripture] is the term <i>orech</i> used to refer to height?<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Further in the narrative, in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:36, <i>Rashi</i> interprets the words <i>orech chamishim amah</i> as referring to a height of 50 cubits.</i> <i>Orech</i> always refers to one of the coordinates of area. The term <i>govah,</i> by contrast, refers to height or depth.
The fact that the measure of the pillars used in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh is referred to as <i>komah</i> in the book of <i>Melachim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:15.</i> but as <i>orech</i> in <i>Divrei HaYamim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Divrei HaYamim</i> 3:15.</i> does not contradict the above. In <i>Divrei HaYamim,</i> the pillars were measured as they lay on the ground [before being erected in the building]. Therefore, they are described by the term <i>orech,</i> “length.” [In contrast, in <i>Melachim,</i>] the pillars are described as they stood, therefore, the term <i>komah,</i> “stature,” is used.
<i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation can be explained as follows: <i>Rashi</i> interprets the first words of the verse, <i>al pnei</i>, as meaning “the height of”. Thus, the verse is discussing height, and the term <i>orech</i> must similarly mean height. Why does <i>Rashi</i> interpret <i>al pnei</i> as “the height of”? <i>Pnei</i> is derived from the word <i>panim</i>, which is often translated as “face” or “surface.” Since a person’s face is part of the upper portion of his body, <i>Rashi</i> understood that this verse is referring to height.
Alternatively, the word <i>pnei</i> can be derived from the term <i>pinah,</i> “corner.” Thus the prophet Tzephania<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tzephania</i> 1:16.</i> speaks of <i>pinos hagevohos,</i> which <i>Radak</i> interprets as meaning, “the high towers.” In his <i>Shorashim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaShorashim, shoresh pen.</i></i> <i>Radak</i> justifies his interpretation by explaining that towers are built with tall stones.
Based on <i>Radak</i>’s explanation that <i>orech</i> refers to length, the recurring statement that the <i>orech</i> of the structure’s different elements was 50 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:25, 29, 33, 36.</i> can be explained as follows: As I understand it, the height of all the structures was measured when they were lying on the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">With this paragraph, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is reconciling <i>Radak’s</i> interpretation with that of <i>Rashi.</i> Ultimately, both commentaries are discussing the height of the structures. <i>Radak</i> uses the term “length,” however, because the height of the structures were measured when they were lying on the ground, as <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> proceeds to explain.</i> Thus, when explaining the statement that the angel speaking to Yechez­kel held “a linen cord in his hand and a measuring rod,”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:3.</i> <i>Radak</i> comments that the cord was used to measure large measures of length on the ground.
We interpret that verse as meaning that the angel held the cord in his hand so that he could easily take accurate measures of the objects’ heights. It would have been much more difficult to measure objects that were more than one measuring rod high when the objects were upright, and might have required the angel to measure them several times. The angel would have to mark the place where the measure ended so that he could place the measuring rod there, and measure it again, repeating the process several times. Measuring the structures with the cord avoided this repetitive process. True, an angel was speaking to Yechezkel, and Yechezkel was witnessing a vision from above; there was no actual physical measuring done. Nevertheless, it can be assumed that the vision reflected the most practical way of measuring the structures. Therefore the cord was very long so that it could be used to measure even the highest structures at one time. Afterwards, the cord was laid on the ground and its length was measured with the measuring rod. Measuring the length of the cord on the ground is a far easier process than measuring an object’s height.
If this explanation does not sufficiently explain why the term “length” is used to describe a measurement of height, we can still accept <i>Rashi’s</i> explanation despite the textual anomaly. There was certainly a deep, hidden reason why the text was written this way, for there is neither a small nor a large element in this awesome vision that does not have an underlying reason of untold depth.
Nevertheless, there is still a question regarding <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation. <i>Rashi</i> writes that all the gateways in the narrative were this height, yet the text does not support this statement.
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel justifies the questions raised by <i>Radak</i>, but ultimately agrees that <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation is more compatible with the simple meaning of the text.
It was taught:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> “All the gateways had gates except the gateway to the <i>Ulam.</i>”

Chapter 4

“There were three cells of the eastern gateway on one of its sides, and three on the other. All three were of the same measure.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:10.</i> These cells were located outside the gateway, close to the wall around the courtyard, 2 1/2 cubits away from the inner space of the gateway on either side. This is indicated by the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12.</i> that states <i>U’g’vul lifnei ha’ta’os amah echas v’amah echas</i>, “There was a space of one cubit by one cubit before the cells.” In addition, the vestibule would extend 1 1/2 cubits beyond its gateway, as will be explained shortly.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 5-6.</i> We translated the word <i>g’vul</i> as “space,” based on <i>Rashi</i> who interprets it as meaning “empty space.” The 1 1/2 cubits where the vestibule would extend is also part of the empty space before the cell. Thus, in addition to the 1 1/2 cubits of the vestibule’s frame, there was, as the verse states, “a space of one cubit before the cells.” Thus the cells were located 2 1/2 cubits from the inner space of the gateway.
The wall of the cell was 5 cubits, as it is written: “The cell was one measuring rod long and one measuring rod wide, between the cells” – i.e., the wall between the cells – “was 5 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:7.</i> Thus the space from the edge of the roof of the cell on one side of the gateway to the edge of the roof on the other side was 25 cubits. This sum is derived as follows: The thickness of the wall on either side was 5 cubits, for a total of 10 cubits. The walls are included in our calculation of the space between the edges of the roofs, because the roof did not cover the wall. Rather, it only covered the empty space called “the cell.” Adding the 2 1/2 cubits on either side that we discussed above, brings the total to 15 cubits. The inner space of the gateway was 10 cubits. Thus there were 25 cubits between the edge of the cell’s roof on one side of the gateway to the edge of the roof on the other side of the gateway. This is the meaning of the verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>13.</i> “He measured the gateway from the roof of the cell to the roof [of the opposite cell] as 25 cubits.”
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes: <i>Radak</i> asks: Why does the verse state, “He measured the gateway”? It should have stated, “He measured from the roof of the cell to the roof of the opposite cell,” without mentioning the gateway. To me, this does not present a question, because while he is measuring from the roof of the cell to the roof of the cell, he is, as a matter of course, measuring the gateway between them.
The latter verse concludes, “an entrance opposite an entrance,” which teaches that the chain of three cells on either side had entrances to this empty space,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to the space before the gateway.</i> one opposite the other. One would enter the first cell through this entrance and then proceed to the second cell and then to the third cell. The second and third cells did not have entrances to the outside.
“There were narrowing windows to the cells”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>16.</i> and to their sidewalls towards the inside of the gateway. The term “sidewalls” refers to the structures at the sides of the two entrances to the chain of cells that opened to the gateway. The verse continues, stating that the windows were “all around facing the inside,” i.e., to the wall, to the inside of the Women’s Courtyard.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they extended through the wall that surrounded the courtyard and opened to its inside (see <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:15).</i>
In his commentary to the verse, “There were… windows to the cells,” <i>Rashi</i> writes that these sets of three cells did not have cells built on top of them. In his commentary to the Talmud<i>,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Basra</i> 61a.</i> <i>Rashbam</i> writes: “The cell was one measuring rod, i.e., 6 cubits, long. This refers to the middle level.” Thus, <i>Rashbam</i> understood that the cells resembled the <i>tzelaos,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechez­kel</i> 41:6-9.</i> the side-chambers that surrounded the Sanctuary. The <i>tzelaos</i> had other side-chambers built upon them, as will be stated in sec. 64. <i>Rashbam</i> maintains that the same pattern applied with regard to the cells at the gateway, since it seems from that Talmudic passage that the side-chambers and the cells are identical.
The simple interpretation of the verses does not appear to support <i>Rashbam</i>’s interpretation. For were there to have been other levels of cells, the prophet would have mentioned them, as he mentioned the three levels of the side-chambers. With regard to the cells, by contrast, the prophet only states: “There were three cells of the eastern gateway on one of its sides, and three on the other,”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>10</i> and “There were 5 cubits between the cells.”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>7.</i> The intent of the latter verse is that the wall separating the first cell from the second cell, and the second cell from the third cell, was 5 cubits thick. Since we know that the cells were next to each other, why does the verse state, “There were three cells… on one of its sides”? To teach that there was a line of three adjacent cells and no others. Thus, the narrative does not mention other cells built upon these cells as it does regarding the side-chambers, indicating that there were no such cells, as <i>Rashi</i> states (<i>Mahadura Basra</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahadura basra</i> means “later edition.” Before this text was printed, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> reviewed the galleys. When doing so, new insights came to mind which he added at the back of the text, marking where they should be inserted, In the present printing, we have incorporated them in those designated places.</i>
None of the cells in the entrance to the courtyards is mentioned in the description of the building of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This difference between the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and Yechezkel’s prophesy is even more significant when learning <i>Radak</i>’s interpretation, because <i>Radak</i> maintains that there were also cells in the wall around the Temple Mount.

Chapter 5

<b>SECTIONS 5–6</b>
“He measured the vestibule (<i>ulam</i>) of the gateway from the inside.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:8.</i> This verse is teaching that this vestibule is located inside the gateway, unlike the other vestibules which were like anterooms on the outside, e.g., the <i>Ulam</i> before the Sanctuary, or the vestibules before the Chamber of the Israelites. This vestibule, and similarly all the other vestibules of the gates to the Women’s Courtyard, were inside the gateway. The angel measured the thickness of the walls on the right and the left of this vestibule, and they were “one measuring rod” thick. The vestibules had doors and doorposts at their edge, as the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>7.</i> “The doorpost of the gateway on the inside of the vestibule of the gateway was one measuring rod” wide.
The walls of the vestibules were positioned a cubit-and-a-half from either side of the inner space of the gateway.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the walls of the vestibule were 1 1/2 cubits away from the inner space of the gateway. Thus, the inner space of the vestibule was 13 cubits wide.</i> Thus, the measure of the expanse of the vestibule from north to south was 13 cubits: There were 10 cubits inside the gateway, and 1 1/2 cubits between the wall of the vestibule and opening of the gateway on either side. This is implied by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:11.</i> “The length of the gateway was 13 cubits.”
The wall of the vestibule was 6 cubits thick, and it was positioned 1 1/2 cubits from the inner space of the gateway. The wall of the cell was 5 cubits thick, and it was positioned 2 1/2 cubits from the inner space of the gateway. Thus the inner space of the cell was positioned 6 cubits from the inner space of the vestibule. This is the meaning of the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12.</i> “The cell,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the cells and the vestibule were on different sides of the gate, the prophet mentions one in connection with the other.</i> six cubits on one side and six cubits on the other side.” Even though the narrative mentioned the dimensions of the cell previously,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>7.</i> here, the verse is saying that both the southern and the northern cells were positioned 6 cubits from the inner space of the vestibule. <i>Radak</i> offers a similar interpretation.
“He measured the width of the vestibule of the gateway as 8 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>9.</i> This refers to the extent that vestibule juts out from east to west. The passage states:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>15.</i> <i>Al lifnei ulam hashaar…chamishim amah</i> – “The height of the vestibule of the gateway was 50 cubits.” In this verse, the word <i>al</i> means “height.”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 3 above.</i> The vestibule had narrowing windows.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>16.</i>

Chapter 6



Chapter 7

“Its pillars were 2 cubits.” The pillars were round, treelike structures made from hewn stone. They stood at the end of the vestibule, with one attached to the right wall and the other attached to the left wall. These pillars took the place of doorposts. The diameter of each one was 2 cubits. Thus, including the pillars, the vestibule jutted out 10 cubits from east to west. Each of the pillars was 60 cubits high.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:14.</i> At the top of each pillar, was a coronet resembling a palm tree, as stated in the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>16.</i> “On each pillar, a palm-like coronet.”

Chapter 8

I<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Yechezkel’s vision (see <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:20), the angel proceeded from the eastern gate to the northern gate. Hence, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> follows that pattern in this text.</i> already wrote above<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 1.</i> that the northern and southern walls of the outer courtyard were 317 cubits long. More details concerning these walls are discussed in sec. 74. It is logical to assume that these walls were a measuring rod (i.e. 6 cubits) thick, because the dimensions of this gateway are the same as the dimensions of the first gateway. The text relates that the first gateway’s threshold covered the thickness of the wall, and was 6 cubits thick.

Chapter 9

On the northern side, after proceeding 151 cubits from east to west, the prophet saw a gateway. Although this figure is not explicitly mentioned in Scripture, the calculation can be easily calculated. As explained in sec. 22, the width of the outer courtyard from its eastern end to the eastern wall of the inner courtyard was 100 cubits. The eastern wall of the inner courtyard was 6 cubits thick, for all the walls of the courtyard were 6 cubits thick, as explained above.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 8.</i> The inner courtyard was a square measuring 100 cubits by 100 cubits, as will be stated in sec. 36. There was a gateway in the center of each side. Each gateway was positioned directly opposite one of the outer wall’s gateways. Since each gateway was 10 cubits wide, the beginning of the gateway on the north side of the inner courtyard would thus begin after 45 cubits of the courtyard’s inner space as measured from east to west. Thus we can calculate the position of the northern gateway of the outer wall, which is positioned opposite the inner wall. It begins after the 100 cubits of the outer courtyard, the 6 cubits of the inner wall, and the 45 cubits of the inner space of the inner courtyard, i.e. the gate would begin after a total of 151 cubits.
All the above is summarized by the verses,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:20-22.</i> “He measured the length and the width of the gateway that faced north…. It had three cells on one side and three on the other…. Its vestibule was the same measure of the first gateway, 50 cubits high. The width [from the edge of the roof of the cell to the edge of the roof of the second cell] was 25 cubits. Its windows, its vestibule, and its palm-like coronets were the same as those of the gate that faced east.”
“They would ascend to it by seven steps.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.,</i> 22.</i> <i>Radak</i> and Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explain that the steps mentioned here were necessary to accommodate the difference in height between this courtyard and the Courtyard of the Israelites. It is as if the verse is saying that people leaving this courtyard would ascend seven steps to the Courtyard of the Israelites.
<i>Rashi</i>, however, explains that the steps mentioned in this verse refer to steps outside the gateways, and were built to accommodate the difference in height between the Women’s Courtyard and the outer area of the Temple Mount. In my opinion, this verse should be understood according to <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary: that the steps are outside these gateways. Although the narrative does not mention the number<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The word number is missing in the text of the <i>Tzuras HaBayis</i> here. Nevertheless, it was included in a later version of the text that <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> wrote as a commentary to the Yechezkel's vision. We are forced to say that this is the intent, because the steps to the Eastern Gate are mentioned in <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:6, as stated in sec. 2.</i> of steps to the Eastern Gate, based on the <i>mishnah,</i> we stated that there were twelve steps.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 2, which refers to <i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> It is, however, possible that there were only seven steps on the northern and southern sides, because the mountain could have had a greater incline on its eastern side.
“Its vestibules were toward the inside,” i.e., the vestibule was further inside than the steps (i.e. the steps were outside the vestibule). Thus, one would ascend the steps before entering the vestibule.

Chapter 10

The southern wall of the outer courtyard was 317 cubits long and 6 cubits thick, like the northern wall described above.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 8; see also sec. 1.</i> More details concerning the outer courtyard’s southern wall will be explained in sec. 74, with God’s help.

Chapter 11

Similarly, after proceeding 151 cubits along the wall on the southern side,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 9.</i> the prophet saw a gateway, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:24-26.</i> “[God]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Scripture does not mention God’s name. By mentioning God's name, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> may be trying to emphasize that it was God, and not the angel, Who led Yechezkel through the vision of the Third <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> took me towards the south. Behold, there was the Southern Gate. He<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The angel.</i> measured its pillars and its vestibules, and they were the same as these measures.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., those of the Northern Gate.</i> It had windows and vestibules around it like these windows. Its height<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The verse uses the term <i>orech,</i> see sec. 3 above.</i> was 50 cubits. The width [from the edge of the roof of the cell to the edge of the roof of the second cell] was 25 cubits. Its stairway had seven steps. Its vestibule was further inside than them. It had palm-like coronets, one on one side and one on the other, on its pillars.”

Chapter 12

The western wall of the Outer Courtyard is not mentioned in the Scripture. It is, however, logical to assume that its length and thickness were the same as those of the eastern wall.
It is possible to explain that the western wall is not mentioned in the narrative because the prophet did not see a gate there, and when describing the courtyards, he only mentions the gates and not the walls. Similarly, when describing the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the <i>Mishnah</i> does not mention any gate on the western wall.
The sixth chapter of <i>Kaftor Va­Ferach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The first Hebrew book focusing on the geography and historical background of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, written by Ishtori Haparchi. (5040-5115; 1280-1355 CE).</i> which focuses on the special <i>mitzvos</i> practiced in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, mentions a gate on the western wall that was called <i>Parbar.</i> I do not know the source for these statements. Our text of the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 1:4.</i> follows the approach of the Sages,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Shekalim</i> 6:2.</i> who do not mention any such gate. Moreover, our Sages interpreted the Scriptural term <i>ulaparbar</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Divrei HaYamim</i> 26:18.</i> which could be understood as meaning, “and to the <i>Parbar,</i>” as meaning <i>k’lapei bar,</i> “and on the outside.” Thus, according to the <i>mishnah</i>, the word <i>ulaparbar</i> is teaching that the Levites stood guard outside of the walls of the courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, as stated in Tractate <i>Tamid</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamid</i> 27a.</i>
It could be argued that every place where the Levites would stand guard outside the walls of the courtyard, was located outside one of its gates. If so, there was also a gate on the west, for they would guard there, behind the <i>Kapores.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., behind the Holy of Holies.</i>
The Levites would stand guard outside a gate so they would easily be able to enter the place they were guarding. Indeed, guards normally stand at entrances. The Levites stood guard outside the gates, rather than inside them, so they would be able sit down, rather than stand the entire night. (Only kings from the House of David were allowed to sit inside the gates of the Courtyard.) The Levites were, however, permitted to walk inside the gates at night, as they were during the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if necessary. Under ordinary circumstances, Levites were not permitted to enter the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> beyond the <i>Duchan.</i></i> True, the gates to the Courtyard were locked every night. It is, however, possible that the gates had smaller wicket entrances at their sides, or that the Levites had keys to each gate. The fact that the Levites also stood watch at the corners of the Courtyard and the corners of the wall around the Temple Mount, and yet there were no gates there, does not contradict the above explanation, for there were gates on each side of the wall. For this reason, <i>Kaftor VaFerach</i> maintains that there was also a gate on the western side for the Levites guarding outside that wall. According to his logic, the <i>mishnah</i> does not mention this gate in its reckoning, because it was not used as a doorway, since, as the <i>mishnah</i> states,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:1.</i> little use was made of the western area of the Temple Mount. It can be said that the narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i> does not mention this gate for the same reason.
This rationale could also explain a later <i>mishnah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>6.</i> which states that there were thirteen gates to the Courtyard. When enumerating the gates, the <i>mishnah</i> includes two gates on the western side that are nameless. These gates were nameless because they were not used to enter or exit the compound. When explaining that <i>mishnah,</i> <i>Kaftor VaFerach</i> felt the need to cite two other gates in the western wall because otherwise, according to the conception in that text, there was only the <i>Parbar</i> Gate on this side – and it had a name. That text, therefore, counts the Gate of the <i>Ulam</i> and the Gate of the <i>Heichal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even though these gates were in the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, and not in the wall around the Courtyard, <i>Kaftor VaFerach</i> counts them among the 13 gates.</i> among those 13 gates, stating that they did not have a specific name.
I wonder what forced <i>Kaftor VaFerach</i> to offer such an interpretation. The Sage who authored the <i>mishnah</i> saying that there were thirteen gates obviously differs with the Sage who maintains that there were only seven gates. Thus, just as he counts an additional gate in the south and in the north even though there were no such gates according to the Sage who maintains that there were seven gates, so too, he maintains that there were two more gates in the west that did not have a name. These gates were located behind the <i>Kapores</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., behind the Holy of Holies.</i> These were not the Gate of the <i>Ulam</i> and the Gate of the <i>Heichal,</i> for it is very difficult to include these gates in this reckoning even though they are to the west of the other gates to the Courtyard.
Although with the <i>Parbar</i> Gate, there are fourteen gates, this is not a problem. The same question could be raised according to the Sages who maintain that there are seven gates, for with the <i>Parbar</i> Gate, there are eight. We see that the <i>Parbar</i> Gate is never included in the reckoning. Rabbeinu Shemayah’s commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:6.</i> supports this interpretation. When explaining the opinion that there were thirteen gates, Rabbeinu Shemayah states that there were two gates to the west, behind the <i>Kapores.</i> Nevertheless it is logical to assume that since the prevailing opinion in the <i>Mishnah</i> counts only seven gates and does not count any gates in the west, there were no gates there. The reason why there were no gates in the western wall is that little use was made of that portion of the Temple Mount.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:6.</i> Hence, there was no need to enter or depart from that direction.
Similarly, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes that there was no gate in the western wall of the Temple Courtyard, and gives two other reasons for this: a) So people would only enter the compound facing the Sanctuary. Thus, they could bow down facing the Sanctuary, and see its entrance. This is not possible when entering the compound from the west. b) One is obligated to bow down when entering the Temple Courtyard. One entering from the west will bow down facing east, i.e., opposite the sun. God would distance us from such conduct because it resembles the abominations of the non-Jews.
This explains why, in his vision of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, Yechezkel did not see a gate in the west. Therefore, he did not mention the western wall, because he only mentioned walls which had a gate.
“And He brought me into the outer courtyard.”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:17.</i> Until this point, the prophet was walking around the Temple Mount to see the cells on the outside of the gate. At this point, he entered the courtyard.
“Behold, there were chambers and a balcony.” The verse is teaching that there was a loft that projected outward on the top of the walls of the courtyard, and there were chambers built upon it.
