This balcony surrounded the entire outer courtyard, as stated in ch. 42.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:3.</i> The chambers that were near the cells (which will be explained in sec. 71) were positioned opposite the balcony, in the northwest and the southwest sides of the complex. Since the prophet mentions that the balcony began at the eastern gate,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i> 40:18.</i> it can be concluded that the balcony ran along these 3 sides at least. This can also be inferred from the phrase, “all around.” This is also reflected in the commentary of Rabbeinu Shemayah cited below. It is logical to assume that the balcony also encompassed the west side.
This balcony was built at the top of the outer wall of the courtyard. Its floor was the same height as the top of the gates, i.e., 50 cubits, as stated in sec. 3 above. Wherever there was a gate in the wall, the balcony would be interrupted by the gate. This is implied by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i></i> “The balcony was on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, “on the shoulder.”</i> of the gates,” i.e., at their side, and not above them. The balcony was also interrupted by the space above the open chambers that will, with God’s help, soon be described.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 13-16.</i>
The verse relates,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:17.</i> “There were thirty chambers on the balcony.” In contrast, the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> did not have a balcony with chambers on top of its walls. It is possible to say, however, that there was something similar, as the <i>mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Middos</i> describes:<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:5.</i> “Originally, [the walls of] the courtyard were even. Afterwards, they built a balcony around them so that the women could observe from above and the men from below. Thus, they would not be intermingled.”
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:5.</i> <b>The first outer courtyard:</b> Our Sages said that during the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this courtyard was called “The Women’s Courtyard,” and they built a balcony for it. I saw in Christian sources that this first courtyard was designated for non-Jews who come to pray in the House of God, as Shlomoh said in his prayer,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 8:41; this prayer was recited at the dedication of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> “And also a foreigner who is not of Your people will come….” There are grounds for those concepts in our sources, because our prophets have foretold that in the Future, many people shall go up to the mountain of God, to the House of the God of Yaakov<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Yeshayahu</i> 2:3; <i>Michah</i> 4:1.</i> to receive His Torah. Therefore, it is appropriate that there be an outer place dedicated for them, one that is furthest removed from holiness. Perhaps, this courtyard will also have a balcony for women, as our Sages taught. The women will then observe from above and the non-Jews will be in the courtyard below.
This sage forgot the teaching in the first chapter of Tractate <i>Keilim</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Keilim</i> 1:8.</i> “The <i>chayl</i> is holier than it (the Temple Mount), for non-Jews and those impure due to contact with a human corpse may not enter there.” Thus, non-Jews were not allowed to enter the entire area of the Temple Mount, merely parts of it. Now, the <i>chayl</i> was positioned after the <i>soreg</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A low partition that served as a line of demarcation, marking off the inner portion of the Temple Mount. See the diagram on the following page</i> but before the Women’s Courtyard. This holds true according to both interpretations of the <i>chayl:</i> that advanced by <i>Rashi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 16a.</i> and of Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, and that advanced by <i>Rambam</i>. Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura comments on the <i>Mishnah’s</i> statement<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:3.</i> that the <i>chayl</i> was 10 cubits, and explains that it was an area 10 cubits wide, positioned before the Women’s Courtyard. <i>Rambam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 5:3.</i> interprets the <i>chayl</i> as being the wall around the Women’s Courtyard, which was 10 cubits wide, stating: “Concerning it, <i>Lamentations</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eichah</i> 2:8.</i> ‘The wall and the rampart mourned.’ ” According to <i>Rambam,</i> “The rampart” refers to the wall of the Women’s Courtyard, which was a low wall, and “the wall” refers to the wall around the Courtyard of the Israelites, which was a high wall. Thus all these authorities maintain that the <i>chayl</i> was before the Women’s Courtyard, and that non-Jews were forbidden to enter there.
Nevertheless, the above concepts are not in contradiction with King’s Shlomoh’s prayer, “And also a foreigner who is not of Your people will come…,” since it can be explained that there was a place for non-Jews on the Temple Mount. That place, however, was before the <i>Soreg.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, in 5632 (1871), an archeological expedition led by Charles Simon Clermont-Ganneau found a stone (seemingly from the <i>Soreg</i>) with an inscription forbidding non-Jews to enter beyond it. The stone is presently located in the Istanbul Archaeology Museums. Such an inscription is also mentioned in Josephus’ <i>Wars of the Jews.</i></i>
It seems to me that this was the purpose of the <i>Soreg:</i> to separate between the area of the Temple Mount that was of greater sanctity – and therefore off-limits to non-Jews and those who were impure due to contact with a human corpse – from the areas of the Temple Mount that had less sanctity. For this reason, the Greek kings broke through this barrier when they entered the <i>Beis HaMikdash:</i> because its purpose was to create a separation, distancing them from the area with greater sanctity. When the Jews merited and conquered the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> from the Greeks, they mended these gaps and ordained that those who enter the Temple Mount bow down once for each of the gaps that were mended as an expression of thanks to God.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahadura Basra.</i></i> This is the courtyard that the Christian sources mentioned. They referred to this portion of the Temple Mount as “the courtyard,” not intending to refer to the Courtyard our Sages spoke about.
It is not appropriate to say that non-Jews had a place in the Women’s Courtyard when they exercised authority over the Temple and only afterwards was their entry restricted. Firstly, it appears that non-Jews were given this place on the Temple Mount with the consent of the Jews and their Sages, not against their will. Moreover, at a time when non-Jews exercised authority over the Temple Mount, who would stop them and prevent them from proceeding further than the Women’s Courtyard? Indeed, this is not what Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel meant. He merely mentioned that non-Jews had a special place on the Temple Mount, which was mentioned by their wise men. Therefore, what I wrote is correct.

Chapter 13

<b>SECTIONS 13–16</b>
“In the four corners of the courtyard, there were roofless courtyards.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:22.</i> They were roofless to allow smoke and steam<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., from the sacrificial foods cooked within.</i> to rise from them. In his commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:5.</i> Rabbeinu Shemayah writes that in Yechezkel’s vision, the balcony that surrounded the courtyard on its walls was interrupted by these courtyards.
“They were forty [cubits] long and thirty [cubits] wide. The four [courtyards] were of the one measure…. There was a row of inlaid stones around all four of them and cooking places were made under these rows,”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:2-23.</i> i.e., a row of inlaid stones jutted out from the wall around the earth. There were holes on top of the stones, with places to put pots. There was empty space below this row of stones where a fire could be kindled beneath the pots placed on top of the stones.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there was a series of cooking hearths around the inner walls of these courtyards.</i>
“And he said to me, ‘These are the cooking places where those who serve in the House will cook the sacrifices of the people.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>24.</i> The sacrifices mentioned in this verse are the breast and the lower leg of peace-offerings, which would not be disqualified by being taken out of the Courtyard of the Israelites. In contrast, this would disqualify the sin-offerings and the guilt-offerings. Hence, a special place was set aside to cook them within the Inner Courtyard, as will be explained in sec. 71.
Tractate <i>Middos,</i> states: [In the Women’s Courtyard,] there were four chambers, [one in each] of the four corners. [Each chamber measured] forty cubits by forty cubits. They were not roofed, nor will they be so in the Future…. What purpose did they serve? In the southeast corner was the Chamber of the Nazirites. There, they would cook their peace offerings and shave their hair, casting it under the cooking urn. In the northeast corner was the Wood Storage Bin. There the <i>kohanim</i> who were physically blemished would check the wood for worms. Any wood that was worm-ridden was disqualified for the Altar. In the northwest corner was the Chamber of Those Stricken with <i>Tzaraas.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tzaraas</i> is a malady similar to leprosy, whose cause is spiritual, not physical (see <i>Rambam,</i> the end of <i>Hilchos Tumas Tzaraas</i>).</i> [The chamber in] the southwest corner: Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov said: “I forgot which purpose it served.” Abba Shaul said: “Wine and oil were stored there and it was called the Oil Storage Chamber.”
These courtyards, which the <i>mishnah</i> calls chambers, were located in the corners of the Women’s Courtyard. In addition to these chambers, there were other chambers built in the middle of the Women’s Courtyard. It would have been fitting to describe the other chambers here. However, since their nature and the order in which they were placed cannot be understood without first explaining the building of the Sanctuary in general and in particular, we will discuss these chambers in sec. 71.
Section 71 also explains that the statement here, that these courtyards were in the corners of the Women’s Courtyard, refers to the courtyard’s inner corners. These courtyards were not on the courtyard’s exterior corners, as depicted by the composer of the picture of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> printed in Manitoba.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is referring to the Manitoba edition of the <i>Mishnah</i> from 5322 (1562 CE). The illustration accompanies Tractate <i>Middos</i> and was prepared by Rav Moshe Kazis, one of the leading sages of Italian Jewry in that era.</i> This drawing is inaccurate, as explained later on in detail. Therefore in the picture of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, may it be built speedily in our days, that we have printed,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned, that design is reproduced as a foldout at the end of this printing.</i> we depicted these courtyards in the inner space of the Women’s Courtyard, two courtyards in the two eastern corners and two courtyards in the two western corners. The location of the courtyards in the western corners differs from their location in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> because, unlike the courtyard in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this courtyard will surround the entire complex and the Sanctuary.

Chapter 14



Chapter 15



Chapter 16



Chapter 17

“And he measured from the [lower] gate…”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:27.</i> of the Outer Courtyard, which was positioned at the bottom of the mountain, “…to the [upper] gate” of the Inner Courtyard, which was positioned at the top of the mountain. The difference in height resulted from the fact that “the Temple complex was not [built] on flat land, but on the incline of the mountain.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 6:1.</i> Therefore, it was necessary to have steps between one gate and the other, as explained in the relevant places. The first measurement was made in the south,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">From the narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:23-24, it might appear that the northern portion of the courtyard was measured first.</i> measuring the empty space in the southern portion of the Outer Courtyard until the southern [wall of] the Inner Courtyard. “And he measured… one hundred cubits.” See sec. 22.
In the second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Women’s Courtyard was not this large, because it ran only from the eastern wall westward.

Chapter 18

The southern wall of the Inner Courtyard was one measuring rod thick, as explained in sec. 8 above. <i>Rashi</i> also states this in his commentary to the phrase, “and before the chambers;”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:4.</i> consult that source. Both the southern wall and the space it surrounded were 100 cubits long.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Explanation is necessary because a wall that surrounds a space on all four sides must be larger than its inner space.</i> The inner space of the Inner Courtyard was a square, measuring 100 cubits by 100 cubits, as will be explained in section 36,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The original printing of <i>Tzuras HaBayis,</i> states sec. 35.</i> with God’s help.

Chapter 19

The gate to the southern portion of the Inner Courtyard was in the center of its wall, opposite the gate of the Outer Courtyard. This is derived from the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel </i> 40:28-29.</i> “And he brought me to the Inner Courtyard through the southern gate. He measured the southern gate according to those measures” – i.e., the angel used the same measures here as he used when measuring the first gate (i.e., that of the Outer Courtyard). “Its cells, its pillars, and its vestibules were of those measures. There were windows in it and in its vestibule all around. It was fifty cubits high<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in sec. 3 above, although the term ארך usually means, “long,” here it means “high.”</i> and twenty-five cubits wide,” i.e., there were 25 cubits between the edge of the cell on one side and the edge of the cell opposite it. “There were palm-like coronets on its pillars and its stairway had eight steps.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:31.</i>

Chapter 20

There was one difference between the structure of the inner gate and that of the outer gate: the vestibules. The vestibules of the outer gate were inside the gate, while the vestibules of the inner gate projected out from the gate into the outer courtyard. The gates were built in this manner so that the vestibules would be opposite each other, and would be attractive. This is implied by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:31.</i> “Its vestibules were toward the Outer Courtyard.”
It is logical to say that the vestibules were built outside the cells of the gate, and not between the cells, because were they to have been positioned between the cells, they would not have been visible from the outside. To explain: The vestibule would project out only 10 cubits. The cell was 6 cubits long, and its outer wall was 5 cubits long. Thus, the cell and its wall would project a cubit beyond the vestibule.
Moreover, the space between the cell’s wall and the wall of the cell opposite it was 13 cubits wide. Every wall of the vestibule was 6 cubits wide. Thus, there was simply no space for the vestibules between the cells. Therefore, it appears that the vestibules for the inner gate were positioned outside the cells.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there were 19 cubits from the beginning of the vestibule to the gate of the Inner Courtyard. The vestibule jutted out 8 cubits like the inner vestibule. Afterwards, there were 11 cubits for the cell and its walls, and only then, did one enter the gate.</i> The vestibule’s wall was built at the edge of the cell’s wall, and opposite the cell’s wall. Furthermore, the vestibule’s wall would project an extra cubit towards the inner space of the gate. This is derived from the fact that the vestibule’s wall was 6 cubits wide, and was positioned 1 1/2 cubits from the inner space of the gate on either side. The gate was 10 cubits wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See secs. 5-6 above.</i> Thus, the vestibule was 13 cubits long, just like the vestibule of the outer gate.
The vestibules would be positioned one opposite the other, making the structure very attractive [see the diagram below]. These vestibules would also have the same dimensions as the vestibules of the outer gate. This is derived from the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:29.</i> “Its vestibules were of those dimensions.” Regarding the vestibules of the outer gate, the verse states:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>11.</i> “The length of the inner space of the vestibule of the gate was thirteen cubits.” This explanation corresponds with <i>Radak</i>’s interpretation of the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12.</i> “The cell was six cubits on one side,” as explained in sec. 6 above.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that both the southern and the northern cells were positioned 6 cubits from the inner space of the vestibule.</i>

Chapter 21

“There were vestibules all around, twenty-five cubits long and five cubits wide.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:30.</i> These vestibules were inside the gate to the Inner Courtyard. We are forced to say this, because outside there were six chambers, whose length, the length of their walls, and the 15 cubits of the width of the gate between the three cells on either side equal 91 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">On each side of the gate, 3 cells and 4 cell walls, totaling 38 cubits. In addition, there were 15 cubits between these two sets of cells. Thus, there were a total of 91 cubits (38+15+38). </i> Now, the entire inner courtyard was only 100 cubits by 100 cubits, as will be stated in sec. 36 and mentioned several times.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, there would not be room for these chambers on the outside of the wall.</i> Instead, these vestibules were located inside the Inner Courtyard around – and indeed, extended into the thickness of – its wall. <i>Rashi</i> also mentions this.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 40:30.</i> Since each vestibule was 25 cubits long, it appears that the prophet saw all these vestibules with one glance, seeing the north, east, and south sides of the courtyard at once. This is alluded to by the words, “all around.” For if the vestibules were located only on the southern side of the wall, the words “all around” would not be appropriate. Furthermore, if the prophet had only seen one side of the courtyard with this glance, he would have only seen two vestibules, one on either side of the gate. Moreover, the part of the wall on each side of the gate was less than 50 cubits long, and it was not long enough to contain two vestibules.
True, in his description of the empty space (<i>hamunach</i>) near the chambers,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See secs. 64-69 below, where this open space is described.</i> <i>Rashi</i> explains<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechez­kel</i> 41:9.</i> that the term “all around,” can be interpreted as “on either side,” as stated in sec. 64. Thus, with regard to these vestibules, one might infer that the prophet’s intent is to say “on either side of the gate.” That, interpretation, however, is not appropriate here. With regard to the open space, there is no alternative but to interpret the verse in this manner even though the interpretation is somewhat forced.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For the Hebrew term <i>soviv</i> implies a circle.</i> With regard to these vestibules, by contrast, there is no need to accept such an interpretation and the term can be interpreted according to its simple meaning.
<i>Radak</i> explains that the combined length of all the vestibules along the wall was 25 cubits. On the phrase, “the width was five cubits,” he explains that the width of each one was five cubits. In this vein, he interprets the phrase “all around” as meaning the vestibules were built “around the wall” or “in its inner space.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>soviv</i> refers to the wall, either the vestibules were around the wall or the wall was around the vestibules.</i> Thus, according to <i>Radak’s</i> interpretation, the prophet may have only seen vestibules along the southern wall. Following <i>Radak,</i> we do not know how the vestibules were built along the northern and eastern walls.
<i>Rashi</i> explains the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:15-16.</i> “the vestibules of the courtyard. Its doorposts, its windows,” as follows: “This refers to the vestibules of the courtyard mentioned above, as it is written: ‘There were vestibules all around.’ ” The phrases “Its doorposts” and the words “its windows” refer to these vestibules. The fact that the verse mentions “the courtyard,” and not “the courtyards,” teaches that this verse is only referring to one courtyard. Thus, this verse is referring to the vestibules located within the Inner Courtyard and not those outside its walls, in the Outer Courtyard.
“Outside the doorpost was wood paneling.”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 41:16.</i> <i>Rashi</i> writes: “All this [paneling] was inside [the Sanctuary]. They would cover it with gold plate, for the entire <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was coated with gold plate and it is impossible to attach gold plate to stone.” <i>Rashi’s</i> comment implies that there was no wood paneling in the vestibules of the courtyard, only in the inner Sanctuary which was described in the verses preceding mention of the vestibules.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in <i>ibid.</i> 41:1-4.</i> The rationale for <i>Rashi’s</i> statement is that it is impossible to say that there was any exposed wood paneling in the complex of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This was a safeguard instituted by our Sages against the transgression of the prohibition:<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 16:21.</i> “Do not plant an <i>asherah,</i> any tree near the altar” of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Thus, there was no exposed wood in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, as stated in the first chapter of Tractate <i>Tamid.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamid</i> 28b; see <i>Rambam, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 1:9.</i> The Torah’s descriptions of the Sanctuary built in the desert, Scripture’s description of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh, and the Talmud’s description of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> only mention walls coated with gold inside the Sanctuary. Therefore, <i>Rashi</i> explains that, “the paneling outside the doorpost,” refers to the walls outside the inner Sanctuary, and not to the walls of the vestibules of the courtyard. Since these walls were not coated with gold, it is impossible for there to be wood paneling there.
There was no parallel to these vestibules in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. However, the <i>mishnah</i> in the first chapter of <i>Tamid</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamid</i> 28a.</i> relates that when building the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> they built an exedra<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A structure with a roof and two or three walls.</i> in the Courtyard of the Israelites. The exedra may have been built as a semblance to the vestibules. This exedra surrounded the courtyard on all sides, as according to our conception of these vestibules in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.

Chapter 22

“And He brought me to the Inner Courtyard via the east.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:32.</i> “And he measured from the lower gate…” to the upper [gate]; “it was 100 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>19.</i> Thus, the width of the Outer Courtyard from its eastern side to the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard was 100 cubits. Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes that the phrase, “Before the Inner Courtyard from the outside,” implies that the measurement was made from the outside and did not include the gate to the Inner Courtyard. The same applies to the southern side, even though Scripture mentions only the east and the north.

Chapter 23

The eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard was 6 cubits thick, as we explained in sec. 8. Its length from north to south was 100 cubits, the same as that of its inner space.

Chapter 24

“And he measured the gate according to those measures.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., with the same measures used for measuring the gate of the Outer Courtyard.</i> Its cells, its pillars, and its vestibules were of those measures. There were windows in it and in its vestibules all around. It was fifty cubits high,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above in sec. 3, although the term ארך usually means “long,” here it means “high.”</i> and twenty-five cubits wide.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechez­kel</i> 40:32-33.</i> This means there were 25 cubits between the edge of the cell on one side and the edge of the cell opposite it. “Its vestibules were toward the outer courtyard. There were palm-like coronets on its pillars, and its stairway had eight steps.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:34.</i>
Tractate <i>Middos</i> states<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:5.</i> that the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> had “fifteen steps leading from [the Women’s Courtyard] to the Courtyard of the Israelites, corresponding to the fifteen “Songs of Ascent” in <i>Tehillim.</i> The Levites would stand on these steps and sing songs. [The steps] were not rectangular, but semi-circular instead.”

Chapter 25

There was a chamber … “on the flank of the eastern gate”, its entrance “facing northward”.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:44.</i> They were “for the <i>kohanim,</i> the keepers of the watch of the altar: they are the descendants of Tzadok, the ones [chosen] from the descendants of Levi to draw close to God and serve Him.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>46.</i>
In the first chapter of <i>Middos,</i> it was taught:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 1:4.</i> “In the east was located the Gate of Nicanor. It had two chambers, one on the right and one on the left. One was the Chamber of Pinchas, the Clothes Butler and one was the Chamber of the Bakers of the <i>Chavitin.</i>” The Clothes Butler would dress the <i>kohanim</i> before their service in the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> and undress them afterwards.

Chapter 26

<b>SECTIONS 26–27</b>
<i>Targum Yonason</i> interprets the <i>hagederes haginah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:12.</i> as “the platform (<i>duchan</i>) of the Levites.” The term <i>gederes</i> implies a structure, a <i>geder</i> (“partition”), of stones on which the Levites would play their instruments and sing. This platform was “on the eastern way,” for the <i>duchan</i> was in the eastern portion of the Courtyard, as stated in the passage in <i>Chronicles</i> that describes the completion of the building of the First <i>Beis HaMikdash:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Divrei HaYamim</i> 5:12.</i> “The Levites of whom all were singers, of Asaf… stood at the east of the Altar.”
The second chapter of Tractate <i>Middos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:6.</i> quotes Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov, “whose teachings were precise and pure,”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 67a. Our translation follows the interpretation of <i>Rambam</i> in the Introduction to his Commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i>.</i> as stating that in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash:</i> “There was a step there, a cubit high. [The platform called] the <i>duchan</i> which had three steps, each half a cubit high, was positioned on this step.” That source also states that the Courtyard of the Israelites was 11 cubits from east to west. Afterwards it states that the step that was 1 cubit high spanned the length of the Temple Courtyard from north to south.
The 11 cubits mentioned immediately above was designated as the Courtyard of the Israelites. The portion of the Courtyard beyond those 11 cubits, in front of the Altar, was designated as the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim</i>. That area was also 11 cubits wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:1.</i> Thus, the entire eastern portion of the Courtyard until the Altar was 22 cubits wide. Similarly, there were 22 cubits between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 3:6.</i> In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the entire portion of the Courtyard until the <i>Ulam</i> was only 76 cubits. The Altar stood in the center of that area, and occupied 32 cubits. That left 44 cubits, i.e., 22 cubits on either side.
In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, may it be built speedily in our days, there will be a total of 100 cubits in this courtyard before the <i>Ulam.</i> When the Altar<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the Ultimate Future, the Altar will measure 32 cubits, as in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i></i> is placed in the center of that area, there will be 34 cubits between the Eastern Gate and the Altar, and 34 cubits between the Altar and the <i>Ulam.</i> The 34 cubits on the eastern side of the Altar will be divided into two parts, one in which the Israelites may proceed and one in which the <i>kohanim</i> will proceed. Each of those areas will measure 17 cubits. The <i>duchan</i> will be placed between these two areas.
True, the area in which the Israelites and the <i>kohanim</i> may proceed in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be 35 cubits shorter than the corresponding area was in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> that area was 135 cubits long, and in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> it will only be 100 cubits long. Nevertheless, the reduction in length will be compensated by an increase in the width of that area. In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this area was 11 cubits wide, while in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it will be 17 cubits wide. Thus, the total area of the additional portion<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the portion beyond the basic area measuring 100 cubits x11 cubits.</i> will be almost twice that of the additional portion in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. To explain: When you subtract an area of 100 cubits x 11 cubits from an area of 100 cubits x 17 cubits, there remains an area of 100 cubits x 6 cubits, or a total of 600 square cubits. With regard to the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, when the area of 100 cubits x 11 cubits is subtracted from its total area, there remains an area of 35 cubits x 11 cubits, or 385 square cubits.
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Abarbanel, Yechezkel</i> 40:27.</i> “In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Courtyard of the Israelites was 135 cubits 1ong and 11 cubits wide. While here (in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>), it will be a square of 100 cubits x 100 cubits, a decisively larger figure.” I find his words difficult to understand, for the area of 100 cubits by 100 cubits in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will also include the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim,</i> the Altar, and the space between the Altar and the <i>Ulam.</i> In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, those areas were included in a total space of 135 cubits x 76 cubits, as mentioned.
It appears that Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel maintains that the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim</i> and the Altar were located beyond the area described here as the Courtyard of the Israelites. According to his understanding, the area measuring 100 cubits by 100 cubits will be solely the Courtyard of the Israelites. The Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim</i> will be in another area. The courtyard (<i>azarah</i>) described in the passage concerning the Altar<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:13.</i> will be an actual courtyard – not as <i>Rashi</i> explains in his commentary to that passage.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In that passage, <i>Rashi</i> and others interpret the term <i>azarah</i> – usually, translated as “courtyard” – as referring to portions of the Altar. As mentioned in the main text, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel and others maintain that its usual meaning also applies here.</i> Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explains this in his commentary to the verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:14.</i> “From the center of the ground until the lower courtyard were two cubits with a width of one cubit, and from the small courtyard to the larger one were four cubits with a width of one cubit.” He refers to the courtyard as the place where the <i>kohanim</i> would stand, and interprets the phrase, “the lower courtyard” as the area to the east of the Altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Because it was lower on the mountain of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> The lower courtyard was also the smaller courtyard. The larger courtyard was the area between the Altar and the <i>Ulam.</i> This courtyard was also the upper courtyard because it was higher up the slope of the mountain. The Altar was positioned between these 2 courtyards.
Similarly, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel interprets the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>17.</i> “The courtyard was fourteen [cubits] long by fourteen [cubits] wide,” as referring to the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim.</i> He states that the verse does not explicitly say to which courtyard – the larger or the smaller one – it refers. Since Scripture mentions the area in which the <i>kohanim</i> can proceed independently,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 43:13-14.</i> Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel therefore maintains that the courtyard that was 100 cubits x 100 cubits was solely for the Israelites.
Nevertheless, <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation that the term <i>azarah</i> mentioned in connection with the Altar refers to the Altar’s foundation and its <i>sovev</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 37 and 39.</i> are based on – and supported by – Talmudic passages. One should not deviate from them left or right. Moreover, even if the term <i>azarah</i> mentioned in connection with the Altar refers to actual courtyards, as Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel maintains, it is still possible that these courtyards are part of the area that was 100 cubits x 100 cubits. It is just that here, the prophet described the area in general and later, he focused on its particulars.

Chapter 27



Chapter 28

“There was a gate to the Inner Courtyard opposite the gate from the north.… He measured one hundred cubits from one gate to another.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:23.</i> From this, it can be derived that the width of the Women’s Courtyard from the north to the south in front of the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard was 312 cubits. The Women’s Courtyard was further extended, surrounding the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> on all sides. There were 312 cubits from its northern wall to its southern wall. To explain how that number is reached: a) there were 100 cubits from the northern wall of the Outer Courtyard to the northern wall of the Inner Courtyard; b) there were 100 cubits from the southern wall of the Outer Courtyard to the southern wall of the Inner Courtyard; c) the area comprising the open space of the Inner Courtyard measured 100 cubits; d) the two walls separating the Inner Courtyard on the north and the south were each 6 cubits wide, totaling 312 cubits. At the beginning of this composition,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 1.</i> I promised to explain this.

Chapter 29

The northern wall of the Inner Courtyard was 6 cubits wide, as explained in sec. 8. That wall measured 100 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The length of the wall does not include its corners. See the picture on p. 80</i> from east to west. The inner space of the courtyard also measured 100 cubits.

Chapter 30

“And He brought me to the Northern Gate. He measured [the gate] according to those measures.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the gate was the same size of the Outer Courtyard gate.</i> It had cells, pillars, and vestibules, and there were windows in it all around. It was 50 cubits high<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above (sec. 3), although the term ארך usually means “long,” here it means “high.”</i> and 25 cubits wide.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:35-36.</i> These verses teach us that there were 25 cubits between the edge of the cell on one side and the edge of the cell opposite it. “Its vestibules were toward” – i.e., projected into – “the Outer Courtyard. There were palm-like coronets on its pillars, and its stairway had eight steps.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:37.</i>

Chapter 31

“In the vestibule of [this] gate, there were two tables on the one side and two tables on the other side to slaughter near them the burnt-offering, the sin-offering, and the guilt-offering.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:39. The verse mentions offerings that are of the highest order of sanctity, קדשי קדשים, which must be slaughtered in the northern part of the courtyard.</i> This verse should be understood as follows:<br><b>“In the vestibule of [this] gate, there were two tables on the one side</b>” on the east <b>“and two tables on the other side”</b> on the west <b>“to slaughter near them</b><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> explains the word אליהם in the verse as meaning עליהם, as <i>Radak</i> states in his commentary. Although עליהם is usually translated as “on them”, it is difficult to imagine sacrificial animals being slaughtered on the tables. <i>Metzudos</i> explains that in this context the word אליהם appears to mean, “next to them,” i.e., the sacrificial animals were slaughtered next to the tables mentioned in the verse. See the interpretation of ועליו in <i>Bamidbar</i> 2:20 by <i>Rashi</i> and others.</i><b> the burnt-offering, the sin-offering, and the guilt-offering.”</b>
These tables were located in a vestibule that extended into the Outer Courtyard. Nevertheless, <i>Rashi</i> explains that the tables shared the Inner Courtyard’s level of sanctity.
“On the flank on the outside on the ascent of the entrance to the northern gate there were two tables and on the other flank, [to the west], there were two tables. Four tables on one side and four tables on the other side near which [the sacrifices] were slaughtered.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:40-41.</i> This verse should be understood as follows:<br><b>“On the flank”</b> towards the opening of this vestibule on the east. <b>“on the outside”</b> of the inner space of the inner entrance to the vestibule, which is positioned towards the Courtyard of the Israelites <b>“on the ascent of the entrance to the northern gate”</b> as one entered the gate and ascended the steps to the entrance to the courtyard, on the eastern flank of the entrance <b>“There were two tables and on the other flank, [to the west], there were two tables.”</b> Thus, there were a total of <b>“four tables on one side and four tables on the other side near which [the sacrifices] were slaughtered.”</b>

Chapter 32

“There was a chamber” – within the inner courtyard, near this gate – “its entrance”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>38.</i> was at the side of this gate, facing the gate. “There, they would wash the burnt-offering.” It appears that this chamber was the butchering area.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the place where the sacrificial animals were skinned and cut into pieces.</i>
It is possible to explain that this chamber opened toward the gate and not to the main area of the courtyard, where the Altar was to prevent the <i>kohanim</i> and the people standing in the courtyard from seeing into it, for there were times when the chamber was soiled with the wastes and blood of the sacrificial animals that were slaughtered and skinned. Alternatively, this chamber may have opened toward the gate so that no one would hear the confessions of the sinners who would bring sin-offerings and guilt-offerings there. A person who brought a sin-offering or a guilt-offering was required to perform <i>semichah,</i> to lean on the sacrificial animal before it was offered as a sacrifice and confess his sins while doing so<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefta, Menachos</i> 10:3.</i> Directly after <i>semichah,</i> the animal would be slaughtered.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachos</i> 93a, b.</i> Thus, the animals were slaughtered near the tables at this gate. <i>Radak</i> and Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak, Abarbanel, Yechezkel</i> 40:39.</i> explain that one side of the courtyard was designated solely for slaughtering the burnt-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The burnt-offerings are not mentioned in the original text. Nevertheless, the omission appears to be a printing error. Precisely because the burnt-offerings were also slaughtered in this area, the identity of the sinners could be protected. An onlooker would not necessarily assume that a person performing <i>semichah</i> on an animal was a repentant sinner; he could have been bringing a burnt-offering.</i> the sin-offerings and the guilt-offerings so that the sinners’ identities would not become publicized.

Chapter 33

In this chamber, “there were four tables [made] of hewn stone for the burnt-offering, one-and-a-half cubits long, one-and-a-half cubits wide, and one cubit high.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:42.</i> The knives for slaughtering the sacrificial animals and the basins in which the blood from the slaughtered animals would be collected, were placed on these tables so that these utensils would be prepared for the <i>kohanim</i> who would slaughter the sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity in the northern portion of the courtyard.
The verse states:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>43. The bracketed phrase was added based on the gloss of <i>Metzudos.</i></i> “And the tables on which the meat of the sacrifices [would be placed].” It appears that the phrase “There they would rinse the burnt-offering”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>38.</i> refers to this chamber, i.e., the meat of the sacrifices was rinsed on the tables inside this chamber. Therefore, the tables were made of hewn stone. Similarly, <i>Middos,</i> ch. 3,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:5.</i> states that there were marble tables between the pillars in the butchering area. Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura states that the entrails of the sacrifices were washed on these tables because marble cools the meat and protects it from spoiling.
In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> there were 8 such tables, as stated by the <i>Mishnah</i> in <i>Shekalim,</i> ch. 6,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shekalim</i> 6:4.</i> “Eight marble tables in the butchering area; the entrails would be rinsed on them.”

Chapter 34

“The hooks, one handbreadth [long].”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:43.</i> In his commentary to this phrase, <i>Rashi</i> explains that these were iron hooks that were embedded in the short pillars<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the verse does not mention pillars with hooks – only hooks on the “House,” i.e., the walls of the Courtyard – based on our Sages’ account of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, <i>Rashi</i> understands that there will also be pillars with hooks in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> that were located in the butchering area in the northern portion of the courtyard. The sacrificial animals were hung on these hooks when they were being skinned. Similarly, the fifth chapter of Tractate <i>Pesachim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 64a.</i> teaches that there were iron hooks embedded in the pillars and the walls of the courtyard. The sacrificial animals were hung from and skinned on these hooks.
It is also taught in the third chapter both of <i>Tamid</i> and <i>Middos:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamid</i> 3:5; <i>Middos</i> 3:5.</i> The butchering area was located to the north of the Altar. In it were 8 dwarf-like – i.e., short – pillars of stone. A square block of cedar wood was affixed to the top of the pillars. And hooks – i.e., iron fork-like spikes – were embedded in the wooden blocks. There were three sets<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary to <i>Middos, loc. cit., Tosafos Yom Tov</i> explains that there were no hooks on the western side of the pillars so that the <i>kohanim</i> skinning the sacrificial animals would not stand with their backs to the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> of hooks in each wooden block, one above the other. Rabbeinu Shemayah<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In Rabbeinu Shemayah’s gloss to <i>Middos, loc. cit.</i></i> explains that the lowest hook was for a sheep to be hung on, the middle one was for a ram, and the upper one was for a bull.

Chapter 35

“Outside the inner gate, there were chambers for the singers, inside the Inner Courtyard, on the flank of the Northern Gate, facing southward.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:44.</i> This verse implies that the Chambers of the Levites who would sing were outside the inner space of the gate’s vestibule, within the Inner Courtyard, on the side of the gate – i.e., behind the gate, as <i>Radak</i> explains. The entrance to these chambers was to the south. This is the meaning of the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>45.</i> “This chamber that faces south is for the <i>kohanim,</i> the guardians of the watch of the House.” Although the verse uses the term <i>kohanim, Rashi</i> comments: “It appears to refer to the Levites who were singers.” Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explains that the Levites are called <i>kohanim</i> in this verse because a <i>kohen</i> is one who serves, and the Levites served through song.
<i>Radak,</i> however, interprets this latter verse as referring to another chamber in addition to the Chambers of the Levites mentioned in the previous verse. Although it is not stated explicitly, the chamber would also face south Thus, <i>Radak</i> explains that the chamber mentioned in the first verse was for the Levites and the one mentioned in this verse was for the <i>kohanim</i>.
In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Chamber of the Levites was located under the Courtyard to the Israelites to the east, and opened to the Women’s Courtyard, as the <i>Mishnah</i> teaches with regard to the eastern gate:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 2:6.</i> “There were chambers under the Courtyard of the Israelites, opening to the Women’s Courtyard, where the Levites would store their harps, lyres, cymbals, and other musical instruments.”

Chapter 36

“And he measured the courtyard. It was square, one hundred cubits long and one hundred cubits wide.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:47.</i> As mentioned many times,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 1 and 26-27.</i> in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this courtyard was 135 cubits wide, the same width as the Women’s Courtyard, which was also only 135 cubits wide. This verse is referring to the Courtyard before the <i>Ulam,</i>that was 76 cubits in length,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there were 76 cubits between the <i>Ulam</i> and the eastern wall of the courtyard.</i> as mentioned in sec. 26.
In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this courtyard surrounded the entire building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, as the <i>Mishnah</i> teaches in <i>Middos,</i> ch. 5:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:1.</i> “The Courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The original text of <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> states “the Courtyard of the Israelites.” The translation follows the wording of the <i>Mishnah</i>.</i> was 135 cubits wide and 187 cubits long.” The 187 cubits are divided as follows: a) the 76 cubits mentioned above, b) the 100 cubits of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, and c) 11 cubits behind the <i>kapores</i> (i.e., behind the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>).
The Courtyard was 35 cubits wider than the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. These 35 cubits were divided equally, with 17 1/2 cubits on the northern side and 17 1/2 cubits on the southern side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, by contrast, this courtyard will only be 100 cubits wide, the width of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The area on the sides of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be included in the external courtyard, as we wrote with regard to the chambers in sec. 71.

Chapter 37

“The Altar was before the building [of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>].”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:47.</i> In sec. 26, it was explained that it appears that in this vision, the Altar stood 34 cubits from the eastern wall of the courtyard and there were 34 cubits between it and the <i>Ulam,</i> the vestibule of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Whether in fact it will be positioned there, in the northern portion of the courtyard, the southern portion or in the middle will be explained in sec. 43, with God’s help.
“These are the dimensions of the Altar in cubits, each cubit [measuring] a cubit and a handbreadth.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:13. I.e., ordinarily a cubit equals 5 handbreadths in measure. These cubits were 6 handbreadths.</i> As I explained in the beginning of this work,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In the Preface.</i> this applied with the exception of the measures of the foundation, the upper portion of the <i>sovev,</i> and the horns. These were smaller cubits, i.e., each comprising 5 handbreadths, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 38 and 42.</i>
“From the center of the ground” – i.e., from the ground – “until the lower <i>azarah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See the end of sections 26-27.</i> – i.e., until the upper surface of the first level, the foundation of the Altar, “were two cubits with a width of one cubit.”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:14.</i>
How was the Altar built?<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 54a.</i> They would bring a square mold made from four beams. Each beam was 32 cubits long and 2 cubits wide. This was the height of the mold.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">More specifically, the mold was slightly larger so that it would produce a surface of that size.</i> They would fill it with stones, lime, tar and molten lead. It would form into a solid block, 32 cubits by 32 cubits and 2 cubits – small cubits – high.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:1.</i>
The foundation did not extend over the entire expanse of 32 cubits by 32 cubits, as stated in Tractate <i>Middos:</i> “The foundation would extend along the entire northern side of the Altar and the entire western side. It would consume one cubit on the south and one cubit on the west.” The intent is that when the square mold for the foundation was made, they would place a filler (i.e., wood or some other substance) in the southeast corner so that it would not be filled with the mixture of stone, lime, and molten lead. Afterwards, the filler would be removed, and that corner would remain empty without a foundation. The empty space would run from the southeast corner along the entire eastern side until the last cubit on the north. Similarly, the empty space would run from the southeast corner along the entire southern side until the last corner close to the west.
I elaborated in the description of the design of the foundation because the printers erred in the picture of the Altar that accompanies <i>Rambam’s</i> Commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i> and depicted the foundation as running along the southern and eastern sides, leaving only one square cubit of open space in the southeast corner. This is an error on their part.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the illustrations drawn by <i>Rambam</i> himself (printed in Rav Kapach’s text of the <i>Mishnah</i>), the foundation is depicted as not running along the southern and eastern sides of the Altar, fitting the conception shared by <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> above. Nevertheless, the early printers did not have those illustrations and produced their own, making the error described above.</i>

Chapter 38

Afterwards, another mold is brought. According to <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Menachos</i> 97b.</i> this second mold measured precisely 30 cubits by 30 cubits. The Talmud states in the first chapter of Tractate <i>Eruvin,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Eruvin</i> 4a.</i> that the cubit of the Altar’s foundation was small.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, in Yechezkel’s vision, a “cubit” usually means a measure equaling 6 handbreadths. However in other contexts, the term “cubit” means a measure equaling 5 handbreadths. In the construction of the Altar, however, some of the “cubits” contained 6 handbreadths and some were “small,” and contained only 5 handbreadths. The Altar in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was also built in a similar manner.</i> <i>Rashi</i> explains in his summation of the matter there, that the Talmud is referring to the cubits used to measure the height of the mold, i.e., the mold’s height was measured using a cubit that equaled 5 handbreadths rather than a cubit equaling 6 handbreadths. However, the width of this mold was measured using a cubit equaling 6 handbreadths. This mold was used to reduce the width of the next level of the Altar, which was one cubit narrower on each side than the 32-cubit wide foundation. Thus, the mold placed on the foundation measured exactly 30 cubits by 30 cubits.
<i>Rambam,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam’s</i> Commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i> introduction to <i>Middos,</i> ch. 3; see also <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 2:6-7.</i> by contrast, explains that both the height and the width of the mold used to reduce the width of the next level of the Altar was measured in 5-handbreadth cubits. Therefore, <i>Rambam</i> explains that the mold was intended to produce a mass measuring 30 cubits and 2 handbreadths by 30 cubits and 2 handbreadths. Since the width of the mold was only 5 handbreadths, there would remain 30 cubits and 2 handbreadths by 30 cubits and 2 handbreadths inside the mold. Together with the mold, there would be 32 cubits to fill up the space of the foundation. <i>Rambam</i> writes that the <i>mishnah</i> states that the mold measured 30 cubits by 30 cubits because it did not want to detail the handbreadths. The height of this mold was 4 cubits. This is alluded to by the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:14. The <i>azarah,</i> usually meaning “courtyard,” is used to refer to the two lower levels of the Altar. The lower <i>azarah</i> (or “the smaller <i>azarah</i>” as mentioned in <i>Yechezkel</i> 43:13) refers to the foundation and “the larger <i>azarah</i>” to the <i>sovev. See the end of sec. 27 above.</i></i> “From the smaller <i>azarah,</i>” i.e., from the upper surface of the lower level, “until the larger <i>azarah,</i>” i.e., the upper surface of the block on it, “were 4 cubits.” This formed the <i>sovev.</i>
Together, the foundation and the <i>sovev</i> were 6 cubits high. Similarly, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> the foundation and the <i>sovev</i> were 6 cubits high. Their individual dimensions were, however, slightly different from the future dimensions of the foundation and the <i>sovev</i> for the <i>mishnah</i> states<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:1.</i> that the foundation was one cubit high and the <i>sovev,</i> five.
This mold was placed on the foundation. It was filled with stones, lime, and molten lead as the first was. The mixture turned into a solid block, 30 cubits by 30 cubits that was attached to the foundation and stood upon it. It was called the <i>sovev</i> because it surrounded and encompassed the entire Altar, in contrast to the foundation, as explained above.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 37.</i>
Thus, the foundation would extend a cubit beyond the <i>sovev</i> on all sides<i>. Rashi</i> maintains that this was an oversized cubit, measuring 6 handbreadths, while <i>Rambam</i> maintains it was an ordinary cubit, measuring 5 handbreadths. This is the meaning of the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:14. </i> “its width, a cubit,” i.e., the width of the foundation was one cubit and it was set in one cubit.

Chapter 39

Afterwards, when fashioning the Altar, they would bring a third mold, which measured 28 cubits and 2 handbreadths by 28 cubits and 2 handbreadths. All authorities agree that the 1-cubit reduction in width made by the <i>sovev</i> was a 5-handbreadth cubit. The <i>mishnah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:1.</i> however, did not detail the number of handbreadths, and only mentions, “28 cubits by 28 cubits.” Similarly, the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:17.</i> “The <i>azarah</i>” – the [next] level – “was 14 in length and 14 in width.” This verse should be understood as meaning that the level above the <i>sovev</i> measured 14 cubits in all directions from its center, a total of 28 cubits by 28 cubits. The handbreadths were not counted. This mold was 3 cubits high. Thus, the upper surface of the Altar was 3 cubits high.
Concerning it, the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>15.</i> “The <i>Harel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i> defines the term <i>Harel</i> as referring to the height of the Altar above the <i>sovev.</i></i> was 4 cubits.” The Talmud<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 53a.</i> explains that the word “<i>Harel</i>” is referring to the “domain of the horns,” i.e., the place for the blood that is to be presented on the corners of the Altar was from the <i>Ariel,</i> i.e., from the upper surface of the Altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi’s</i> definition of the Hebrew term in the verse. Often, Yechezkel uses the term <i>Ariel</i> as referring to the Altar as a whole.</i> downward 4 cubits. Wherever the blood was presented within these 4 cubits, it was considered as having been presented on the upper portion of the Altar. The <i>chut hasikra</i> was located in the center of the Altar to make a distinction between the blood to be presented on the lower portion of the Altar and the blood to be presented on the upper portion of the Altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos, loc. cit.</i> The blood of a sin-offering has to be presented on the upper portion of the Altar while the blood of a burnt-offering has to be presented on the lower portion of the Altar.</i>
It was already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 38.</i> that together, the foundation and the <i>sovev</i> were 6 cubits high. The Altar was 10 cubits high in its entirety, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 42 below.</i> If so, the <i>chut hasikra</i> – which marked the midpoint of the Altar – was positioned at the last cubit of the <i>sovev.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, there would be 5 cubits below it and 5 cubits above it; the upper 5 cubits included the 4 cubits from the <i>Ariel</i> down, as described immediately previously, and one cubit of the horns as described later on.</i> <i>Rambam</i> explains<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam</i>, <i>Middos</i> 3:1; see also <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 2:9.</i> that in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this cubit – i.e., the highest cubit of the <i>sovev</i> – comprised 6 handbreadths. To explain: In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Altar’s foundation was one cubit high. However, this was a 5-handbreadth cubit. The <i>sovev</i> was 5 cubits high, but its cubits were 6-handbreadths cubits. Thus, the foundation and four cubits of the <i>sovev</i> comprised 29 handbreadths. Similarly, the remaining portion – i.e. the final cubit of the <i>sovev,</i> the 3 cubits of the upper surface of the Altar, and the 1 cubit of its horns – also comprised 29 handbreadths. This is because the <i>sovev</i> and the upper surface of the Altar were measured in cubits comprised of 6 handbreadths, and horns were measured in a cubit that was comprised of 5 handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the <i>chut hasikra</i> was in the center of the Altar, both when its height was measured in handbreadths and when it was measured in cubits.</i>
There is, however, a difficulty according to <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding. <i>Rashi</i> agrees that the cubit used to measure the foundation’s height in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was equal to 5 handbreadths. The foundation of the Altar in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, however, will be 2 cubits high. Thus, the placement of the <i>chut hasikra</i> would change.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the <i>chut hasikra</i> would have to be lowered a half a cubit to be in the center of the Altar in handbreadths.</i> It is, however, possible that although the foundation in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be 2 cubits high, only one of these cubits will comprise 5 handbreadths. This can be derived from the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:13.</i> “the foundation, a cubit,” that one of the foundation’s cubits will be an ordinary 5-handbreadth cubit.

Chapter 40

The upper surface of the Altar was a square, measuring 28 cubits by 28 cubits. It was divided by measuring 12 cubits from its center to each side, producing a square 24 cubits by 24 cubits. This was the area where the Altar’s pyres were arranged.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:1.</i> This is alluded to by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:16.</i> “The <i>Ariel</i> was 12 [cubits] in length and 12 [cubits] in width.”

Chapter 41

There was an area 2 cubits wide on all sides of the 24 cubit square described in the previous section. One cubit of this space was designated as the area where <i>kohanim</i> would walk. It was a totally empty area,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the area beyond this square, where the horns were located. They obstructed the movement of the <i>kohanim.</i></i> to allow the <i>kohanim</i> to walk freely without being burnt or tripping over portions of the sacrificial animals or pieces of wood.

Chapter 42

We now have a square measuring 26 cubits by 26 cubits, comprising the Altar’s upper surface. Thus, there was one more cubit of space remaining on each side of the Altar’s surface. The <i>kohanim</i> used this remaining cubit on each side when walking atop the Altar, except that some of this outer perimeter was occupied by the Altar’s “horns,” which were at the 4 corners of the Altar. Each “horn,” measured 1 cubit by 1 cubit. The horns were fashioned by placing a mold, measuring 1 cubit by 1 cubit, and 1 cubit high at each of the corners, and filling it with a mixture of stones, lime, and molten lead.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 37 above.</i> There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis regarding the dimensions of these horns. According to <i>Rashi,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Yechezkel</i> 43:13 and <i>Eruvin</i> 4a.</i> both the cubits of the horns’ height and width comprised 5 handbreadths. <i>Rambam,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 2:6.</i> by contrast, maintained that the only reduction in the width of the Altar’s base was 5 handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 38 above.</i> Accordingly, though the cubit used to measure of the horns’ height comprised only 5 handbreadths, the cubit used to measure their width and length comprised 6 handbreadths. Including the height of the horns, the Altar was 10 cubits high.
Since the horns obstructed the <i>kohanim’s</i> passage around the outer edge of the upper surface of the Altar, it was necessary to provide them with another cubit of space on that surface, on which they could walk freely. Indeed, Tractate <i>Zevachim</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 62a.</i> “Did the <i>kohanim</i> walk between the horns? Instead, say that a cubit was designated where the feet of the <i>kohanim</i> would walk.” On that statement, <i>Rashi</i> comments that it is impossible to say that the <i>kohanim</i> walked between the horns because the horns would prevent them from making the circuit.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahadura Basra.</i></i> The cubit where the <i>kohanim</i> would walk is described in sec. 41, although it is not mentioned in Yechezkel’s narrative.
The horns by contrast, are mentioned, as it is written,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:15.</i> “From the <i>Ariel</i> upward, there were four horns,” and it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>17.</i> “And the edge (<i>g’vul</i>) around it was half a cubit.” The latter phrase refers to the horns. This verse should be understood as meaning, when the area at the edge of the Altar is measured from the center of a horn, it is half a cubit to each side. Thus, each horn measured 1 cubit by 1 cubit in total.
One might object to interpreting the term <i>g’vul</i> as referring to the horns,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This reflects a correction of the Hebrew text which states יסוד, “foundation.”</i> because of the verse referring to the placement of the sacrificial blood on the Altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>20.</i> “And you shall place it on the corners (<i>karnosav</i>)… and on the edge (<i>g’vul</i>) all around.” The word <i>karnosav</i> is referring to the horns. However, if this is so, then what is the word <i>g’vul</i> referring to? It is possible to explain that when the term <i>keren</i> is not mentioned, and <i>g’vul</i> is mentioned, <i>g’vul</i> refers to the horns. When, however, Scripture mentions <i>g’vul</i> and <i>keren</i> together, <i>g’vul</i> has another meaning. To cite a parallel, see <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation of the terms <i>garav</i> and <i>yalefes.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Vayikra</i> 21:20. There, he explains that the meaning of <i>garav</i> depends on the context where it is used. In <i>Vayikra, loc. cit.,</i> since it is used in connection with <i>yalefes,</i> it has one meaning, and in <i>Devarim</i> 28:27 when it is used in connection with <i>cheres,</i> it has a different meaning.</i> Thus, when the term <i>g’vul</i> is mentioned together with the term <i>keren,</i> it refers to the area where the <i>kohanim</i> walk, or the space between the horns, or both of these spaces. Indeed, the <i>Mishnah</i> uses the term <i>g’vul</i> in this manner in Tractate <i>Kilayim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kilayim</i> 3:1.</i> when discussing the <i>g’vul ha’arugah,</i> the area used to walk around a row of produce. Similarly, the <i>Targum Yonason</i> translates the term <i>g’vul</i> as <i>gedanfa,</i> “frame,” when it is used alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Targum Yonason, Yechezkel</i> 43:17.</i> but when that term is used together with the word <i>keren,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Targum Yonason, Yechezkel</i> 43:20.</i> it is translated as <i>techuma,</i> its “area.”

Chapter 43

“[One who ascends] its ramps would turn to the east.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:17.</i> On this verse, <i>Rashi</i> comments: “The ramp via which one would ascend [to the Altar] was in the south, so that one who ascends on it would be facing east. For whenever one would turn, he would turn to the right. When the ramp is placed to the south, one who ascends on it and turns to the right, would thus turn to the east.” His words are reflected by our Sages’ statements in Tractate <i>Zevachim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 62b.</i>
In Tractate <i>Middos,</i> the <i>mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:3.</i> “The ramp was positioned to the south of the Altar. It was 32 cubits long and 16 cubits wide from east to west.” The ramp was a slanted walkway, and was inclined at an angle. The <i>kohanim</i> used it to ascend to and descend from the Altar. The <i>kohanim</i> could not ascend to the Altar by steps, because of the prohibition,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 20:23.</i> “Do not ascend My Altar with steps.” The ramp was 32 cubits long from the south to the north, i.e., this was the length of its incline,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the length of its incline as measured on the ground. The length of the ramp itself (i.e., the hypotenuse of the triangle) was slightly longer, a drop more than 33 cubits.</i> for it extended a cubit over the foundation and a cubit over the <i>sovev.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 62b.</i> On the ground, however, the ramp only extended 30 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the vertex of its angle was slightly obtuse.</i> Thus, when the length and width of the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> is described in Tractate <i>Middos</i>, the <i>mishnah</i> states,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:2.</i> “The ramp and the Altar comprised 62 cubits,” i.e., the Altar took up 32, and the ramp took up 30 cubits, as explained.
There was a hairsbreadth of space between the ramp and the Altar, as stated in Tractate <i>Zevachim.</i> The Talmud also states<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 63a.</i> that the gradient of the Altar’s large ramp was 3 ½ cubits and 1 1/3 fingerbreadths for each cubit of height. This figure is derived as follows: The Altar (not counting its horns) was 9 cubits high. The length of the ramp’s incline was 32 cubits. 9 times 3 cubits equals 27 cubits. 9 times half a cubit equals 4 ½ cubits. Adding the 27 cubits and the 4 1/2 cubits produces a sum of 31 ½ cubits, which is still half a cubit short of the length of the ramp. Since these cubits are equal to 6 handbreadths, half a cubit equals 3 handbreadths. Each handbreadth equals 4 fingerbreadths, so 3 handbreadths equal 12 fingerbreadths. Thus for each of the nine cubits of the Altar’s height, there is an additional fingerbreadth and 1/3. Thus the gradient of the Altar was 3 ½ cubits and 1 1/3 fingerbreadths for each cubit of height.
The ramp was built with such a gentle incline to enable the <i>kohanim</i> to ascend while carrying the heavy limbs of the sacrificial animals.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Zevachim, loc. cit.</i></i> A rise of 10 cubits within 4 cubits of length is considered as difficult walking, as <i>Tosafos</i> explains.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim, loc. cit.,</i> based on <i>Shabbos</i> 100a and <i>Eruvin</i> 58b.</i> Hence, the ramp was built in a manner that allowed for a far gentler rise.
Based on the above concepts, it is necessary to contemplate where the Altar will be located in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>; may it be built speedily, in our days. There is no doubt that its place on Mount Moriah will not change from where it was in the past. As the Talmud states,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 62a.</i> “It was taught, ‘Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov relates: Three prophets ascended with [the Jewish people] from the exile: one gave testimony concerning the Altar and its location.’ ” The Sages required this testimony to ensure that the Altar’s location would not be changed. <i>Rambam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 2:1-3.</i> and <i>Sefer Mitzvos Gadol</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Positive commandment 163.</i> also rule in this manner. The question, however, is whether the design of the structure around the Altar will change, while the Altar will remain in its original location.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Altar and its location, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there is no requirement that the Holy of Holies be located in the same place as it was in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash. </i> It is obvious from the narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i> that the dimensions of the Courtyard and the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in the Future will differ from their dimensions in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Hence, it is possible that the position of the Altar with regard to the Holy of Holies will also be different.</i> After all, as explained above,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 36-37.</i> the Courtyard in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will not resemble the Courtyard in the previous <i>Batei HaMikdash</i> (see secs. 36-37)<i>.</i>
In Tractate <i>Yoma,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 16a.</i> Rabbi Yehudah states that the Altar was situated in the center of the Courtyard. Ten cubits of the Altar’s space were opposite the 10 cubits of the entrance to the Sanctuary, and there were 11 cubits on the north side of the Altar and 11 cubits on the south side of the Altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Altar was 32 cubits wide. In relation to the entrance to the Sanctuary, there were 10 cubits opposite the entrance and 11 cubits on either side.</i> Thus the Altar was positioned in front of the Sanctuary and its walls. According to this opinion, it can be postulated that, in the Future, the Sanctuary will be built so that its entrance will be opposite the center of the Altar, as it was in the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This layout will provide for ample space for the Altar and its ramp. To explain: The Courtyard is 100 cubits from north to south and the Altar is 32 cubits wide. If the Altar is placed in the exact center, it will extend 16 cubits to the north. Thus, there will be 66 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., 50 (half the width of Courtyard) + 16 (half the width of the Altar).</i> from the Altar’s northern side to the Courtyard’s southern wall. As mentioned, the Altar and its ramp will cover only 62 cubits. Thus, there will be an additional 4 cubits between the ramp and the Courtyard’s southern wall.
Similarly, there is no difficulty according to Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai, who maintains<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 53a.</i> that the entire Altar was positioned on the northern side of the Sanctuary with exception of its southern foundation.
