Even according to this opinion, it could still be said that the Altar was positioned “before God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra</i> 4:18, 16:18.</i> i.e., opposite the entrance to the Sanctuary. According to Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai, the Altar’s ramp was located on the south side, so that the <i>kohanim</i> ascending the Altar could fulfill the charge that one who turns should turn to the east.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:17; see sec. 43, above.</i> Since Rabbi Shimon maintains that the entire Altar was positioned in the north, according to his approach there was even more space between the ramp and the southern wall than according to Rabbi Yehudah’s approach.
There is, however, a difficulty according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s approach. In Tractates <i>Zevachim</i> and <i>Yoma,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 59a; <i>Yoma</i> 16a, 37a.</i> <i>et al.,</i> he maintains that the entire Altar was in the south. As stated in those sources, it is written that the sacrifices of the most sacred order must be slaughtered “in the north, before God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra</i> 1:11.</i> Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov understands the verse as intimating that the northern portion of the Courtyard must be empty, containing neither sacred vessels, nor the Altar. <i>Toras Kohanim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Toras Kohanim,</i> sec. 67.</i> explicitly states: “ ‘In the north, before God,’ the northern portion of the Courtyard must be empty,” these are the words of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov. [According to him,] the Altar was positioned in front of half the entrance to the Sanctuary, in front of one of the doors, and extended to the south.
Thus, according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov, there is a difficulty, for in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Courtyard will be a square measuring 100 cubits by 100 cubits. Thus, the entire southern portion will only measure 50 cubits. Now, the Altar and its ramp are 62 cubits long, as stated. Thus, there will be at least 12 cubits in the northern portion of the Courtyard, in addition to some space left between the southern wall and the Altar. After all, it must be possible to walk there without stepping on the ramp. Moreover, the Altar will be positioned in front of the entire entrance to the Sanctuary, since the Altar will extend at least 12 cubits into the northern portion, as stated, and the opening to the Sanctuary only extended 5 cubits to the north.
It is desirable to resolve the issue according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s understanding, for our Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 67a.</i> describe his teachings as, “precise and pure.” Moreover, in his redaction of the <i>Mishnah, Rabbeinu</i> <i>HaKadosh, Yoma,</i> ch. 3,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 3:9.</i> states a teaching regarding the Altar based on Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s understanding, without mentioning him by name.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, that is a sign that the opinion cited is accepted by the majority of Sages.</i> Thus, on the <i>mishnah’s</i> words, “to the north of the Altar,” the Talmud comments:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 37a.</i> “To the north of the Altar”: From this, one can conclude that the Altar is not located in the north. Whose approach does this follow: Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s, as it is taught, “ ‘In the north, before God,’ ‘i.e., the northern portion of the Courtyard must be entirely empty,’ these are the words of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov.”
Similarly, <i>Rambam</i> rules in this manner in the third chapter of <i>Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim</i> 3:2, see below in the text where this ruling is discussed.</i> as explained by the <i>Kessef Mishneh.</i> Hence, there is a great difficulty regarding his understanding of the Altar’s position in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
<i>Tosafos,</i> in their gloss to <i>Zevachim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim,</i> 63a, <i>s.v. kol.</i></i> mentions a further difficulty according to the opinion that the Altar was located entirely in the southern portion of the Courtyard: In the Sanctuary that accompanied the Jews through the desert, the width of the Courtyard from north to south was 50 cubits. Thus, the southern portion of the Courtyard measured 25 cubits. The Altar was 5 cubits wide. This leaves a maximum of 20 cubits for the length of the ramp. Now, according to Rabbi Yossi,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 59b.</i> the Altar was 10 cubits high. Thus, according to his view, one would have to ascend 10 cubits using a ramp that spanned a maximum length of 20 cubits. This would be a steep incline, which would be considered walking with difficulty. Indeed, our Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbos</i> 100a; <i>Eruvin</i> 58b.</i> categorize a mound with an even smaller gradient as a private domain for that reason.
Furthermore, an explanation is also necessary regarding the Altar’s position in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, according to the opinion that the Altar was located entirely in the southern portion of the Courtyard. The <i>Mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Middos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:1-2.</i> delineates the 135 cubits of the width of the Courtyard as follows: “The Altar and the ramp occupied 62 cubits. There were 8 cubits between the Altar and the rings (used for slaughter), the area of the rings comprised 24 cubits. There were 4 cubits between the rings and the tables and 4 cubits between the tables and the pillars.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> to this <i>mishnah,</i> which explains different interpretations regarding the space the tables themselves comprised, and why that space was not included in this calculation.</i> There were 8 cubits between the pillars and the [northern] wall of the Courtyard. The remainder comprised the space between the ramp and the [southern] wall and the space of the pillars.” Now when the remainder, 25 cubits, is divided in 2 – which is logical, even though it is not explicitly stated – there will be 12 ½ cubits for the pillars and 12 ½ cubits between the southern wall and the ramp. Thus, the space from the southern wall until the end of the Altar will be 74 ½ cubits – 62 cubits for the ramp and the Altar and 12 ½ cubits between the southern wall and the ramp. Now, the southern half of the Courtyard only measured 67 ½ cubits (i.e., 135 ½ cubits divided by 2). Thus, <i>Tosafos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 59b, <i>s.v. sheyihe.</i></i> states that one must carefully interpret the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos</i> so that it will not present a difficulty to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov, who maintains that the entire Altar was located in the southern portion of the Courtyard, for in Tractate <i>Yoma,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 16a.</i> our Sages explain that the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos</i> follows Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s approach.
In his commentary to <i>Yoma,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 17a, see <i>Rashi</i> there.</i> <i>Rashi</i> explains the above <i>mishnah</i> as teaching that there were only 5 ½ cubits between the ramp and the southern wall. <i>Rashi</i> bases his statement on the following calculation: As mentioned above, the Altar and the ramp occupied 62 cubits. Thus the entire Altar was situated in the southern portion of the Courtyard, which measured 67 ½ cubits. The Altar marked the end of that area. Thus, there were 5 ½ cubits between the ramp and the southern wall. The remainder of the 25 cubits alluded to by the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos</i> were allocated to the pillars. Thus, these 25 cubits were not divided equally.
<i>Rambam</i> follows a different approach. In his <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah,</i> ch. 5,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 5:15.</i> <i>Rambam</i> clearly states that the 25 cubits, were divided equally, half on either side of the Courtyard. Thus, according to <i>Rambam,</i> there were only 60 ½ cubits from the northern wall of the Courtyard until the Altar. It cannot be argued that the opinion presented in this <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos</i> presents a fourth opinion, differing with Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov and differing with the other Sages mentioned above, following the conclusion of <i>Rashi</i> in <i>Yoma.</i> For even were one to say that the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos</i> presents another opinion, <i>Rambam’s</i> rulings reflect a contradiction: In <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah, Rambam</i> follows the opinion of the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Middos.</i> However, in <i>Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Avodas Yom Kippurim</i> 3:2.</i> <i>Rambam</i> rules according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov, quoting the <i>mishnah,</i> which states, “to the north of the Altar,” as <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains in his gloss to the latter teaching.
For this reason, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that an emendation should be made to <i>Rambam’s</i> text in ch. 4 of <i>Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim,</i> which states:<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 4:1.</i> “His bull would stand between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar,” maintaining the text should read, “would stand opposite the place of the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar,”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the bull would be standing in the northern part of the Courtyard. Since, according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov, the Altar did not extend into the northern part of the Courtyard, it cannot be said that it was standing between the Altar and the <i>Ulam,</i> but rather in a place opposite that area.</i> without taking into account <i>Rambam’s</i> statements in ch. 5 of <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah.</i>
There is a further difficulty in <i>Rambam’s</i> rulings: <i>Rambam</i> rules<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 5:16.</i> that the rectangle in the northern portion of the Courtyard, where sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity were slaughtered, measured 60 ½ cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the 60 cubits remaining from the edge of the Altar to the northern wall of the Courtyard.</i> by 76 cubits (the length from the eastern wall until the <i>Ulam</i>). He reiterates this concept at the beginning of <i>Hilchos Maaseh HaKorbanos,</i> ch. 5.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Maaseh HaKorbanos</i> 5:2.</i> A question arises: Why wasn’t the entire 67 ½ cubits of the northern portion of the Courtyard included in this measure? For <i>Rambam</i> follows the ruling of Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i> who maintains<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 20a; <i>Yoma</i> 36a.</i> that even the northern portion of the Courtyard that is in the Courtyard of the Israelites and the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the first 22 cubits of the Courtyard.</i> is an acceptable place for the slaughter of these sacrifices. Similarly, in those sources, the Talmud discusses and concludes that Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i> adds further space in addition to that mentioned by Rabbi Eliezer ben Shimon, who maintains that it is even acceptable to slaughter these sacrifices in the area between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar. Thus, according to Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi,</i> it is acceptable to slaughter these sacrificial animals in the entire rectangle of 67 ½ by 76<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it’s true that a certain portion of the extra 7 ½ cubits of space is taken up by the Altar, but as <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> has explained, there is an even greater portion that the Altar does not cover.</i> cubits. Seemingly, this is problematic: Since <i>Rambam</i> accepts Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi’s</i> opinion, he should have mentioned this entire area.
Therefore, in conclusion, it appears that <i>Rambam</i> is ruling according to the <i>mishnah</i> at the end of Tractate <i>Middos,</i> and that he views that <i>mishnah</i> as a fourth approach.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See also <i>Tosafos Yom Tov, Middos</i> 5:2, which explains that <i>Rambam</i> understands that <i>mishnah</i> as differing with the perspective of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov.</i> Therefore, he does not include the entire northern half of the Courtyard in the rectangle defined as “the north.” The difficulty mentioned above – that <i>Rambam</i> quotes the wording of the <i>mishnah,</i> “to the north of the Altar,” in <i>Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim</i> – should not be understood as proof that he rules according to Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov. True, the <i>Talmud</i> makes such a deduction from an analysis of the wording of the <i>Mishnah.</i> Nevertheless, different principles apply when analyzing the words of the <i>halachists</i> from those which apply when analyzing the wording of the <i>Mishnah.</i> Instead, it can be assumed that <i>Rambam</i> was simply quoting the wording of the <i>mishnah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> began the latter portion of this section in an attempt to explain the perspective of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov with regard to the position of the Altar in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. He posed several questions concerning that approach, and left them unresolved, leading to the conclusion that in that future time, the Altar’s position will not be in accord with Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov’s approach.</i>

Chapter 44

As mentioned above,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 43.</i> it was forbidden to ascend the Altar by steps because of the prohibition,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 20:23.</i> “Do not ascend My Altar with steps.” If so, the term ומעלתיהו in the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:17.</i> “Its ramps [would allow one to] turn to the east,” should have been singular. Why is it plural?<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> introduces this question by mentioning steps because the word ומעלתיהו could also be translated as “and its steps,” in which instance, the use of the plural would have been understandable. This concept is also mentioned in his commentary to <i>Zevachim</i> 5:3.</i>
A resolution can be offered based on our Sages’ statement:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 62b.</i> “Rav Yehudah said: There were two small ramps that emerged from the [main] ramp. On them, [the <i>kohanim</i>] would turn to the foundation and the <i>sovev.</i> [These ramps] were separated from the Altar by a hairs-breadth, because it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Many verses (<i>Vayikra</i> 1:11, 3:2, <i>et al.</i>) speak of casting blood “all around” the Altar.</i> ‘All around.’ ” In his commentary to the Talmud, <i>Rashi</i> states: <b>“There were two small ramps”</b> – The one which led to the <i>sovev</i> emerged from the east [side] of the [main] ramp to its right. It began its ascent in the midst of the incline of the ramp, continuing to ascend at an angle until it reached the <i>sovev.</i> The one which led to the foundation emerged to the west [side of the main ramp] so that the remainder of the blood of a sin-offering could be taken [to the southern corner of the foundation]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As the <i>Mishnah</i> (<i>Zevachim</i> 5:3) relates, the blood of a sin-offering would be presented on the four corners of the Altar. A <i>kohen</i> would ascend this small ramp and turn to the <i>sovev.</i> He would first present the blood on the southeastern corner and then circle the Altar on the <i>sovev</i>, presenting the blood on each of the corners. <i>Zevachim</i> 6:3 states: “All those who ascend to the Altar would ascend to the right, circle it, and descend to the left.” In his commentary to <i>Zevachim</i> 6:3, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> emphasizes that after ascending to the <i>sovev</i> and presenting the blood of the sin-offering on the 4 corners of the Altar, the <i>kohen</i> would not retrace his steps, but would descend to the foundation using a second ramp on the left. <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> maintains that this ramp was v-shaped, running from the corner towards the main ramp and then from the main ramp to the foundation. Afterwards, as stated towards the end of this section, there was a third level descending from the foundation to the ground. (It must, however, be noted that in his gloss to the <i>mishnah, Tosafos Yom Tov</i> mentions only two levels. The concept of three levels is derived from joining his statements here with those in his commentary to the <i>Mishnah. For technical reasons, a depiction of this three-leveled ramp was not included in the pictures of the Altar presented in this text.</i>)</i> where it would be poured out. When he descended from the Altar, he would descend the ramp, before he reached the ground, he would turn to the left on a small ramp. This ramp began from a low portion of the ramp, far from the Altar so that the descent to the foundation would be gentle.
<b>“Because it is written: ‘All around’ ”</b> – Implied is that a complete circuit of the Altar must be possible. If the ramps were joined to the Altar, there would be no complete circuit. A person would not even be able to wind a string around it.
In that source, the Talmud states<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 63a.</i> that the gradient of all the smaller ramps was one cubit over the length of 3 cubits. If so, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, when the foundation will be 2 cubits high, the incline of the smaller ramps will extend over the length of 6 cubits. In contrast, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Altar’s foundation was only one cubit high and hence, its ramp extended only 3 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">One might ask: Why was there a ramp to the foundation? As <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> states in his gloss to the above-mentioned <i>mishnah</i>, the three activities performed at the northwest corner of the Altar were performed while the <i>kohen</i> was standing on the ground. At present, this writer does not know how to resolve that question.</i> The ramp to the <i>sovev</i> will be the same in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> as it was in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> for the <i>sovev</i> will be 6 cubits high in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, just as it was in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>., as explained in its place.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 38.</i> If so, its ramp will extend over a length of 18 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">From the statements here, it appears that <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> understood that there were – and will be – separate ramps leading to the <i>sovev</i> and the foundation. These ramps did not and will not extend out from the main ramp, for were they to extend from the main ramp, their length or their gradient would be different.</i>

Chapter 45

The <i>Mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:6.</i> “The basin was situated between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar, positioned towards the south.” True, the basin is not mentioned in Yechezkel’s vision. Nevertheless, it is frequently mentioned<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shmos</i> 30:18, 31:9, 38:8.</i> in the Torah conveyed to us by Moshe, and the words of Yechezkel do not, heaven forbid, contradict Moshe Rabbeinu’s words.
Originally, the basin had only two faucets. Afterwards, one of the <i>Kohanim Gedolim,</i> ben Katin, upgraded the basin so that it had 12 faucets, as stated in the <i>Mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Yoma.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 3:10.</i> This improvement enabled all the <i>kohanim</i> who would be involved in offering the daily sacrifice to sanctify themselves at the same time.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 25b.</i> There were twelve <i>kohanim</i> – in addition to the slaughterer. The slaughterer was counted separately, since he did not have to be a <i>kohen.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 27a. As <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> notes, the passage states that in fact thirteen <i>kohanim</i> were involved in the offering of the daily offering and explains that since it was permitted for the slaughterer to be an Israelite, even when – as was usually the case – he was a <i>kohen,</i> it was not absolute obligatory for him to sanctify his hands and feet. Hence, a faucet was not fashioned for him.</i> The 12 are enumerated in the <i>Mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Tamid.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamid</i> 3:1.</i>

Chapter 46

“And He brought me to the Vestibule (the <i>Ulam</i>) of the House…. There were steps by which one ascended to it.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:48-49.</i> With regard to the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the <i>Mishnah</i> teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 3:6.</i> “There were 22 cubits between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar and there were twelve steps there. Each step was 1/2 a cubit high and one cubit wide.” <i>Rambam</i> had a different version of the text which stated that the steps were also 1/2 a cubit wide, as stated in sec. 2. As explained in sec. 26-27, it appears that in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> there will be 34 cubits between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Altar. Consult that section.

Chapter 47

“The length of the <i>Ulam” –</i> from north to south – “was 20 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:49.</i> “The width of the gate was 3 cubits on one side and 3 cubits on the other side.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>48.</i> The intent is that the entire entrance to the <i>Ulam</i> in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be 20 cubits wide. There will be 3 cubits at each end that will be closed, leaving 14 cubits of open space.
In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, by contrast, the entire 20 cubits of the <i>Ulam</i> was open across its full length in front of the Sanctuary. Thus, when explaining our Sages’ prohibition against building an exedra that matches the dimensions of the <i>Ulam</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 43a.</i> <i>Tosafos</i> states that the <i>Ulam</i> was open entirely on its eastern side. This conception is reinforced by our Sages’ statement:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Basra</i> 25a.</i> “[Our] world resembles an exedra; its northern side is not enclosed.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., from this statement, we see that the fourth side of an exedra – and by extension, the <i>Ulam</i> – is open.</i> It is, nevertheless, referred to as, “the opening<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the term <i>petach,</i> “opening,” implies a structure with a gate or door.</i> of the <i>Ulam</i>,” in many places in the <i>Talmud.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yoma</i> 67a, 77a, <i>et al.</i> See also sec. 50 below which continues the discussion of the <i>Ulam.</i></i> This resembles the expression, “the opening to the Sanctuary,” found in the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 40:6.</i> and which describes the eastern side of the Sanctuary that was also open entirely. See the explanations in sec. 50 below.
The wall of the <i>Ulam</i> was 5 cubits thick. This is reflected by the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:48</i> “He measured the doorpost of the <i>Ulam,</i>” which covered the wall. “It was 5 cubits on this side and 5 cubits on the other side.”

Chapter 48

“There were pillars near the doorposts, one on this side,” i.e. on the right, “and one on the other side,”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>49.</i> i.e. on the left. These paralleled the Yachin and Boaz pillars that were present in the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 7:21.</i>

Chapter 49

“The width of the <i>Ulam</i>” – from east to west – “was 11 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:49.</i>
“There were narrowing windows and date palm designs on either side on the flanks<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The verse uses the word כתפות, which literally means “shoulders.”</i> of the <i>Ulam,</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:26</i> i.e., at each side of the entrance.

Chapter 50

“He measured the length of the building at the front of the structure,… [including] its projections on either side as one hundred cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:15.</i> The verse uses the word אתיקיהא, which <i>Targum Yonason</i> translates as, “its projections.” Thus, the verse is teaching us that when the angel measured the length of the building, he included the projections on the corners. The projections refer to the structure which the <i>Mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Middos</i> refers to as the <i>Beis HaChalifos,</i> “the Chamber of the Knives,”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7. This chamber was given this name because the slaughterers’ knives were stored there (Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura).</i> as it states:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i></i> “The <i>Ulam</i> was fifteen cubits longer than [the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>] on either side. [That area] was called <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i>”
This wording implies that only the 15 cubit area projecting on each side was called <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> The entire area in front of the Sanctuary and its walls was called the <i>Ulam,</i> “vestibule.” The statement<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 40:49.</i> “The length of the <i>Ulam</i> was twenty [cubits]” refers to the area in front of the entrance to the Sanctuary, which served as a vestibule to the Sanctuary. Nevertheless, the <i>Ulam</i> itself was wider, extending over the entire area including the walls of the Sanctuary. Rabbeinu Shemayah gives a similar explanation in his commentary to the above <i>mishnah,</i> stating, “In addition to [<i>Beis</i>] <i>HaChalifos,</i> the walls of the <i>Ulam</i> should be extended to the north and the south, opposite the cells.”
Although, even in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the entire east side of the <i>Ulam</i> was not open, <i>Tosafos</i> still refers to the <i>Ulam</i> as an exedra,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 43a. As explained in sec. 47, the term exedra implies a structure that is open entirely on one side.</i> because it was referred to as “the Sanctuary’s vestibule,” and the area in front of the entrance to the Sanctuary was totally open. Alternately, it can be said that <i>Tosafos</i> maintains that the <i>Ulam</i> was not extended opposite the walls of the Sanctuary, for the entire area opposite the Sanctuary was called <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> When the <i>Mishnah</i> states that the <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> was 15 cubits on each side, it was not saying that only this area comprised the <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> Instead, it was merely saying that this portion is included in the area called <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> Nevertheless, there were also other areas that were called <i>Beis HaChalifos,</i> e.g., the areas opposite the walls of the Sanctuary.
In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the walls of the Sanctuary will extend into the <i>Ulam</i> opposite the 5 cubits of the open space (<i>hamunach</i>). This applies both according to <i>Rambam’s</i> conception – with which we agree – and even according to that of <i>Rashi,</i> as will be explained at length in sec. 65.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Sec. 65 does not appear here as a separate section, but is part of secs. 64-69.</i> Thus, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> the <i>Ulam</i> will only be 20 cubits wide, at least in the area opposite the 5 cubits of the <i>munach.</i>
The 15 cubit projections on either side of the building extended only 10 cubits. This is evident from the statement concerning the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:12.</i> “Its length was one hundred cubits.” This verse is teaching us that the length of the building from west to east, until the <i>Beis HaChalifos</i>, is 90 cubits. The entire building including the projections, i.e., <i>Beis HaChalifos,</i> is 100 cubits, as will be explained in sec. 65. Thus from east to west, <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> is only 10 cubits. <i>Rashi</i> offers a similar explanation in his commentary to the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:4.</i> “In front of the chambers, there was a passageway of ten cubits.”
Of these 10 cubits, 5 were taken by the eastern wall of the <i>Ulam,</i> which was 5 cubits wide, as stated in sec. 47. Therefore this area’s empty space did not even measure 5 cubits, because some of the remaining 5 cubits was taken up by the western wall. It is possible that the statement that the width of the <i>Ulam’s</i> wall was 5 cubits only applies to the portion in front of the <i>Ulam,</i> and not to the portion in front of <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> There, the <i>Ulam</i> was not as wide. Alternatively, it is possible to say that the 24 lockers of the 24 priestly watches were embedded into the 5 cubits of the eastern wall, with 12 lockers on either side of the building, as stated in Tractate <i>Sukkah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sukkah</i> 56a.</i> This appears to be the correct interpretation, and I depicted it in this manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> prepared a diagram of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. A copy of that diagram is included as a foldout at the end of this volume.</i>
The above applies to the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> However, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the inner space of <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> may not have been so small because it is possible that the width of this area was more than 10 cubits, for the length of the building from the west until <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> is not explicitly stated in the <i>Mishnah,</i> as it is explicitly stated in Yechezkel’s prophecy. Nevertheless, it appears that <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> did not extend over the entire length of the building on both sides. For if it did extend the entire length of the Sanctuary on both sides of the building, how would one explain the <i>mishnah’s</i> statement:<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> “The Sanctuary building was broad in its front and narrow in its rear”?
<i>Rambam</i> does not rule in this manner. In <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rambam, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:5.</i> he writes that the Sanctuary building measured 100 cubits from north to south. The <i>Ulam’s</i> walls were 5 cubits thick, and there were 10 cubits from the wall of the <i>Ulam</i> until the wall of the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">There were a series of walls and chambers that encompassed 25 cubits on each side of the Sanctuary, and the Sanctuary itself was 20 cubits wide. If we add the 15 cubits on each side mentioned above, we arrive at a total of 100 cubits (15+25+20+25+15=100). <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is emphasizing that according to <i>Rambam,</i> the width of the entire Sanctuary building, and not only its front façade, was 100 cubits.</i> Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura offers a similar interpretation stating<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, <i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> that the <i>Ulam</i> added 15 cubits to the north of the Sanctuary’s building. Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura arrives at this figure because the <i>Ulam’s</i> walls were 5 cubits thick, and the <i>Ulam</i> itself measured 10 cubits. The same calculation applies on the south side.
The text, <i>Kaftor VeFerach,</i> ch. 6, follows <i>Rambam’s</i> approach, stating: The <i>Ulam</i> was the wall for the gateway for the Sanctuary. Its length was 100 cubits. In its center, there was a gateway 40 cubits high and 20 cubits wide. There were 11 cubits between it and the wall of the Sanctuary. Similarly, at the end of those 100 cubits on all sides, from the <i>Ulam</i> until the western wall of the Sanctuary<i>,</i> there was a space of 10 cubits. This space was called <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> The thickness of the <i>Ulam’s</i> wall on all three sides was 5 cubits, for the <i>Ulam</i> surrounded the Sanctuary on three sides until the western wall.
<i>Kaftor VeFerach</i> also states: One who enters inside the gateway can go around the Sanctuary only from three sides, its entire eastern side, and its entire southern and northern sides. This area, <i>Beis</i> <i>HaChalifos</i> did not exist on the western side at all. For the end of the walls of <i>Beis</i> <i>HaChalifos</i> on the west [side] stand opposite the western wall of the Sanctuary and do not pass beyond it.
<i>Ra’avad,</i> however, takes issue with <i>Rambam,</i> stating in his <i>Hasagos:</i> Avraham states: “All of this is emptiness and fantasy. For the <i>Ulam</i> did not have a wall at all [on those sides]. This is a great error, for the <i>Ulam</i> only had an eastern wall in front of Sanctuary. Therefore, [our Sages] said: “The Sanctuary was narrow at its rear and broad in its front.” The width of the <i>Ulam</i> is counted in the width of the Sanctuary.
In his <i>Kessef Mishneh,</i> Rav Yosef Caro defends <i>Rambam’s</i> approach, stating: The entire reason <i>Ra’avad</i> ascribes an error to <i>Rambam</i> stems from the <i>mishnah’s</i> statement that the Sanctuary was narrow its rear and broad in its front, like a lion. This seemingly conflicts with the statement that it [the Sanctuary] was 100 cubits by 100 cubits. Nevertheless, <i>Rambam</i> already clarified that issue in his Commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i> stating: “The entire Sanctuary was 100 [cubits] by 100 [cubits], but the structure would taper inward, narrowing on its western side. As it proceeded to the east, it would broaden, like a lion whose front is broad, but whose rear is narrow.” If so, there is no conflict with the subject under discussion here.
Now, it is true that <i>Rambam</i> does not interpret this <i>mishnah</i> as referring to <i>Beis HaChalifos,</i> but rather explains that in its rear, the walls of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> would taper inward, and would become broader toward the front of the building. Nevertheless, an explanation is still required, because <i>Rambam’s</i> statements appear to contradict each other. On one hand, <i>Rambam</i> states that the building measured 100 cubits by 100 cubits, seemingly implying that it was of equal dimensions on all sides. Nevertheless, he also states that the wall would taper inward.
For this reason Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, who follows <i>Rambam’s</i> approach regarding the <i>Ulam</i> and the broadening of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> concludes his discussion of the issue by saying:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, <i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> “It has not been explained to me how it is possible for [the building] to be an even 100 cubits on all sides.”
I will not withhold a possible resolution of <i>Rambam’s</i> approach – that the figure 100 by 100 refers to the foundation of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, i.e., although the walls of the building would narrow, the building’s foundation was 100 cubits by 100 cubits. Support for this interpretation can be drawn from the description of the foundation in the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:8.</i> “Its size was 6 cubits.” In his interpretation of that verse, <i>Rashi</i> states that the foundation was 6 cubits high, including the portion embedded in the ground. The portion above the ground, however, was only 5 cubits high. See sec. 64, where this concept is explained. A similar statement can be made regarding the width of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Below the ground, the building was a 100–cubit square; above the ground, the walls would taper.
Although <i>Rambam’s</i> statements can be explained regarding the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> such an explanation will not be valid regarding the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, as we will explain in sec. 70, when interpreting the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>10.</i> “Between the chambers [and the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>], there was a 20-cubit space.”

Chapter 51

“And he brought me into the Sanctuary and he measured the doorposts”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:1. “Doorposts” is the translation of the Hebrew, .אילים See <i>Metzudos David</i> there.</i> that covered the wall that intervened between the <i>Ulam</i> and the Sanctuary. “They were six cubits wide.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel, loc. cit.</i></i>
“I saw the height of the House, all around.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>8.</i> The exact height is not stated in the verse. Tractate <i>Middos</i> states<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:6.</i> that the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was 100 cubits high and delineates the details of this height. Nevertheless, the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> may not be this height, for the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh was 20 cubits taller than the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, as Scripture states:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Chronicles</i> 3:4.</i> “It was 120 cubits high.”
This raises a question on <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary<i>.</i> One of our Sages’ interpretation<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Basra</i> 3a.</i> of the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chaggai</i> 2:9.</i> “The honor of this later House will be greater than that of the First,” is that the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be larger than the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. <i>Rashi</i> states that this means that the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be taller than the First <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> Now, the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was 100 cubits high, as stated in Tractate <i>Middos.</i> However, the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was 120 cubits high, as stated above. It is possible to resolve this difficulty if we explain that the Sanctuary of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was higher than that of the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. For, as will be explained, in the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Sanctuary was only 30 cubits high, while in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it was 40 cubits high.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahadura Basra.</i></i>
Thus, the height of the Sanctuary itself – not including the other levels above it – differed in the First and Second <i>Batei HaMikdash.</i> In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Sanctuary was 40 cubits high, while in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh, it was 30 cubits high, as <i>Rashi</i> states in his commentary to <i>II Chronicles,</i> and <i>Radak</i> states in his commentary to <i>I Melachim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 6:2.</i> Therefore, we have no way to determine how high the Sanctuary – or the levels above it – will be in the Third <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Fortunate is the one who knows!
Since, as indicated by the verse, the Sanctuary building will be high, thick beams will be placed between the wall of the Sanctuary and the wall of the <i>Ulam</i> to stabilize them. Thus, it is written,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:25.</i> “There was a thick wooden beam (וְעָב עֵץ) projecting toward the front of the <i>Ulam</i> from the outside.” On that verse, <i>Rashi</i> comments, “Cedar beams were fixed between the wall of the Sanctuary and the wall of the <i>Ulam</i> so that it will not buckle, as stated in Tractate <i>Middos.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 3:8 and <i>Rambam’s</i> commentary.</i>
In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> to <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Kessef Mishneh, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 1:9.</i> Rav Yosef Caro questions this <i>mishnah,</i> for as <i>Rambam</i> states in that source, no protruding wooden beams were found in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> as a safeguard against the prohibition,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 16:21.</i> “Do not plant an <i>ashera,</i> any tree, near the altar.” Rav Yosef Caro attempts to resolve the issue, explaining that the beams were not permanent parts of the structure. Instead, they were held in place by the pressure of the walls, without being permanently affixed to the structure.
<i>Radak,</i> by contrast, interprets וְעָב עֵץ as referring to a beam, similar to the lintel of a doorframe that was embedded in the outer façade of the <i>Ulam’s</i> wall<i>.</i> Our Sages refer to this beam as a <i>morish.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Kama</i> 67a.</i> In the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh, such beams are referred to as<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 6:36.</i> “cedar beams.” It appears to me that the parallel in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> were the five cedar beams embedded in the façade of the <i>Ulam’s</i> wall<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 3:7.</i>
The width of the Sanctuary from north to south was 20 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:2.</i> In the center of its eastern wall, there was an entrance that was 10 cubits wide. This is derived from the verse, “On the flanks of the entrance, there were 5 cubits on one side and 5 cubits on the other.” “Its doorpost was rectangular.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>21.</i>
“There were two [sets of] doors to the Sanctuary and to the Holy [of Holies]. The [sets of] doors [each] comprised two doors; these two doors would revolve [on a hinge]. The door [on] one [side] comprised two doors and the other door comprised two doors.”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>23-24.</i> Similarly, it was taught in Tractate <i>Middos:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:1.</i> “The entrance to the Sanctuary… had four doors, two on the inside and two on the outside.” <i>Rashi</i> explains this <i>mishnah</i> in depth in his commentary.
“Engraved on them, on the doors of the Sanctuary, were cherubs and date-palms as they were engraved on the walls,”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:25.</i> as will be explained in depth in sec. 54.

Chapter 52

“Then he returned me in the direction of the outer gate of the Sanctuary which faces the east.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 44:1.</i> This verse refers to the gate of Sanctuary which was before the Holy of Holies. The narrative continues, describing the wicket entrance next to this gate, “It was closed. God said to me, ‘This gate shall be closed. It will not be opened. Man shall not enter through it, because God, the Lord of Israel, has entered it. It has been closed.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>1-2.</i> Our Sages understood this verse as referring to the wicket entrance at the southern side of the gate to the Sanctuary. Thus, the <i>Mishnah</i> states in both Tractate <i>Tamid</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tamid</i> 3:7.</i> and Tractate <i>Middos:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:2.</i> “The Great Gate<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the gate to the Sanctuary.</i> had two wicket entrances [at its sides], one in the south and one in the north. No one ever entered the one in the south. The [prophet] Yechez­kel speaks explicitly concerning it: ‘This gate shall be closed.’ ” In his commentary, Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura states that the <i>mishnah</i> is referring to two small entrances that were slightly removed from the gate.
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel comments on this <i>mishnah:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Abarbanel, Yechezkel</i> 44:3.</i> [The Sages’] conception was that the prophet’s vision here was identical with [the structure] that was made in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. My mind cannot accept this. In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, [God’s] Glory did not enter via the eastern gate.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., with regard to the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it is written (<i>I Melachim</i> 8:10-11): “And it came to pass, when the <i>kohanim</i> departed from the Sanctuary, the cloud filled the House of God and the <i>kohanim</i> could not stand to minister because of the cloud; for the glory of God filled the House of God.” In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, by contrast, there was not such a manifestation of Divine Glory, for as our Sages said (<i>Yoma</i> 21b), the Divine presence did not rest in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> If that [prophecy] was not yet fulfilled, how can it be said, “This gate shall be closed… because God, the Lord of Israel, has entered it”? The implication is that the closing of the gate will come after [God’s] Glory enters the House.
In resolution: We must precisely analyze the words of the <i>mishnah,</i> “The [prophet] Yechezkel speaks explicitly concerning it.” The <i>mishnah</i> does not conclude: “And so it will be in the Future,” as it does in the <i>mishnah</i> which discusses the unroofed courtyards.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 2:5.</i> With this choice of wording, the Sages prevent the question raised by Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel from arising. Were the <i>mishnah</i> to have said, “And so it will be in the Future,” thus equating the wicket entrances in the two <i>Batei HaMikdash,</i> the question could have arisen: How can one say they were similar? In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the wicket will be closed after God’s Glory enters, while in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, His Glory had not yet entered. For that reason, the <i>mishnah</i> states, “The [prophet] Yechezkel speaks explicitly concerning it,” i.e., in this instance, the wicket was closed in anticipation of the fulfillment of the prophecy to Yechezkel. Thus, the intent is not that the situation will be the same in both <i>Batei HaMikdash,</i> because the motivating rationale is different in each instance. Although the wicket will be closed in both <i>Batei HaMikdah,</i> the reason it will be closed is not the same.
This concept is intimated by the fact that in both Tractates <i>Middos</i> and <i>Tamid,</i> the <i>mishnah</i> does not only quote the verse’s conclusion, “It has been closed.” Instead, the <i>mishnah</i> also quotes the beginning of the verse, “This gate shall be closed.” Seemingly, the verse is redundant. However, the first clause uses the future tense, while the last clause uses the past tense. This implies that the gate was already closed, before this future time. The use of the past tense can apply to Yechezkel’s vision, even though the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> had not yet been built, because Yechezkel recorded his prophecy <b>after</b> having been shown the vision of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> When the <i>mishnah</i> says, “The [prophet] Yechezkel speaks explicitly concerning it,” the <i>mishnah</i> is referring to this final clause, “it has been closed,” which refers to the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. By contrast, the rationale given by the verse, “God, the Lord of Israel, has entered it,” does not apply to the closing of the wicket in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, but to its closing in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Thus, the question raised by Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel does not apply.
The rationale that the southern wicket was closed in the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in anticipation of God’s Glory entering in the Future is alluded to in the above verse: “This gate shall be closed.” The future tense refers to the Ultimate Future, “because God, the Lord of Israel, has entered it.” “It has been closed,” in expectation of that future time. The wicket’s being closed was a sign to those who returning from exile to look to the future, knowing that ultimately, God’s Glory would enter through this gate, and that He would cause His presence to dwell in His <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
The passage continues, stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 44:3.</i> “The <i>nasi</i> will sit within it to partake of food in God’s presence.” Here, the term <i>nasi,</i> literally, “the uplifted one,” refers to the <i>kohen gadol,</i> the High Priest. Since the <i>kohen gadol</i> is more prestigious than the other <i>kohanim</i>, he would not usually eat together with the other <i>kohanim</i> in the various chambers. Instead, he would partake of his portion of the sacrificial offerings while sitting in the entranceway of this wicket.

Chapter 53

“So declares God, the Lord: ‘The gate to the Inner Courtyard facing east”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:1.</i> – this refers to the wicket at the northern side of the gate to the Sanctuary – “shall be closed during the six days of work; it shall be opened on the <i>Shabbos</i> day and it shall be opened on the day of the New Moon.” The Sanctuary is referred to as “the Inner Courtyard,” as reflected in <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to the verse, “And it will come to pass when they enter the gates of the Inner Courtyard.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 44:17.</i> Consult that source.
“And the <i>Nasi</i> will enter the courtyard from the outside via the vestibule of the gate. He will stand at the doorpost of that gate,”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:2.</i> i.e., at the northern wicket. “The <i>kohanim</i> will prepare his burnt-offering and his peace-offering. He will prostrate himself on the threshold of the gate and depart, but the gate will not close until the evening. The people of the land will prostrate themselves at the entrance to that gate on <i>Shabbasos</i> and the days of the New Moon before God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>2-3.</i> “When the <i>Nasi</i> will offer a freewill-offering… they shall open the gate.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12.</i> Previously, in his commentary,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Yechezkel</i> 45:17.</i> <i>Rashi</i> states, “Here, the term <i>Nasi</i> refers to the <i>kohen gadol.</i> This applies whenever the term is mentioned in the passage. [However,] I heard in the name of Rabbi Menachem that it refers to the king.”
Concerning this wicket, the <i>mishnah</i> teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:2, regarding opening the Sanctuary in the morning. See sections 64-69 where this concept is explained at length.</i> “He would take the key, open the wicket, and enter the cell. From the cell, he would enter the Sanctuary.” <i>Rambam</i> explains<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:6.</i> that after entering the wicket, “he would proceed between the two walls<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The wall of the cell and the wall of the Sanctuary.</i> until he reached a place that opened to the left side of the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when looking to the east, the north is to the left.</i> He would enter the Sanctuary through it, proceed to the Great Gate, and open it.”

Chapter 54

“He measured its length,”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:2.</i> i.e., the length of the Sanctuary from east to west, “as 40 cubits.”
“There were doorposts… and the windows were covered,”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid,:</i>16.</i> i.e. wooden frames were made for the windows and the frames “were covered,” plated with gold, for the entire Sanctuary was plated with gold, and gold plate cannot be applied to stone. There were large boards that would cover, “to the space above the height of the entrance… on the entire wall, all around,”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>17.</i> i.e., on all the walls of the Sanctuary, “in the inner [chamber],” i.e., the Holy of Holies, “and in the outer [chamber],” i.e., the Sanctuary. The boards were “carefully measured.” Designs were carved on these boards, including “cherubs and date palms; there was a date palm between one cherub and another. Each cherub had two faces, and there was a human face.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>18-19.</i>

Chapter 55

“One span;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zeres,</i> in Hebrew, a measure equal to the size of an outstretched hand, defined as a 1/2 cubit.</i> this was the upper surface of the Altar.” <i>Rashi</i> explains that the upper surface of the Golden Altar was measured with an ordinary cubit, i.e., one that equaled 5 handbreadths. The Golden Altar was one cubit long and one cubit wide. Thus, there was a span, i.e., half a cubit, from the center of the Altar to the edge in any direction. With regard to the Outer Altar as well, the measures were made from its center, as stated above.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 39-40.</i>
In <i>Parshas VeAtah Tetzaveh,</i> the Golden Altar is described: “Its height will be two cubits…. It will have four horns and a gold border.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 30:2-3.</i> The Golden Altar was placed in the Sanctuary exactly halfway between the Table and the <i>Menorah,</i> positioned slightly to the east, as <i>Rashi</i> states in <i>Parshas Terumah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shmos</i> 26:35.</i> There, <i>Rashi</i> states that all three were placed within the inner half of the Sanctuary.

Chapter 56

“The Altar was made of wood.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:22.</i> Here, the term “Altar” refers to the Table for the Showbread,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Although the verse describes the Table for the Showbread, it refers to it as “the Altar.” <i>Rashi</i> explains that, in this way, the verse teaches that in the present age, a person’s table – i.e., when he shows hospitality to guests – can bring atonement for him like the Altar (<i>Menachos</i> 97a).</i> as the verse states later: “And he said to me, ‘This is the Table that is before God.’ ” “It was three cubits high.” Thus, the Table in the <i>Batei HaMikdash</i> was twice the height of the Table made in Moshe’s time.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shmos</i> 25:23. In his responsa, <i>Chasam Sofer</i> (<i>Yoreh Deah</i> 236) asks: How is it possible that changes were made from the design of the vessels of the Sanctuary made by Moshe? Fashioning the vessels is a <i>mitzvah</i> and one of the cardinal principles of faith (see <i>Rambam’s</i> Thirteen Principles of Faith listed in his Commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i> Introduction to <i>Sanhedrin,</i> ch. 10, principles 8-9) is that the Torah and its <i>mitzvos</i> will never change. True, the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was built according to prophetic vision, as reflected in our Sages’ interpretation (<i>Zevachim</i> 62a, <i>Chulin</i> 83b, <i>et al.</i>) of the verse (<i>I Divrei HaYamim</i> 28:19): “Everything is written down [transmitted] by the hand of God, according to the understanding granted.” Even so, a prophet is forbidden against deviating from the commands given to Moshe (<i>Rambam, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah</i> 9:1). <i>Chasam Sofer</i> resolves that question explaining that our Sages (<i>Shevuous</i> 15a) interpreted the words “and so you shall do” in the verse (<i>Shmos</i> 25:9), “According to the entire vision I will show you…, so shall you do,” as meaning “ ‘so shall you do’ in future generations.” That statement can be understood as teaching that in the future, you should also do “as I will show you,” i.e., God’s original command implies that visions will be shown to prophets in the future and they will build the Sanctuary and its vessels according to those visions.</i> The Table was “two cubits long”; the narrative does not mention the Table’s width. In Moshe’s time, “its length was two cubits and its width, one cubit.”
Describing the other particulars of the Table will merely lengthen the narrative without contributing to our purpose. Details can be found in the <i>Talmud,</i> in Tractate <i>Menachos,</i> ch. 11.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Menachos</i> 97a-99b.</i>
In his commentary to the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shmos</i> 26:35.</i> <i>Rashi</i> states that the Table was positioned 2 1/2 cubits from the northern wall. The Sanctuary of that era was 10 cubits wide. Hence, in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, where the Sanctuary was 20 cubits wide, it would seem appropriate for the Table to be positioned 5 cubits from the wall were the same proportions to be maintained.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See, however, <i>Menachos</i> 99a.</i>

Chapter 57

Although the prophet does not say explicitly whether or not he saw a <i>Menorah,</i> there is no doubt that it will be present in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, for the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will not lack any of the vessels described in the narrative God communicated to Moshe. Furthermore, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel interprets the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:21. The phrase is translated here according to Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel’s interpretation. According to <i>Rashi</i> and others<i>,</i> it would be translated differently.</i> “Within the holy place, there was a light like a light” as meaning “Inside the Sanctuary (‘within the holy place’), the <i>Menorah</i> radiated light” (“there was a light”) throughout the entire <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> (“that was like a light”, i.e. like the light every person has at home near his table). [This interpretation can be derived from] the Shunamite woman, who says with regard to the prophet Elisha,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Melachim</i> 4:10.</i> “Let us place there a bed, a table, a chair, and a lamp for him.” The word the Shunamite woman uses for lamp, is “<i>menorah</i>.”
Therefore, in our depiction of the Sanctuary, we placed the <i>Menorah</i> opposite the Table on the southern side of the Sanctuary, 5 cubits from the wall. As explained above with regard to the Table,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 56.</i> this measure is derived from <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary regarding the Sanctuary,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shmos</i> 26:35.</i> which states that the Table was positioned 2 1/2 cubits from the wall.
One who desires to know the design of the <i>Menorah</i> as a whole and in detail should study the <i>mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Menachos,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachos</i> 3:7.</i> as explained by <i>Rambam.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">There are several differences between <i>Rambam’s</i> conception of the <i>Menorah</i> as reflected in the drawings that accompany the manuscript copies of his Commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i> and the popular illustrations. The most well-known is that <i>Rambam</i> maintains that the branches of the <i>Menorah</i> extended diagonally, not in a rounded form. <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is emphasizing that <i>Rambam’s</i> conception should be adhered to.</i>

Chapter 58

“Then he entered before the inner [Sanctuary],”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:3.</i> i.e., to the wall that separated between the Sanctuary and the Holy of Holies. “He measured the doorpost<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of the Hebrew word איל.</i> of the entrance,” i.e., the width of the frame that covered the wall, “it was two cubits.”
In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> this place was occupied by the <i>amah traksin,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">According to <i>Rashi</i> (<i>Yoma</i> 52a), the term means “the cubit that is inside and outside,” i.e., the Sages were unsure whether its measure should be part of the Sanctuary or part of the Holy of Holies.</i> in which there were two curtains, not a wall, as stated in Tractate <i>Yoma.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yoma</i> 51b-52a; see also <i>Middos</i> 4:7. <i>Bava Basra</i> 3a, b explains that they did not build a wall because the Second Temple was higher than the First. In the First Temple, the Sanctuary was only 30 cubits high, while in the Second Temple, it was 40 cubits high. A wall only a cubit thick would not be sturdy if built to such a height. Nevertheless, the width of the wall was not increased, because its original width was established by Divine revelation. Thus, they returned to the pattern established in the Sanctuary of Moses and utilized curtains as dividers.</i> Similarly, in the <i>Mishkan</i> in the desert, there was a curtain separating the Sanctuary from the Holy of Holies.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shmos</i> 26:33.</i> In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there will be a wall<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in addition to the curtain. The curtain is required, for we are commanded (<i>Shmos</i> 26:31): “You shall make a curtain.”</i> with a doorway between the Sanctuary and the Holy of Holies, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:23.</i> “There were two doors between the Sanctuary and the Holy Chamber.”
“The entrance was six cubits” high. “The width of the entrance was seven cubits.” “Its doorposts were rectangular”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>21.</i> and “it had two doors”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>23.</i> that would close one opposite the other, one attached to the northern doorpost, and one to the southern one.

Chapter 59

“He measured its length as 20 cubits and its width as 20 cubits…. He said to me, ‘This is the Holy of Holies.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:4.</i> “There were doorposts, covered windows, and corner structures… Opposite the doorpost, there were wooden boards all around, [from] the earth to the windows – and the windows were covered; to above the entrance, until the inner chamber, all around.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>16-17. The same description is found with regard to the walls of the Sanctuary in sec. 54, because, as the verse states, these features were common to all three of the areas mentioned.</i>

Chapter 60

Although the prophet does not mention the Ark of God, Commander of legions, there is no need to prove that the Ark will return and be present in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. For our entire hope and trust is that the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will return to its initial eminence. Indeed, “the glory of this later House will surpass”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Chaggai</i> 2:9.</i> that of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in the five matters that were lacking in it, one of them being the Ark.
The Ark was entombed prior to the destruction of the First <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> because King Yoshiyahu had a prophetic vision that the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> would be destroyed. The Ark was not present in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, because it too was destined to be destroyed because of our sins.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Horios</i> 12a; <i>Rambam, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:1.</i> There are other Sages who maintain that the Ark was not entombed, but taken in captivity to Babylon. This opinion is based on the prophecy,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Melachim</i> 20:17; this verse communicates God’s warning to King Chizkiyahu that all the treasures of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> would be taken to Babylon.</i> “Nothing shall remain,” i.e., not even the Tablets, as stated in Tractate <i>Yoma.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is taken from <i>Yoma</i> 53b, which explains the prophet’s words, לא יותר דבר. Our Sages explain that the word <i>davar,</i> which means “nothing” alludes to the <i>dibros,</i> the “commandments,” i.e. the Tablets into which the Ten Commandments were hewn.</i> Nevertheless, the Ark was not present in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> for the same reason stated above. Since the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> would ultimately be destroyed, God did not want His Ark and the Tablets to be exiled again.
The design of the Ark, its staves that will not be removed from it, and its golden cover with the cherubs on it are described in <i>Parshas Terumah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 25:10-22.</i>

Chapter 61

“He measured the wall of the House,”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:5.</i> i.e., the thickness of the western wall of the Holy of Holies, “it was six cubits.” According to <i>Rambam,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Middos</i> 4:3; <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:4.</i> in the Second <i>Beis Hamikdash</i> the wall was not a solid 6 cubits. Instead, there were two walls with open space between them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The open space is sometimes referred to as a תא, “cell.”</i> The width of the walls and the open space added up to 6 cubits.
It would be possible to give a similar explanation in this instance – that the “wall” referred to by the verse refers to two walls with open space between them. Accordingly, the open space would be one of the 33 side-chambers (צלעות), as reflected in the explanations below<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 64-69.</i> that follow <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation. We did not offer such an explanation because we want <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation to accord with <i>Targum Yonason</i>, as will be explained in sections 64-65.

Chapter 62

The verse cited previously<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:5, cited at the beginning of sec. 61.</i> continues, “The width of the side-chamber (צלע) was 4 cubits.” In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the parallel structure was 6 cubits wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Middos</i> 4:7 states: “The cell was six.”</i>
There was a second side-chamber built on top of this side-chamber, and there was a third side-chamber built on top of the second. These were three of the 33 side-chambers that will be described in sec. 64.
The verse states that the side-chambers, “surrounded the House all around.” In sec. 64, these side-chambers will be discussed in detail.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See a depiction of the three levels of side-chambers on page 178.</i>

Chapter 63

“The foundations of the side-chambers were a full measuring-rod wide, a space of 6 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:8.</i> The verse uses the word אצילה, which <i>Targum Yonason</i> explains means “space”. <i>Radak</i> offers a similar interpretation, stating that אצילה reflects “size” or “greatness,” as in the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shmos</i> 23:11.</i> “Against the great ones (אצילי) of the Children of Israel.” <i>Rashi</i> explains that the verse in Yechezkel’s prophecy refers to the foundation of the wall of the side-chamber which was embedded in the ground. Above the ground, however, the wall was 5 cubits wide, as reflected by the following verse:
“The width of the wall of the side-chamber toward the outside was 5 cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:9.</i> As mentioned in sec. 61, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the western wall of the Holy of Holies was comprised of two parallel walls, with a hollow space between them. There was a cell inside the hollow space. Similarly, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> this outer wall was constructed in a similar manner, with a cell inside its hollow space.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> However, we did not depict the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> according to this interpretation, even according to <i>Rambam’s</i> explanation, because we wanted <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation to agree with that of <i>Targum Yonason,</i> as will be explained in sec. 64.

Chapter 64

<b>SECTIONS 64–69</b>
“There were side-chambers, one side-chamber on top of another, 33 in number.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:6.</i> We have described three of these side-chambers previously.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 62. I.e., the side-chambers on the western side.</i> Here, we will explain the thirty others. Since the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:7.</i> “[The side-chambers] became progressively wider. There was a spiral [stairwell]…. From the lowest level, one would ascend to the highest level, via the middle level,” it is apparent that there will be three levels of side-chambers.
Similarly, Tractate <i>Middos</i> teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:3.</i> There were 38 cells there, 15 on the northern side, 15 on the southern side and 8 on the western side. On the northern and southern sides, there were 5 [cells] on top of 5 [cells], and 5 [cells] on top of them. On the western side, there were 3 [cells] on top of 3 [cells], and 2 [cells] on top of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> proceeds to explain, there are two approaches to the understanding of the 15 cells on the northern and southern sides: a) that of <i>Rambam</i> and the Rabbis who follow his conception: according to this view, there were three levels of five cells proceeding from the inner wall of <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> to the wall of the Sanctuary on both sides, running from north to south and from south to north, each of the cells parallel to the entire length of the Sanctuary. b) that of <i>Rashi</i> and the Rabbis who follow his conception: according to this view, there were three levels of five cells proceeding from <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> to the western wall of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> on both sides of the Sanctuary running from east to west so that the total length of all five cells and their walls was equal to the length of the Sanctuary. <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> first explains <i>Rambam’s</i> approach and then that of <i>Rashi</i>.</i>
Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura explains this <i>mishnah</i> as follows: Later in the chapter,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>7.</i> when [the <i>mishnah</i>] details the 70 cubits [of the width of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">That <i>mishnah</i> does not count the 15 cubits of <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> on either side.</i> from north to south, it states: “The wall of the winding stairwell was 5 [cubits wide], the winding stairwell was 3 [cubits wide], the wall of the cell, 5, the cell, 6, and the wall of the Sanctuary, 6.” It also makes a similar reckoning regarding the southern [side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>]. The intent is not that the wall of the winding stairwell, the wall of the cell, and the wall of the Sanctuary were that [thick. Instead, these “walls” had empty space and the <i>mishnah</i>] is reckoning [for example,] the thickness of the wall of the Sanctuary, and the space between it and the next wall. Thus, the 5 cubits of the wall of the winding stairwell, the 3 of the winding stairwell, the 5 of the wall of the cell, the 6 of the cell, and the 6 of the wall of the Sanctuary, all contained a [small] chamber, i.e., a cell [and a wall]. Thus, there were five cells on the northern [side of the Sanctuary], and an equal number on the southern [side]. Above these five, there were another five [cells], and five more on top of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This translation reflects the version of the <i>mishnah</i> found in Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura’s text. The Hebrew text here reads slightly differently.</i> a total of 15 in the north and the same number in the south. Similarly, in the west, later on in the chapter, [the <i>mishnah</i>] states: “The wall of the Sanctuary was 6 [cubits] wide, the cell, 6, and the wall of the cell 5.” There was a chamber inside each of these [three]. The wall of the cell was not 6 cubits thick. Instead, the wall together with the cell it contained were 6 [cubits wide]. Similarly, the other cell outside of it [together with its wall] was 6 [cubits wide], and the wall of the other cell outside of them with the cell it contained was 5 [cubits wide]. Thus, there were three chambers. On top of these three were built three other chambers, and two [were built] on top of them. Thus, there were eight chambers in the west.
<i>Rambam</i>’s conception is the same, as he writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rambam, Middos</i> 4:3.</i> Do not err [and think that there is a difficulty] because first, [the <i>mishnah</i>] calls them [by individual names]: the wall of the winding stairwell, the winding stairwell, the wall of the cell, the cell, and the wall of the Sanctuary, and afterwards, calls them all cells, because the space left between the two walls is a cell. Thus, they are all cells. The wall of the winding stairwell, the wall of the cell, and the wall of the Sanctuary were not solid walls. Instead, there were two walls, and there was space between them. Some of these chambers had names. For example, on the lowest level of [these cells], there was the winding stairwell, the chamber for water to descend, and others.
The Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be built in a similar manner. The lowest level on the northern and southern sides will comprise five side-chambers that will be located in these five empty spaces. There will be five side-chambers on top of them, and five more on top of those. The side-chambers may have other descriptive names that are not mentioned in the verse.
According to <i>Radak</i> and Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, the winding stairwell is mentioned explicitly in Scripture. These commentaries interpret the word ונסבה,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:7.</i> as referring to a winding stairwell. In contrast to this, <i>Rashi</i> and the <i>Targum</i> interpret the word ונסבה as meaning a ladder that was used to ascend to the upper levels, as will be explained.
Even though we equate the order of the side-chambers mentioned in the narrative here with the order of the cells mentioned in the <i>mishnah,</i> their width will not be the same, as will be explained later on with regard to the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12.</i> “the width of the western side.”
As explained in the previous sections,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 62.</i> there will only be three cells on the western side of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. They will be built one on top of the other, and will occupy the space between the western wall of the Holy of Holies and the outer western wall of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The space between these walls is the side-chamber mentioned in the verse.
We did not think that the side-chambers might be located next to each other, with two side-chambers being situated between two additional walls that were not that thick, as we explained with regard to the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and with regard to the northern and southern sides of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The reason for our choice is that <i>Targum Yonason</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Targum Yonason, Yechezkel</i> 41:6.</i> states that there will be “eleven [side-chambers] in a row.” This can be understood as follows. On the western side, the side-chambers were built one on top of other. When the five lowest side-chambers in the north, the five side-chambers in the south, and the lowest side-chamber in the west are viewed as a whole, there will be “eleven [side-chambers] in a row.” This concept will also apply regarding the second and third levels. However, if the three levels of chambers in the west are interpreted as being next to each other, there will be 13 side-chambers in a row, not “eleven [side-chambers] in a row.”
