The supporting beams for the side-chambers “approached the wall of the House [near] the side-chambers all around so that they would be held fast, but they were not attached inside the walls of the House.”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel, op. cit.</i></i> The intent of this verse is that the beams that supported the higher side-chambers were placed between the lowest chamber and the middle chamber, and between the middle chamber and the highest chamber. The beams would rest on “the wall near the side-chambers,” i.e., on the wall that was visible from the inside of the side-chamber. Implied is that the thickness of the wall was progressively reduced, leaving a ledge to support the beams, as will be explained shortly.
“They would be held fast, but they were not held fast inside the walls of the House,” i.e., the beams were supported by the wall’s indentation, but not by the wall itself, for holes were not made in the wall into which the beams would be inserted. Instead, they were supported by the wall’s indentations.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i> in his commentary to <i>I Melachim</i> 6:6 explains that this was done because it is more attractive. <i>Radak</i> in his commentary to <i>ibid.</i> 6:7 gives another reason. Metal tools were not allowed to be used while building the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> <i>Radak</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 41:6.</i> and <i>Ri</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ri, Tosafos, Yoma</i> 52a, <i>s.v." kosel".</i></i> offer similar explanations, except according to their opinions, this indentation only existed on the side of the wall adjoining the Sanctuary, and not on the outer side. Their opinion is based on their interpretation of the structure, which accords with that of <i>Rashi</i>, as will be explained shortly.
According to our interpretation, which is based on the conception of <i>Rambam</i> and those who follow his conception, the walls were indented to support the beams of the side-chambers on both of their sides. Thus, the walls were indented on either side of the side-chambers to support their beams.
“[The side-chambers] became wider and the spiral stairwell ascended higher and higher to [the higher level of] the side-chambers, for the House was surrounded by [side-chambers] on higher and higher levels, all around the House; therefore, the widest level of the structure was the highest.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:7.</i> The prophet’s use of the words וְרָחֲבָה and וְנָסְבָה, imply separate concepts. As <i>Rashi</i> explains, the <i>vav</i> preceding each word distinguishes between the two concepts. Accordingly, וְרָחֲבָה can be explained as <i>Rashi</i> does, that the side-chambers would progressively widen on their higher levels. Therefore, the walls would become narrower as a result of the indentation made to support the side-chambers. Indeed, this is the way high walls are built, somewhat narrower on top, to reduce the weight on the lower levels.
We cannot agree, however, with <i>Rashi</i>’s interpretation that the wall was indented a full cubit at each level, for we explained above that the walls were not 5 or 6 cubits thick. Rather, the two parallel walls and the empty space between them encompassed 5 or 6 cubits. According to our understanding, it would be impossible for the wall to be indented a cubit on the middle level and another cubit on the upper level. <i>Rashi</i>, however, understood that the walls were actually 5 or 6 cubits thick, as will be explained below.
The <i>mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:4.</i> “The lowest level was five [cubits] wide, the surface above it was six, as indicated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 6:6.</i> ‘for he made recesses in the House all around.’ ” <i>Rambam</i> and Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura interpret this as meaning that an indentation measuring one cubit was made on all three levels. However, they maintain that this <i>mishnah</i> and this verse are referring to a structure outside the wall of the winding stairwell. On the outside of that wall, there were three balconies, one on top of the other. This structure was called a <i>yetzia</i> in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 6:5, 10.</i>
The wall of the winding stairwell would become narrower on the outside as it ascended. When it reached the surface above the lowest level, it would be indented an entire cubit. The beams supporting the balcony were placed on that cubit of space. When the structure reached the surface above the middle story, which served as the floor of the third story, the thickness of the wall would be further reduced. At that point the wall was indented another cubit, so the ends of the beams could be placed on it. This progressive indentation is alluded to by the phrase, “for he made recesses.”
We are thus forced to say that the empty space inside the wall of the winding stairwell was very narrow. Hence, the wall was thick enough that its outside could be indented a cubit on the middle level and a cubit on the upper level. This would enable us to accept <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation that a similar pattern was followed regarding the other walls.
However, it is unnecessary to offer such an interpretation. <i>Rashi</i> offers it because he equates the term וְרָחֲבָה, which the text uses when discussing the widening of the side-chambers in Yechezkel’s prophecy, with the phrase, “for he made recesses in the House,” mentioned in connection to the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by Shlomoh. Nevertheless, <i>Rambam</i> understands that the recesses were only made in the wall outside the winding stairwell. Thus, they do not relate to the widening of the side-chambers. Moreover, reducing the thickness of one wall by 2 cubits is feasible because one could also reduce the thickness of the wall on the other side. If, however, one is reducing the wall on both sides by 2 cubits, initially the wall would have to be very thick. This would mean that the inner space on the bottom level would be very narrow.
True, it is possible to explain that the intent when saying that the recesses were one cubit is that they were one cubit in total, i.e., half a cubit on each side, as <i>Ri</i> explains, quoted in <i>Tosafos</i> to Tractate <i>Yoma</i>. Nevertheless, since according to <i>Rambam,</i> these recesses are not related to the side-chambers, but rather are features of the outside of the wall, it is not necessary to employ any of these forced explanations. Instead, the word וְרָחֲבָה can be interpreted as meaning that the width of the side-chambers expanded the same amount as the walls of the structure were narrowed, without defining it to a specific figure.
A related understanding is reflected in <i>Targum Yonason,</i> which understands the words וְרָחֲבָה וְנָסְבָה as a single concept. According to this explanation, the <i>vav</i> in וְנָסְבָה is extraneous. This is similar to the <i>vav</i> in the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Daniel</i> 8:13. Although there is a <i>vav</i> before וקֹדֶשׁ, “the holy one,” it appears superfluous, since that word does not seem to be connected to the preceding word, the verbתת .</i> תֵּת וְקֹדֶשׁ וְצָבָא מִרְמָס “enabling the holy place<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary. <i>Metzudos</i> understands וקדש as referring to “the holy nation,” i.e., Israel.</i> and the host to be crushed,” where the <i>vav</i> in the word וקדש seems to be unnecessary. Similarly, in the verse, נִרְדָּם וְרֶכֶב וָסוּס,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tehilim</i> 76:7. In this verse as well, although there is a <i>vav</i> before וְרֶכֶב, that word is not connected to the preceding word, the verb נרדם.</i> “the chariot and the horse slumbered,” the <i>vav</i> in the word ורכב seems to be unnecessary, as <i>Rashi</i> explains in his commentary to those verses. Thus, <i>Targum Yonason</i> translates the verse as, “The spiral stairwell became wider and ascended upward.”
According to this interpretation, this phrase is not referring to the later phrases in the verse, and does not mean that the side-chambers became wider, as <i>Rashi</i> explains. Instead, the verse is teaching us that the spiral stairwell became wider as it ascended. As <i>Targum Yonason</i> continues: “The structure [of the steps] was a spiral, [enabling one] to ascend very high, going round and round the structure.” Therefore, it was wider because of its height. <i>Targum Yonason</i> adds the word לסלקא, “[enabling one] to ascend,” to indicate that the phrase refers to the widening of the stairwell and not to the side-chambers.
Nevertheless, the following phrase, עַל כֵּן רֹחַב לַבַּיִת לְמָעְלָה, must be interpreted as referring to the structure of the side-chambers. It should be interpreted – as <i>Rashi</i> does – as meaning “therefore, the widest level of the structure was the highest.” This phrase teaches us that the side-chambers became wider from level to level. The middle level was wider than the lowest level and the highest level was wider than the middle level. The word therefore refers to the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:6.</i> לִהְיוֹת אֲחוּזִים, “to be held fast,” in the preceding verse.
<i>Radak</i> offers a similar interpretation, stating: “ ‘Therefore’ refers to what was stated previously. Since they were ‘held fast by the walls of the House,’ ‘the widest level of the structure was the highest,’ i.e., the highest tier of the side-chambers was wider than all the others.” There was, however, no fixed measure for the widening of the side-chambers. Instead, the width of the side-chambers expanded according to the extent that the walls of the structure were narrowed, as explained above.
The height of the side-chambers is not explicitly mentioned in Yechezkel’s prophecy. They were evenly divided and each side-chamber and its roof comprised one-third of the height of the Sanctuary. Scripture did not desire to reveal the height of the Sanctuary in the Future<i>,</i> and were it to have informed us of the height of the side-chambers, one would have been able to deduce the height of the Sanctuary. Therefore, their height was not mentioned.
<i>Rashi</i> equates the side-chambers described here with the balconies that existed in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by King Shlomoh, as will be explained below. Concerning the balconies, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 6:10.</i> “And he built the balcony… it was five cubits high.” Similarly, <i>Rashi</i> comments on the term הגזרה,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:12.</i> “<i>Targum Yonason</i> translates it as בְּצוּרְתָא, ‘the tall structure.’ I say that this term refers to the tall edifice of the structure. The low cells around it are called בנין, ‘the building.’ ” Thus, it is obvious that <i>Rashi</i> does not think that the side-chambers were the same height as the main structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
“The building that was on the outer side of the edifice on its western side.” <i>Rashi</i> interprets הגזרה as the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. <i>Radak</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Radak, Yechezkel</i> 41:12.</i> – and similarly, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Abarbanel, Yechezkel</i> 41:14.</i> questions this interpretation, noting that shortly thereafter, the narrative states,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:14<i>.</i></i> “the width of the outer side of the House and the גזרה on the east.” According to <i>Rashi</i>, the verse is redundant, because the גזרה and “the House” are identical.
I do not see the verses as problematic. The phrase, “The building that was on the outer side of the edifice,” refers to the side-chambers built on the outer side of the main structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> Similarly, the phrase, “the width of the outer side of the House,” refers to everything that was built on the outer side of the House, even though it does not specifically say, “and the building,” i.e., the side-chambers.
A similar usage is found in a gloss of <i>Tosafos</i> to tractate <i>Yoma.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 52a, s.v. “<i>kosel</i>”<i>.</i></i> <i>Tosafos</i> cite the verse, “For he made recesses in the House all around,” and explain that although the recesses were made in the wall of the cells which were positioned around the main building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it is still appropriate to say that they were made, “in the House.” In our instance as well, the “outer side of the House” includes what was built on its outer side, i.e., the side-chambers. Afterwards, the verse mentions the גזרה, i.e., the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. It is as if the verse stated that the angel measured what was on the outer side of the House and the House itself, i.e., he measured the width of the side-chambers and the width of the main edifice.
The first verse did not specifically mention the measurement of the width of the House separately because in that verse the angel is narrating as he is standing in the west, and the side-chamber runs across the entire width of the west side of the House. Hence, the angel measured the House and the side-chamber as a single unit. In the second verse, the angel is narrating as he is standing in the east, in front of the House. There were no side-chambers there. Hence, the main edifice is singled out.
As a stylistic technique, the verse varied the terms, using both the term “House” and the term “גזרה”, saying, “the outer side of the House and the גזרה,” instead of saying, “the outer side of the House and the House,” or “the outer side of the גזרה and the גזרה.”
“On its western side, the width [of the building] was 70 cubits.” The <i>Mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> There were 70 cubits from the north to the south. The wall of the winding stairwell was 5 cubits, the winding stairwell was three [cubits wide], the wall of the cell - five, the cell - six, and the wall of the Sanctuary - six, its inner space - twenty, the wall of the Sanctuary - six, the cell - six, the wall of the cell - five, the chamber for water to descend - 3 cubits, and [its] wall - 5 cubits.
It was already explained that the walls mentioned were not solid walls, and that the <i>mishnah</i> is mentioning five spaces, i.e., five cells, on both the northern and southern sides. Similarly, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there will be five cells, referred to as צלעות, “side-chambers,” one next to the other, on the northern and southern sides. The Holy Sanctuary will be situated in the center, and the width of the entire expanse will be 70 cubits.
In general, the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will parallel each other. In particular, however, the width of the side-chambers in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will not be the same as the width of the cells – or the other structures – in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Thus, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:5.</i> “The width of the side-chamber was 4 cubits, surrounding the House all around.” This verse is referring to the side-chamber on the west, saying that it was 4 cubits wide, and that this side-chamber would extend on the north and south sides of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> as well. Thus, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the side-chambers which the <i>mishnah</i> refers to as cells, will be 4 cubits wide. This conception is followed by Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, who comments on the above verse: “Every side-chamber was 4 cubits wide. The side-chambers were connected on the outer side of the three walls of the Sanctuary, [i.e.,] on the west, the north, and the south. This is reflected by the phrase, ‘surrounding the House all around.’ ” Similarly, the alternate interpretation presented by <i>Rashi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 41:9 with regard to the <i>munach</i>.</i> supports Rav Abarbanel’s interpretation, even though it is not entirely germane to this subject.
The difference between the width of the side-chambers in Yechezkel’s vision and that of the cells in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> on the northern and southern sides, was 2 cubits, as the <i>mishnah</i> states: “The cell, six” (for, as mentioned above, the cells in Yechezkel’s vision were only 4 cubits wide). Therefore, these 2 cubits must be added to the other elements of the structure on the northern and southern sides, to produce the width of 70 cubits mentioned in Yechezkel’s prophecy. As will be explained shortly, <i>Rashi</i> compensated for the reduction on the western side by adding to the width of the foundation of its wall. We will follow the same approach and add one cubit to the foundation of the wall of the cell and one cubit to the foundation of the wall of the winding stairwell on the northern and southern sides.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difficulty in adding a cubit to the foundation of the winding stairwell and to the wall of the chamber for water to descend, because they are outer walls and the foundation can protrude beyond the building’s walls. It is, however, difficult to understand how the foundation of the wall of the cell can be expanded without disturbing the layout of the entire structure.</i>
True, the implication of the verse is that the wall of the winding stairwell will be only 5 cubits wide, as the <i>mishnah</i> states, for it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechez­kel</i> 41:12.</i> “The wall of the building was five cubits wide all around.” The implication is that the outer wall of the building, i.e. the wall of the winding stairwell, will be 5 cubits wide “all around,” i.e., on the north and the south. The rationale is that the wall of the winding stairwell is a continuation of the outer wall of the building on the western side, which was also 5 cubits wide, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>9.</i> “The width of the outer wall of the side-chamber was five cubits.” Nevertheless, the verse can be interpreted as referring to the portion above the foundation. The foundation, by contrast, was 6 cubits wide.
Similarly, with regard to the western wall, the narrative explicitly states that the foundation was 6 cubits wide, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>8.</i> “The foundations of the side-chambers were a full measuring rod wide, a space of six cubits.” <i>Rashi</i> explains that this verse is referring to the foundation of the outer wall of the side-chambers, as explained in sec. 63. It is also explained later in this section that the phrase, “Its length was ninety cubits,” includes the 6 cubits of the foundation as part of the length of the House.
Similarly, it can be said that the foundation of the northern and southern walls – which extended from this wall –was 6 cubits wide, and was included in the width of 70 cubits. Indeed, this is indicated by the wording of the narrative, which refers to “the foundations of the side-chambers.” Were that phrase only to be referring to the foundation of the western side-chamber, the verse would have used the singular “side-chamber” instead of the plural “side-chambers.”
Moreover, it appears that using a singular form in that verse would have been appropriate even if it was referring not only to the western wall, but to the entire outer wall, i.e., the wall of the winding stairwell on the north and the corresponding wall on the south, in addition to the western wall. Indeed, the narrative already referred to the entire outer wall – including the western, northern, and southern walls – in the singular in the phrase, “the wall of the building was five cubits wide all around.” Therefore, it can be said that the phrase, “the foundations of the side-chambers,” is written in plural because it includes the foundation of the wall of the cell which was also “a full measuring rod wide.” Thus, we have clear support for our thesis that the cubits that will be reduced from the width of the cell will be added to the width of the foundation of the wall of the winding stairwell and to the foundation of the cell. Just like the width of the foundation of the outer, western wall of the cell is counted in the reckoning of the 100 cubits of the length of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, so too, the width of these two foundations mentioned above are included in the reckoning of the 70 cubits of the width of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
“Its length was 90 cubits. He measured the House as 100 cubits in length.”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>12-13.</i> Although the narrative mentions two different measures of length, they are not contradictory. As <i>Rashi</i> states in his commentary to the verse, the measure of 100 cubits refers to the entire building, as detailed by the <i>mishnah</i> in Tractate <i>Middos:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i>
It was 100 cubits from the east to the west: The wall of the <i>Ulam</i> was 5 cubits, [the <i>Ulam</i> was] 11 cubits wide. The wall of the Sanctuary was six, its length 40. There was the <i>amah traksin</i> (one cubit)<i>,</i> the chamber of the Holy of Holies was 20 cubits, a wall - six, the cell - six, and the wall of the cell - five.
<i>Rashi</i> continues: “Above [i.e., in the narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i>], all of these measures are explicitly stated with the exception of the <i>amah traksin.</i>” As we explained in sec. 58, in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the <i>amah traksin</i> will be replaced by a wall that will be 2 cubits thick. Compensation for the additional cubit will be made by reducing the width of the cell by one cubit. This can be derived from the <i>mishnah,</i> which states that the cell will be 6 cubits wide and its wall - 5, while in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the side-chamber will be 4 cubits wide and its wall - 6, as reflected by the verse, “The foundations of the side-chambers were a full measuring rod wide,” as explained in sec. 63. The width of the foundation is included in the calculation.
The reckoning of 90 cubits only includes the space until <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> – the Chamber of the Knives – which was to the east, and encompassed 10 cubits. In total, the span was 100 cubits, as explained in sec. 50. The verse mentions the two separately, because they were of different width. The main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> (which was 90 cubits long) was only 70 cubits wide. <i>Beis HaChalifos</i>, which was 10 cubits long,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">We have assumed that the term וְהָעֲזרָה is a printing error and the intent is העשרה, “the ten.”</i> was 100 cubits wide. This is also the intent of the <i>mishnah’s</i> statement that “the Sanctuary was narrow at its rear and broad in its front.” This was discussed sufficiently in sec. 50.
In the edifice that was 90 cubits long, the 5 cubits at the end closest to <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> were left empty. There was space between the side-chambers and <i>Beis HaChalifos,</i> to which the cells opened. This space is alluded to by the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:9.</i> “[There was] an [open space] that was <i>munach</i> [before] the structure of the side-chambers of the House.” <i>Targum Yonason</i> also speaks of, “space that was left,” i.e., space that was without a structure, which was the same width as the side-chambers, which was left in the corners of the edifice. Thus, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>11.</i> “The width of the place left open (<i>munach</i>) was five cubits all around.” <i>Rashi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 41:9.</i> explains that, “the phrase ‘all around’ stated with regard to the <i>munach</i> should not be interpreted according to its simple meaning, at all four corners. Instead, it means ‘at either side,’ on the south and on the north, i.e., at the southeast corner and at the northeast corner.”
<i>Rashi</i> also writes regarding the phrase, “an [open space] that was left”: There was an open space left at the southeast corner and at the northeast corner, for the side-chambers would not surround the entire House. The entrances to the side-chambers were in those empty spaces in the eastern corner, as it is written: “The entrance to the side-chamber was from the <i>munach</i>. There was one entrance on the north and one on the south.” For the side-chambers did not have entrances to either the Courtyard or to the Sanctuary. Only those in the northeast and southeast corner had an entrance in the wall. They opened up to this empty space and [the <i>kohanim</i>] would enter this side-chamber through this entrance. From this side-chamber, they would enter the second one, and the third, surrounding [the Sanctuary]. Thus, the <i>mishnah</i> teaches in Tractate <i>Middos:</i> “Each of the cells had three openings: one to the cell on the right, one to the cell on the left, and one to the cell above it. Similarly, the <i>Targum Yonason</i> renders the words וַאֲשֶׁר מֻנָּח, “a place was left.”
Similarly, Rabbeinu Shemayah, <i>Rashi’s</i> student, writes in his commentary to Tractate <i>Middos:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:3.</i> “Also in the building described by Yechezkel, there were 5 cubits left open before the final cell whose space was positioned opposite the thickness of the wall of the Sanctuary, according to the reckoning.” With God’s help, his words will be cited below at length.
<i>Rashi’s</i> statement cited above – that the side-chambers did not open up to the Sanctuary – should not be understood as meaning that none of the side-chambers opened up to the Sanctuary. Rather, <i>Rashi’s</i> intent appears to be that the opening of the side-chamber to the Sanctuary was not used to enter and exit the side-chambers, for the reason to be explained below. Therefore, it was necessary that there be an entrance to the <i>munach</i> that could be used to enter and exit the side-chambers.
What compelled me to such an interpretation is that the <i>mishnah</i> states:<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Rabbeinu Shemayah's commentary on <i>Middos</i> 4:2.</i> “[The cell] in the northeast corner had five openings: one to the cell on its right, one to the cell above it, one to the winding stairwell, one towards the wicket,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 52 above.</i> and one to the Sanctuary.” <i>Rambam</i> in his <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah,</i> explains:<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:11.</i> “The cell<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, <i>Rambam</i> interprets the five cells, not as being positioned from the east to the west, but from the north to the south, each one parallel to the entire length of the Sanctuary.</i> in the northeast corner on the second level<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously in this section, there were three levels of cells.</i> had five entrances: one to the cell on its right,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam</i> uses the expressions right and left assuming one is facing the Holy of Holies, i.e., north and south respectively.</i> one to the cell above it, one to the winding stairwell, one towards the cell with the wicket, and one to the Sanctuary.”
<i>Rambam</i>’s intent can be understood based on his commentary to the above <i>mishnah:</i> “This cell was on the middle level of the cells. It was above the winding stairwell.” Thus, it had five openings: a) one to the cell on its right, which was the wall of the winding stairwell<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, in his commentary to this <i>mishnah</i>, <i>Rambam</i> understands the term “the wall of the winding stairwell” as being the name of one of the cells.</i> which had empty space in its midst that was called a cell, b) one to the cell above it, because it was on the second level and there was a third level above it, c) one to the winding stairwell below it that enabled one to ascend to it, and d) one to the cell that has the wicket entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> will explain the fifth opening to the cell below.</i> With regard to the latter opening, in his commentary to the above <i>mishnah, Rambam</i> writes: “The intent is [to refer] to the cell within which was located the northern wicket through which one would enter the Sanctuary. This is the opening that existed on the northern side of the cell that had five openings.”
To explain <i>Rambam’s</i> words: <i>Rambam</i> confronted a conceptual difficulty because the wicket entrance was near the Sanctuary, i.e., in the wall of the Sanctuary itself. According to <i>Rambam,</i> who understands the cells as being positioned from north to south, a question arises: How is it possible that the cell above the winding stairwell will have an opening to the wicket entrance? There are two other cells – named “the cell” and “the wall of the cell” – between this cell and the wicket entrance. Therefore, <i>Rambam</i> explains that the <i>mishnah’s</i> words, “one to the wicket entrance,” do not mean to the wicket entrance itself, but to the cell that was next to the wicket entrance, i.e., to the cell that was named “the cell.” It appears to me that the words, “in the northern side of the cell,” in <i>Rambam’s</i> commentary are an error, and the text should read, “to the southern side.”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, Rav Kapach’s translation of <i>Rambam’s</i> Commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i> states: “to the cell on the left,” which, when facing the Holy of Holies, refers to the south.</i>
The <i>mishnah’s</i> statement that the fifth opening was “to the Sanctuary” does not mean to the Sanctuary itself, but rather “to the wicket that led to the Sanctuary,” for these openings were positioned facing each other. Hence, this opening was also referred to as an opening of the cell over the winding stairwell, even though in truth this cell did not have an opening to the wicket, and certainly not to the Sanctuary, but rather to the open spaces, i.e., the cells, between the wicket and the Sanctuary. This is the explanation of <i>Rambam’s</i> words. If they are not, I pray that I be able to understand their interpretation.
Nevertheless, it appears to me that the <i>mishnah</i> should be interpreted differently. The <i>mishnah</i> is referring to the lowest level of cells and the cell referred to as “the cell.” The cell referred to as “the cell” had five openings. The <i>mishnah</i> mentions the northeast corner, i.e., in contrast to the southeast corner, for regarding the wicket on the southeast, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 44:2.</i> “This gate shall be closed. It will not be opened.” Hence, it is not appropriate to mention the opening to the wicket and the opening to the Sanctuary from that side. This interpretation also fits according to <i>Rambam.</i> The statement in the <i>mishnah</i> can thus be interpreted: “one to the cell on the right,” i.e., to the cell in the wall of this cell, “one to the cell above it, one to the winding stairwell,” i.e., via its wall, “one to the cell that has the wicket,” i.e., to the cell in the wall of the Sanctuary, “and one to the Sanctuary,” i.e., via this wicket.
This interpretation is based on the concept that these two cells – the wall of the cell and the wall of the Sanctuary – are called “walls,” although they are empty spaces. These cells are called “walls” because they were not constructed for their own sake, i.e., because there was a need for cells. Instead, there was a need for walls and, by the way, as it were, the wall was made hollow and, as a result, a cell was made. Accordingly, if there is an entrance that opens from the cell that is unique in that it is called “the cell”<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it was given that name because it was made to serve this purpose.</i> to the Sanctuary or to the winding stairwell, the entrance is identified with the cell called “the cell,” even though it passes through these hollow walls. True, these walls were hollow and are called cells. Nevertheless, they are identified with the walls, as evidenced by their names, “the wall of the cell,” and, “the wall of the Sanctuary,” as explained.
For this reason, the <i>mishnah</i> does not mention that this cell had an opening to the empty space in the wall of the winding stairwell. The winding stairwell is a unique and significant entity.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the cells named “the wall of….”</i> Even if the opening from the winding stairwell to the hollow in its wall<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fifth cell was called “the wall of the winding stairwell.” In this instance as well, the empty space was not a significant entity; what was important was the wall.</i> was positioned opposite the opening leading from “the cell” to the winding stairwell, the opening from the winding stairwell to the cell in its wall would not be identified with “the cell” – i.e., the sixth opening to “the wall of the winding stairwell” would not be mentioned – because the opening is from the winding stairwell and is identified with the winding stairwell (since the winding stairwell is a significant entity). The <i>mishnah</i> does, however, discuss openings from “the cell” to the winding stairwell that is on its side, and to the Sanctuary, even though there are two spaces – “the wall of the cell” and “the wall of the Sanctuary” between “the cell” and the Sanctuary. The rationale is that these two empty spaces were not given an independent name. Therefore, the two openings mentioned above were not identified with them but with the cell given the name “the cell.” The <i>mishnah</i> did, nevertheless, count the openings from “the cell” to the empty spaces between the walls because these cavities are referred to as a cell, and there are openings from them to “the cell.” This appears to be how the <i>mishnah</i> should be understood<i>.</i>
According to <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation, by contrast, an explanation is required as to why these openings are identified with this cell, for the explanation given above cannot be employed according to <i>Rambam’s</i> understanding. Furthermore, it is necessary to understand why <i>Rambam</i> does not count the opening to the “wall of the cell” – i.e., the empty space within the walls – as he counts the opening to “the wall of the winding stairwell,” for it appears that the phrase “the opening to the cell on its right” mentioned by the <i>mishnah</i> refers to the “wall of the cell.” One might posit that the phrase “the cell on its right” means the cell on the left side when facing the Holy of Holies, which can be called on its right because it is south of “the cell.”<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In general, the south is identified with the right.</i> In that instance, one can ask the opposite: Why isn’t the opening to the wall of the winding stairwell mentioned? A labored explanation can be given that the <i>mishnah</i> cites the opening to the cell called “the wall of the cell” because it focuses on the openings leading to the Sanctuary. Nevertheless, this explanation is not satisfactory in my eyes.
Whatever the explanation is, it is obvious that it is possible to go from the Sanctuary to the cells, for the cells have openings to the Sanctuary. What compelled me to interpret <i>Rashi’s</i> statement that they did not open to the Sanctuary as meaning “they did not have an opening used for entering and departing” is that it would be very denigrating if one would not walk to the cells directly, but instead passed through the Sanctuary of the King, blessed be He, using the Sanctuary as a shortcut.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yevamos</i> 6b.</i> Furthermore, one who enters the Sanctuary while not performing an act of service is deserving of lashes, as stated in Tractate <i>Menachos.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Menachos</i> 27b. Entering the Sanctuary while not involved in the service of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> violates the prohibition (<i>Vayikra</i> 16:2): “Do not enter into the holy place at all times.”</i> Indeed, <i>Sefer Mitzvos Gadol</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sefer Mitzvos Gadol,</i> negative commandment 303.</i> states that this applies even when one enters to prostrate himself<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This is noteworthy since prostration is an act of homage permitted to the <i>kohanim.</i></i> at a time other than after completing an act of sacrificial service.
Although <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation of this <i>mishnah</i> – and our interpretation – does not fit with <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding of the <i>mishnah</i> as will be explained, even <i>Rashi</i> would agree that the cells have an opening to the Sanctuary. Indeed, the aforementioned <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:3.</i> explicitly states: “one to the Sanctuary.”
In truth, it was not necessary to elaborate in the explanation of the <i>mishnah</i> at all, for the <i>mishnah</i> clearly states that the cells opened to the Sanctuary, and the point we made would have been stated. Nevertheless, through this discussion, other issues were clarified.
The <i>mishnah</i> does not mention if there was a parallel to the <i>munach,</i> the empty space, in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and the commentaries do not discuss this point. Only Rabbeinu Shemayah<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See his commentary to <i>Middos, loc. cit.</i></i> raises this question.
“The width of the outer side of the House and the edifice on the east was one hundred cubits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:14.</i> I have already explained that the term, “the outer side of the House” refers to the side-chambers built on the outer side of the main structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> and that the term, “the edifice” refers to the main building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The total width on the eastern side was 100 cubits. Even though the width of the western side was 70 cubits, the width of the eastern side was 100, because the <i>Ulam,</i> the vestibule, extended an additional 15 cubits on each side. Adding these 30 cubits to the 70 cubits of the main structure produces a total of 100 cubits, as explained at length in sec. 50. Consult that source.
Based on these explanations, the 30 side-chambers were divided, with 15 on the north side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and 15 on the south side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The 15 side-chambers on the north side were built as follows: The wall of the Sanctuary measured 6 cubits, and was hollow, containing a cell. On its (northern) side was the cell called “the cell,” which was 4 cubits wide. Next to the cell was the cell called “the wall of the cell.” Its foundation was 6 cubits wide, but it was only 5 cubits wide above the ground, i.e., the wall and the cell it contained were 5 cubits wide. That cell was the third of the five cells on the lowest level of the northern side. To its north was another cell, which measured 3 cubits wide, and which the <i>Mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:3,7.</i> refers to as, “the winding stairwell.” The foundation of the wall of the winding stairwell<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This wall was the outer wall of the main structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> was 6 cubits wide. However above the ground, the wall and the cell contained in this wall were only 5 cubits wide. This cell which is referred to as “the winding stairwell” was the fifth cell on the lowest level of the northern side.
There was a second story on top of these cells, which also contained five cells. There was a third story, which also contained five cells, on top of the second story. Thus, there were 15 cells on the north side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the foundation of which was 25 cubits long.
There was a parallel structure made up of 15 cells on the southern side. However, the fourth cell on the southern side was not called “the winding stairwell” in the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> but rather, “the chamber for water to descend.”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, the wall to the cell's south was called, “the wall of the chamber for water to descend.”</i> Thus, the south side of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was also 25 cubits long.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This including the additional width of a cubit for the foundation, as explained above.</i> In the center was the Holy Sanctuary that was 20 cubits wide and the total width of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was 70 cubits. This was true with the exception of the easternmost 10 cubits. Those 10 cubits included an additional 15 cubits in width on each side. That area was called <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> – the Chamber of the Knives.
Thus, only the easternmost 10 cubits in the length of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> were 100 cubits wide. The portion of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that was 90 cubits long was only 70 cubits wide. Within this 90-cubit area, there was 5 cubits of open space, which did not have any structure built on it. This open area was called “the <i>munach.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:11.</i> It comprised these five cubits and adjoined<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>munach</i> was to the west of <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i></i> <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> All four outer side-chambers (the wall of the winding stairwell, the winding stairwell, the wall of the cell, and the cell, on the north side; and the four on the south, the wall of the chamber for water to descend, the chamber for water to descend, the wall of the cell, and the cell) end at the beginning of the <i>munach</i>. The opening to “the cell” was within this space. From it, one would enter the other cells adjoining “the cell” and above “the cell”. Thus, it is written: “The entrance to the side-chamber was from the empty space that was left. There was one entrance on the north and one on the south.”
All the above explanations are based on <i>Rambam’s</i> statements,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:5; see <i>Rambam, Middos</i> 4:3, 7.</i> and those of Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bartenura, Middos, op. cit.</i></i> who follows <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation. <i>Rambam</i> and Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura’s opinions diverge from that of Rabbeinu Shemayah, <i>Rashi’s</i> disciple. It should be noted that this is so even though Rav Ovadiah saw Rabbeinu Shemayah’s work, as is evident from his commentary in many places in this tractate and in others. From <i>Ra’avad’s</i> comments<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ra'avad's</i> comments on <i>Rambam’s Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 4:9,11.</i> regarding the order of the cells, it appears that he too accepts the basis of <i>Rambam’s</i> approach.
<i>Rashi,</i> in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> and, hence, in his understanding of the <i>mishnah,</i> follows a different conception. <i>Rashi</i> comments on the term, <i>hatzlaos,</i> “the side-chambers”:<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 41:6. The Hebrew word <i>tzelaos,</i> translated as “side-chambers,” literally means “ribs.” These chambers resembled ribs, because they surrounded the Sanctuary the same way the ribs surround the chest. Occasionally, the side-chambers are referred to as תאים, “cells.”</i> I say that the cells in the north and the south were arranged in the following manner: Each one was 12 cubits long. Thus the five cells comprised 60 cubits. There were five walls, each 5 cubits thick, bringing the total to 85 [cubits]. Together with the 5 cubits of the <i>munach,</i> the 90 cubits of the northern wall have thus been encompassed. The same applies to the south. On the west, there was one cell on top of another, and a third on top of it. They were 20 cubits long, parallel to the Holy of Holies. Thus, there were eleven [cells] in each row. Similarly, <i>Targum Yonason</i> speaks of there being “eleven in a row.”
Thus, <i>Rashi</i> understands that the five cells ran along the length of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, one after the other. There is a severe difficulty in <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation. <i>Rashi</i> states that the five cells have five walls. However, for five spaces to be enclosed on both sides, six walls are necessary. According to <i>Rashi’s</i> explanation, we would have to say that the outer cell was entirely open on one side. This would be a forced and unnecessary interpretation. After all, why should one wall be removed in order to increase the length of the cells? This question is particularly relevant since it is architectural practice to strengthen the outer part of a structure.
It is difficult to say that in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> the last cell in the row, i.e., the southwest and the northwest cells, was closed because it was flush with the wall of the courtyard, for we know that in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there were 11 cubits of open space between the Holy of Holies and the wall of the courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 5:1.</i> Furthermore, it is difficult to conceive that in the Third <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this space will be eliminated. Moreover, the cell in the west has an outer wall, for that wall is included in the calculation of the 100 cubits that make up the length of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Thus, if the wall of the courtyard will be flush with the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there would be two walls, one after the other. That is not logical.
The interpretation of Rabbenu Shemayah, <i>Rashi’s</i> student, does not present such difficulties. He states:<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rabbeinu Shemayah, Middos</i> 4:3.</i> The width of the cell is explicitly stated, “the cell, six.” A measure for its length, by contrast, was not stated, neither by the <i>mishnah,</i> nor by Scripture. According to the reckoning in the prophet’s words,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the narrative in <i>Yechezkel.</i></i> our revered teacher of blessed memory must allocate 16 cubits for the combined length of each cell and its wall. According to Scripture, there are only 33 cells. One on the west, across the entire span of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, and five on the north and the south. <i>Targum Yonason</i> thus states that there will be “eleven [side-chambers] in a row.” There were another eleven [side-chambers] on top of them, and eleven [more] on top of them. My teacher also edited [his commentary] in this manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">This change is not, however, found in the standard editions of <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary.</i>
Thus, one can count 5 cubits for the wall of each cell, and 11 cubits for the length of each cell. Five times sixteen equals 80. There were an additional 5 cubits for the <i>munach,</i> and 5 cubits for the sixth wall of the cell, bringing the total to 90 cubits, which is the length of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> until <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> Rabbeinu Shemayah did not specifically mention that wall, just as he did not specifically mention the <i>munach,</i> because his intent was only to mention the length of each cell. He then mentioned the wall of the cell parenthetically, including the cell and its wall in the same figure. He included the wall of the cell together with the cell, because the wall between each cell was not mentioned in the <i>mishnah.</i> When the <i>mishnah</i> mentions the wall of the cell, and when the Scripture mentions the wall of the side-chamber, they only refer to the outer wall. Accordingly, Rabbeinu Shemayah did not mention the sixth wall of the cells because it was an extension of the outer wall of the cell on the west. Thus, the sixth wall of the cells is included in the outer wall of the cell on the west.
According to these Rabbis,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>Rashi</i> and Rabbeinu Shemayah.</i> the wall of the Sanctuary was actually six cubits thick and the wall of the cell, five cubits. They did not have a hollow of open space. This is reflected in <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation of the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:6. See also the explanation of this verse above, towards the beginning of this section.</i> “They approached the wall of the House [near] the side-chambers all around so that they would be held fast, but they were not attached inside the walls of the House”:
The beams of the higher stories of the extension: their ends that were on the side of the outer partitions of the side-chambers would approach and be inserted within the inner side of the [outer] wall [of the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> This is reflected in the phrase,] “the wall of the House [near] the side-chambers,” i.e., [it refers to] the side [of the wall] that was inside the side-chambers. Thus, the verse states, “they would be held fast,” in the wall of the House, i.e., strongly affixed. [The words] “they were not attached inside the walls of the House” teaches that the ends of the beams that were on the side of the wall of the Sanctuary did [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The word “not” is a translator’s addition that follows from the context, but is not found either in the text of <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> or in the original text produced by <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i>.</i> penetrate into the wall. Instead, they would rest on the indentation of the wall of the Sanctuary that would jut into the side-chambers, similar to the description of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> built by Shlomoh:<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 6:6.</i> “For he made recesses in the outer part of the House all around.” “The lowest story of the extension was 5 cubits wide.” Its ceiling was 6 cubits, for the wall of the Sanctuary was recessed for a cubit. Thus, the width of the open space in the lowest cell was 5 cubits. “The middle story was 6 cubits wide and the upper story, 7 cubits wide.” [The side-chambers in Yechezkel’s vision] also followed this pattern.
By saying “they were not attached inside the walls of the House,” the verse implies that the beams would not penetrate the walls of the Sanctuary like the ends of other low beams, for which holes are made in the walls and the ends of the beams are inserted in the hole.
<i>Rashi’s</i> commentary continues on the beginning of the following verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 41:7.</i> “They became wider”: The side-chambers became progressively wider as they ascended. The middle story was a cubit wider than the first, and the third story was a cubit wider than the second.
According to <i>Rashi’s</i> conception, the wall of the Sanctuary was 6 cubits thick. Hence, even if the wall’s thickness was reduced by 2 cubits because of the indents, it would remain substantial. We did not want to offer such an interpretation according to <i>Rambam’s</i> understanding, because his conception is that the wall is hollow. Hence, we explained these verses differently above.
Regarding the 70 cubits of the width of the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> Rabbeinu Shemayah writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbeinu Shemayah</i>, <i>Middos</i> 4:6.</i> The winding stairwell and its wall were 8 cubits wide. The chamber for water to descend and its wall were 8 cubits in width. This was the measure [for the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> recorded in] the <i>Mishnah.</i> However, with regard to [Yechezkel’s account in] Scripture, these measures must be made 20 cubits wide<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., ten cubits on each side.</i> because the width of the cell on both sides is reduced, for it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechez­kel</i> 41:5.</i> “The width of the side-chamber was 4 cubits.” Thus, he reduced the width of the cells [a total of] 4 cubits, [2 cubits on] either side. To compensate, the winding stairwell and [the chamber for water] to descend were expanded [a total of] 4 cubits to fill the 70 cubits of the width of the Sanctuary at its rear. Our teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>Rashi.</i></i> resolved [the matter] in this way to me and my colleagues.
We, however, calculated a width of 70 cubits by proving from the narrative that the foundation of the wall of the cell and the foundation of the wall of the winding stairwell were each 6 cubits thick. This explanation also agrees with <i>Rashi</i>’s commentary. For this reason, in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Yechezkel</i> 41:12.</i> <i>Rashi</i> cited the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> without any explanation. Thus, <i>Rashi</i> was able to derive the width of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> from the <i>mishnah</i>, making the necessary increases or reductions either in the manner explained by his student Rabbeinu Shemayah, or as we explained it according to the verses of the narrative. According to <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding, the winding stairwell<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the intent is not the cell named “the winding stairwell,” but the actual stairwell that allowed access to the higher levels of the structure.</i> was not mentioned at all in Yechezkel’s narrative, for he does not include the winding stairwell as one of the 30 side-chambers. Nevertheless, the verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechez­kel</i> 41:12.</i> “On the western side, it was 70 cubits wide,” compels one to calculate how to reach that width. It appears that it should be reached in the manner described.
Remember that <i>Radak</i> and Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel interpreted the term <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:7.</i>וְנָסְבָה as referring to the winding stairwell.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>I Melachim</i> 6:8: “They went up with winding stairs to the middle chamber, and from the middle chamber to the third.”</i> True, <i>Rashi</i> did not, instead saying that it refers to the steps leading from the lower level to the middle level and the upper level. Nevertheless, we can accept <i>Rashi</i>’s interpretation in one place and that of <i>Radak</i> in another.
Since we discussed the <i>mishnah’s</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:3.</i> statement that the cell in the northeast corner had five openings according to our understanding of <i>Rambam’s</i> perspective, it is appropriate to offer an explanation according to <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding, even though it is not of immediate relevance to the matter at hand.
Rabbeinu Shemayah comments on the <i>mishnah’s</i> statements,<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>2.</i> “He opened the wicket”: <b>He opened the wicket</b> on the northern side of the Great Gate, <b>and entered the cell</b>, one of the cells in the northeastern corner of the Sanctuary, the last one in the row. Part of its empty space was parallel to the thickness of the eastern wall of the Sanctuary. They hollowed out the portion of the wall of the Sanctuary that was parallel to the cell and made an opening in the cell through which he could enter the middle of the Sanctuary’s wall. There, he could proceed until he reached the northern wicket near the Great Gate. Thus, he could enter the cell from the wicket. In addition, they made an opening from that cell toward the inner space of the Sanctuary. He would enter the Sanctuary from the cell and open the inner gates of the Sanctuary.
On the following <i>mishnah,</i> Rabbeinu Shemayah states: <b>In the northeast corner</b>, i.e., the cell that was in the northeast corner of the Sanctuary, <b>had five openings.</b> This follows the Sages’ understanding. One of the openings led to the wicket, and one [opening led] to the Sanctuary, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This is referring to the commentary cited immediately beforehand.</i> One [opening] led to the winding stairwell, as the <i>mishnah</i> states later on,<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 4:5.</i> <b>the winding stairwell would ascend from the northeast corner to the northwest corner.</b> Via it, one would ascend to the roofs of the cells. And in Scripture it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:11.</i> <b>The side-chamber opened to the <i>munach</i>. It had one entrance to the north and one entrance to the south.</b> I maintain that in the northeast corner of this last side-chamber, they made an opening at the corner’s edge. For this side-chamber, which opened to the <i>munach</i> in its front at the east, is slanted to the north, since this corner relates to two dimensions. At its northern side was the bottom of the winding stairwell that proceeded at the side of all the cells, and its bottom ended in the corner of this cell. Thus, they would ascend to the winding stairwell from the opening in the corner. [According to this explanation,] the words of the <i>mishnah</i> and Scripture agree. If there was no <i>munach</i> in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the entire opening can be relegated to the north, i.e., this is<b> the one to the winding stairwell</b> mentioned by the <i>mishnah.</i>
According to his explanation, the <i>mishnah’s</i> statement that this cell had five openings can be understood according to its simple meaning, without resorting to the forced explanations we used to resolve <i>Rambam’s</i> interpretation or my own interpretation that followed <i>Rambam’s</i> basic premise. Similarly, the remainder of Rabbeinu Shemayah’s words is self-explanatory.
His statement that, “part of its empty space was opposite the thickness of the eastern wall of the Sanctuary,”<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">See p. 211 above which mentions the <i>munach.</i></i> is also easily explained: <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> was opposite the 5 cubits on the eastern side of the <i>Ulam,</i> as stated in sec. 50. Behind it, were the 5 cubits of the <i>munach.</i> Thus, there is still another cubit of the width of the <i>Ulam</i> remaining, for the <i>Ulam</i> was 11 cubits wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 4:7.</i> That cubit was opposite the cell’s outer wall, which was 5 cubits thick like all the other cell walls. Thus, 4 cubits of the cell’s wall are opposite the eastern wall of the Sanctuary, which is 6 cubits thick, as explained in sec. 51. It follows that there are 2 cubits from the open space of the cell that are opposite the width of the Sanctuary’s eastern wall.
To summarize, according to the words of these Rabbis the thirty cells are arranged in the following manner: The five northern cells are positioned one after the other. The length of each cell and its wall was 16 cubits. There was a sixth wall, since six walls are necessary to create five cells. The sixth wall – like the other walls – was 5 cubits thick. The <i>munach</i> was in front of the cells, and comprised 5 cubits. This entire arrangement was located outside the Sanctuary’s wall. That wall itself was 6 cubits thick on the north and on the south.
In the north and in the south, there was a second group of five cells on top of these five cells. There was a third story of cells built on top of the second. The cell’s outer wall was 5 cubits thick, and its foundation was 6 cubits thick. This was the wall of all the cells, as is self-evident. The winding stairwell was outside the wall. The winding stairwell was 3 cubits thick. Its outer wall was 5 cubits thick, and its foundation was 6 cubits thick. The outer cell on the northeast and southeast sides had five openings: to the <i>munach,</i> to the winding stairwell, to the cell behind it, to the wicket, and to the Sanctuary.
This reflects the <i>Rashi’s</i> primary interpretation in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:9, as explained by his student Rabbeinu Shemayah. The alternate explanation offered there by <i>Rashi</i> is self-evident and need not be cited, with the exception of his interpretation of the words, “[The side-chambers] entered the wall… and became progressively wider,”<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:6-7.</i> which must be understood as we explained these phrases according to <i>Rambam.</i> We did not cite <i>Rashi’s</i> second explanation here, because Rabbeinu Shemayah stated that <i>Rashi</i>, his teacher, edited his text to reflect the primary interpretation.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Nowhere in the existing printings of <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary is this explicitly stated.</i> Similarly, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explains <i>Rashi’s</i> perspective according to the primary interpretation. Also, a manuscript copy of <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary does not include the second interpretation. Maybe it was added from comments a student might have written on the margin.
Even though <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary accords with both Scripture and the <i>Mishnah,</i> nevertheless, we designed the sketch of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that we produced<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned, that design was not published in the original text of <i>Tzuras HaBayis</i>, but rather printed separately and included as an insert to the book. It is reproduced as a foldout in the back of this printing.</i> according to <i>Rambam’s</i> understanding because the treatment of the subject at large follows his interpretation in his Commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i>. This is reflected in the fact that Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, who was the last authority who wrote on the subject, saw Rabbeinu Shemayah’s interpretation which follows <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding and, nevertheless, chose to follow <i>Rambam’s</i> perspective. True, <i>Tosafos,</i> in Tractate <i>Yoma</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yoma</i> 52a, <i>s.v. kosel.</i></i> and <i>Bava Basra</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Basra</i> 61a, <i>s.v. vehatei.</i></i> follow <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding, but it is possible that they did not see <i>Rambam’s</i> words, for his writings had not been widely publicized at that time. In particular, this is true of <i>Rambam’s</i> Commentary to the <i>Mishnah,</i> which was translated into the Holy Tongue<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam</i> wrote his commentary to the <i>Mishnah</i> in Arabic.</i> only in the days of <i>Rashba.</i>
Nevertheless, having explained <i>Rashi’s</i> perspective above, it would only be proper to make a picture of these cells from which his understanding of these cells would be apparent.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">That drawing is reproduced on p. 209 above.</i>

Chapter 65



Chapter 66



Chapter 67



Chapter 68



Chapter 69



Chapter 70

“Between the chambers [and the House], there was [a space] twenty cubits around the House to either side.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:10.</i> There were chambers built in the outer courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the phrase “the outer courtyard” does not apply to “the Women’s Courtyard” that surrounded the entire inner courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> but to the portion of the courtyard that is separated from the Altar. The area directly around the Altar was surrounded by a wall and called “the inner courtyard.” Although the Twenty Cubit Space described here is outside that wall, it is fit to have sacrifices of the most sacred order cooked and eaten within it. Therefore, it is also referred to (<i>Yechezkel</i> 42:3) as “the inner courtyard” in certain contexts.</i> to the north and the south of the cells, as will be explained in sec. 71. This verse explains that there was a space of 20 cubits between the cells and those chambers. The words סָבִיב סָבִיב, which literally mean, “all around,” is translated as, “to either side,” i.e., to the north and to the south, as explained above<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 64-69.</i> with regard to the <i>munach.</i>
The chambers (referred to as לְּשָׁכוֹת) mentioned here are those mentioned in sec. 71. That term can also be used to refer to the cells, because it is a general term that can refer to all the rooms in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, regardless of their particular nature.
The 20 cubits between the cells and the chambers on each side include: a) 15 cubits equal to the length that the <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> protruded beyond the Sanctuary on the northern and southern sides and b) an additional 5 cubits. This entire space is referred to as “the inner courtyard,” as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:3.</i> “Opposite the twenty [cubits] of the inner courtyard.”
“And He brought me through the entranceway… to the Holy Chambers… that faced northward. Behold, there was a place at its western flanks. And he said to me, ‘This is the place where the <i>kohanim</i> will cook the guilt-offering and the sin-offerings, where they will bake the meal-offering, so as not to bring them out to the Outer Courtyard, [lest] they mingle with the people.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:19-20, translated according to <i>Targum Yonason</i> and <i>Rashi.</i></i> Although the verse only mentions the northern side, it is logical to assume that the Twenty Cubit Space on the southern side was consecrated for the same purpose. After all, why should one side be different from the other?
It is, however, possible to explain that meat from the sacrifices of the most sacred order is more often found in the north because these sacrifices must be slaughtered there.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Zevachim</i> 47a.</i> It is even possible to say that just as for all time, the north was designated as the place where these sacrifices must be slaughtered, in the Ultimate Future, it will also be designated as the place where they must be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This hypothesis requires explanation for <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:13 specifically states that these sacrifices can be eaten in the Twenty Cubit Space on the south.</i>

Chapter 71

“He brought me to the chamber<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The term לשכה, “chamber,” sometimes appears in the singular and sometimes in the plural, for the two chambers themselves consisted of many smaller chambers.</i> that was opposite the edifice,”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:1.</i> i.e., the Sanctuary, “and opposite the structure,” i.e., the cells, “on the north.” These chambers did not exist in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. It is possible that to parallel them, they built the Chamber of the Hearth and the four chambers that it contained. Nevertheless, the Chamber of the Hearth was situated in a different place, and was not opposite the Sanctuary. Nor was it on both sides of the Courtyard, only on the north, as stated in Tractates <i>Tamid</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tamid</i> 3:3.</i> and <i>Middos.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Middos</i> 1:6.</i>
The place where these chambers were built is called, “the Outer Courtyard,” as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:1.</i> “And he led me out to the Outer Courtyard in the direction leading north, and he brought me into the chamber.”
“The length,”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>8. The term length here refers to the distance from north to south, i.e., the distance from the Temple building to the wall of the Outer Courtyard. It was 120 cubits long, while the portion of the courtyard that ran from east to west was only 100 cubits wide.</i> i.e., the measure from north to south, “of the chambers located in the Outer Courtyard was fifty cubits.” In this verse, the term “length” refers to the width of the chambers, because the end of the verse discusses the chambers’ length: “Behold, in front of the Sanctuary,” i.e., the entire span of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> from east to west, including the <i>Ulam,</i> the Sanctuary, the Holy of Holies, and the cell behind it “was 100 cubits [long].”
In front of these chambers was a courtyard 50 cubits wide, as indicated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>2.</i> “Facing the 100 cubit length at the northern entrance was [a space] 50 cubits wide.” <i>Rashi</i> comments: Opposite the entrance to the chamber that faces the north, to the Outer Courtyard, there is a span 50 cubits wide until the northern wall of the Outer Courtyard. <b>The 100 cubit length</b>, i.e., the chambers comprised a length of 100 cubits from the east to the west. Their width from the north to the south was 50 cubits, as stated below. The open space of the Outer Courtyard was 100 cubits in length, as was stated previously,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:23.</i> “He measured 100 cubits from one gate to another.” Thus, there was an empty space 100 cubits long and 50 cubits wide in front of these chambers.
The verse’s statement that the length of the courtyard in front of the chambers measured 100 cubits, only refers to the portion of the courtyard that faced the chambers. The length of the courtyard itself extended far further eastward, continuing past the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the Courtyard of the Israelites, and even further. Similarly, the courtyard extended further to the west, as the <i>Mishnah</i> states in the last chapter of Tractate <i>Middos,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:1. See sec. 74 where <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> elaborates concerning this subject.</i> “11 cubits behind the Holy of Holies.” Similarly, <i>Rashi</i> explains at the end of <i>Parshas Terumah,</i> that in the Sanctuary built by Moshe, 20 cubits of the area encompassed by the lace hangings were to the west, behind the Tent of the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shmos</i> 27:18.</i> It is befitting to leave this empty space, because it enabled the watchmen to walk around the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> inside the courtyard. Indeed, Tractate <i>Tamid</i> relates<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tamid</i> 1:3.</i> that each morning the <i>kohanim</i> would walk around the courtyard and inspect the sacrificial vessels located there.
“The wall facing the outside, [the space] opposite the chambers, leading to the Outer Courtyard before the chambers, was 50 cubits long.”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:7.</i> <i>Rashi</i> comments: “The wall facing the outside: [The wall of the chamber] that separated between the chambers and the Outer Courtyard on the eastern side was 50 cubits long from north to south.” One may conclude that the corresponding wall of the chamber on the western side was also 50 cubits long. <i>Rashi</i> only mentions the eastern side because the verse only speaks of one wall, and <i>Rashi</i> thinks that it is referring to the wall that faced the larger portion of the courtyard.
Alternatively, <i>Rashi</i> may have mentioned the wall of the chamber on the eastern side because it was exactly 50 cubits. In contrast, the wall of the chamber on the western side was one cubit longer. To explain: Above, in sec. 70, it was stated that the chambers began 5 cubits from <i>Beis HaChalifos.</i> The wall of the Inner Courtyard was built at the end of those 5 cubits on the east side. This wall separated between the Inner Courtyard and the Outer Courtyard, and was 6 cubits thick. Five of those cubits were opposite the 5 cubits mentioned immediately beforehand, and there was an additional cubit. From this wall outward, the Outer Courtyard extends 100 cubits, as explained in sec. 22. This creates an apparent contradiction between two verses: One verse states<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:8. As mentioned above, although the verse uses the term length, it is describing the chambers' width.</i> that the chambers were 50 cubits wide, while another verse<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:2.</i> speaks of “[a space] 50 cubits wide.” This refers to the width of the courtyard in front of the chambers, as we explained above. According to the reckoning above, which calculated that the chambers included 1 cubit that adjoins the wall of the Inner Courtyard, that cubit is not included in the measure mentioned in either verse. Where will it be?
Thus, the verses seem to contradict each other. It is a third verse that resolves this contradiction.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the original, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> refers to the last of Rabbi Yishmael’s <i>Thirteen Principles of Biblical Exegesis</i>.</i> By stating,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:7.</i> “The wall facing the outside…,” it resolved that apparent contradiction. As <i>Rashi</i> explains, this verse is only referring to the wall on the east. That wall was 50 cubits long, and faced the Outer Courtyard. The chambers themselves were 51 cubits wide. On the eastern side, however, the extra cubit did not require a wall, because it was enclosed by the wall of the Inner Courtyard. For that reason, <i>Rashi</i> understands that the verse only mentions one wall, the wall of the eastern chamber. The wall of the western chamber, by contrast, was 51 cubits long, because there was nothing else enclosing it.
“Their entrances [opened] to the north.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:4.</i> Thus, the chambers opened toward the Outer Courtyard. This is evident from the fact that the verse specifically mentions the entrances in the north. In his commentary to the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 46:19.</i> “And he brought me through the entranceway.” <i>Rashi</i> states that these chambers also had entrances to the Twenty Cubit Space between them and the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> This can be concluded from the above verse, that states: “And he brought me through the entranceway… to the chambers,” implying that Yechezkel was brought through the entranceway leading to the Twenty Cubit Space, and from there to the chambers. Similarly, in sec. 73, it will be demonstrated that there were entrances to the chambers from the entranceway. See the end of that section.
“The upper chambers were narrowed, because the pillars reduced the structure of these, on their lower and middle stories. For they had three stories; they did not have pillars like the pillars of the courtyards. Therefore, the lower and the middle stories had their floor space reduced.”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:5-6. The explanations that follow are quoted, with only slight variations, from <i>Rashi</i>’s commentary to these verses.</i> These chambers are described as, “the upper chambers,” because they are located on the upper portion of Mount Moriah, opposite the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> In contrast, as will be explained in sec. 73, there were other chambers located on the lower portion of the mountain.
The verse emphasizes that some of these chambers were narrowed. <i>Rashi</i> explains that the word יוֹכְלו in that verse should be understood as similar to the word יֹאכַל, “consume,” with the [first] <i>vav</i> replacing the <i>alef</i> [of יֹאכַל]<i>,</i> the intent being that the chambers’ space was reduced. The verse explains the reason: It was הָאַתִּיקִים, “<b>the pillars,</b>” that reduced their space. The word מֵהֵנָה, “<b>of these,</b>” refers to the middle and lower stories. The word בִּנְיָן, “<b>the structure,</b>” is the object of the verb “<b>reduced,</b>” i.e., the pillars reduced the floor space of the structure. The verse also explains why the reduction was made: Because “<b>they had three stories,</b>” and thus, they were high. “<b>They did not have pillars</b>” to support the upper stories “<b>like the pillars of the courtyards,</b>” which projected outward and did not take up any floor space inside. Instead, these pillars took up space both inside and outside. “<b>Therefore, the lower and the middle stories had their floor space reduced.</b>” There were no pillars for the third story, because its roof did not require support. This interpretation was found in the works of a great sage.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The literal translation of <i>Rashi's</i> words is, “a large tree,” borrowing the simile used in <i>Pesachim</i> 112a.</i>
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel offers a different explanation of this passage, stating that the term עליונות refers to the highest story, i.e., the third story. It was narrower than the others.
