Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel interprets the term אַתִּיקִים as “chambers,” for he understands the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:3.</i> אַתִּיק אֶל פְּנֵי אַתִּיק בַּשְּׁלִשִׁים as meaning, “There was one chamber on top of another chamber, three, one on top of each other.” The meaning of the word בשלשים in that verse means that there were three stories of chambers. Therefore, when the narrative states,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>5.</i> “The upper chambers were narrowed,” it is indicating that the highest story was the narrowest of the three. This is the direct opposite of the side-chambers built around the Sanctuary. In that instance, the upper chambers were wider than the lower ones. In this instance, by contrast, the upper ones were narrower than the lower ones. The narrative gives the reason for this, כִּי יוֹכְלוּ אַתִּיקִים מֵהֵנָּה מֵהַתַּחְתֹּנוֹת. Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel interprets this as meaning that the inner space of the lower and middle stories took up more of the structure because it was wider below. The narrative gives a further rationale: “<b>For they had three stories; they did not have pillars.</b>”
The narrative states “of these” and specifies that it is referring to the lower and middle stories. Like <i>Rashi,</i> Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel explains that in the word יוֹכְלוּ, the first <i>vav</i> takes the place of an <i>alef,</i> and the intent is, “to take up space,” as found in the words of our Sages,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Basra</i> 14a.</i> “How much space did the Tablets take up (אוכלות) in the Ark? Six [handbreadths]. Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel continues: <i>Radak</i> states that he is unable to understand the structure of these chambers, how the lower stories will be wider than the upper ones. Similarly, neither <i>Rashi</i> nor <i>[Targum] Yonason</i> explained anything about it. Concerning this, <i>Radak</i> states, the prophet Eliyahu will explain this passage.
It is possible to explain that the upper side-chambers [built around the Sanctuary] mentioned earlier, were wider than the lower ones because the wall that supported them was wide at its bottom and narrow at its top. Therefore, the upper chambers took up more space than the lower ones, because the wall was not as thick at that height as it was below. They were also supported by pillars. Therefore, the upper stories could be as wide as possible. There was no fear that they would fall because they were supported by pillars. These chambers, by contrast, were built in three stories, one on top of the other, and thus the wall on one side was not narrower than on the other side, and they did not have pillars. Therefore, it was necessary that the upper chamber be narrower than the lower [ones] out of fear that the upper stories would fall if they would be as wide as the lower stories. No such fear existed with regard to the lower stories, because they were supported by the earth or were close to it. This is intimated by the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:6.</i> עַל כֵּן נֶאֱצַל מֵהַתַּחְתֹּנוֹת וּמֵהַתִּיכֹנוֹת מֵהָאָרֶץ, which means, “for this reason, the upper stories were made shorter (נֶּאֶצְלוּ) than those below them.” The same root as found in the word נֶאֱצַל is used in the verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bamidbar</i> 11:17.</i> “I will separate (וְאָצַלְתִּי) from the spirit that rests on you…,” and “I did not remove (אָצַלְתִּי) from them….”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Koheles</i> 2:10.</i> Similarly, <i>Targum Yonason</i> renders the verse in <i>Yechezkel,</i> “For this reason, they were constricted.”
There were also chambers on the southern side of the main edifice of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> opposite to and resembling those on the northern side. Their length, their width, their openings, their dimensions, and their entrances are the same as the others’. Thus, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 41:10.</i> “Between the chambers [and the House], there was [a space of] 20 cubits around the House to either side.” By saying, “there was [a space of] 20 cubits around the House to either side,” the verse implies that there were chambers on either side of the House, i.e., on the north and the south, as explained in sec. 70. Similarly, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 43:13.</i> “And he said to me, ‘The chambers on the north and the chambers on the south facing the edifice, they are the holy chambers where the <i>kohanim</i> who draw close to God will eat the sacrifices of the most sacred order… the meal-offerings, the sin-offerings, and the guilt-offerings, because the place is holy.’”
In sections 13 and 14 above, we said that in this section, we would describe the roofless courtyards that are mentioned there. The roofless courtyards were located in interior corners of the courtyard, and not on its exterior corners, as depicted by the composer of the picture of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> printed in Manitoba.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is referring to the Manitoba edition of the <i>Mishnah</i> from 5382 (1622 CE). The illustration accompanies Tractate <i>Middos</i> and was prepared by Rav Moshe Kazis, one of the leading sages of Italian Jewry in that era.</i> This is implied by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 46:21.</i> “And He took me out to the Outer Courtyard and caused me to pass by the four corners of the courtyard. Behold there was an enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A literal translation of the verse would be, “a courtyard in the corner of the courtyard.”</i> in the corner of the courtyard.” This verse was recited after the prophet had proceeded into the Twenty Cubit Space mentioned above. Thus it is explained that he had proceeded into the Outer Courtyard, and found these four smaller courtyards.
This conception is also reflected in the words of <i>Rambam</i> in his text.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 5:7 where <i>Rambam</i> writes: “The Women’s Courtyard was in front of the Temple Courtyard on the east, and was 135 cubits long and 135 cubits wide. It had four chambers, each 40 cubits [by 40 cubits, one] in [each of] its four corners.”</i> Rabbeinu Shemayah also offers a similar interpretation, stating:<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary to <i>Middos</i> 2:5.</i> “The Women’s Courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The Women's Courtyard in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was equivalent to the Outer Courtyard in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i> was…135 cubits by 135 cubits. It was all empty space except for the four corners.”
In that commentary, Rabbeinu Shemayah makes a statement that requires explanation. Rabbeinu Shemayah states that the northwest chamber was not sanctified because those afflicted by <i>tzaraas</i> would immerse there<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Negayim</i> 14:8.</i> on the eighth day of their purification process, when they would come to insert their thumbs into the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> so that the sacrificial blood and oil could be placed on them.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Vayikra,</i> ch. 14; <i>Rambam, Hilchos Mechusrei Kapparah,</i> chs. 4-5, which explain that, as part of the purification process that a person afflicted by <i>tzaraas</i> must undergo, sacrificial blood and oil would be placed on the middle lobe of his right ear, his right thumb, and the large toe of his right foot. The afflicted person is forbidden to enter the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Nevertheless, it is forbidden to take the blood of the guilt-offering out of the Courtyard. Hence, the afflicted person must stand outside the Courtyard at the gate and insert his head, hand, and foot, one at a time for the <i>kohen</i> to place the blood and the oil on these limbs.</i> This immersion was required even though one afflicted by <i>tzaraas</i> had immersed himself on the previous evening, for everyone who entered the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was required to immerse himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yoma</i> 30a. As stated in that passage, this even applies to a person who is deemed ritually pure.</i> Therefore,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the afflicted persons would enter before they immersed themselves.</i> Rabbeinu She­mayah maintains that the Chamber of those Afflicted with <i>Tzaraas</i> was not sanctified.
These statements appear unclear to me. The statement that a person may not enter “the Courtyard” until he immerses himself refers to the Courtyard of the Israelites. Although there are two opinions in Tractate <i>Yoma</i> regarding the rationale obligating this immersion, according to both opinions, one is not required to immerse himself until he enters the Courtyard of the Israelites.
To explain: Ben Zoma derives the obligation to immerse through the following <i>kal vechomer,</i> a comparison of a more lenient ruling to a more stringent one: Just as the <i>Kohen</i> <i>Gadol</i> was required to immerse himself when proceeding from one aspect of sacrificial service to another,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the <i>Kohen Gadol</i> would immerse himself five times on Yom Kippur. (<i>Yoma,</i> 30a<i>.</i>).</i> so too, any person seeking to enter the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> from an ordinary place is obligated to immerse himself. Now, the obligation for the <i>Kohen Gadol</i> to immerse himself only applied in the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, where the sacrificial service took place. So too, the obligation for an ordinary person to immerse himself did not apply until he entered that courtyard.
Rabbi Yehudah offers a different rationale for why a person must immerse himself before entering “the Courtyard.” Rabbi Yehudah explains that this immersion is, “an extension of [the obligation to] immerse,” i.e., it is not a Scriptural obligation, but a Rabbinic safeguard so that, if necessary, a person would recall a long-forgotten impurity and purify himself. Such a safeguard is only necessary in a place where a ritually impure person is forbidden to enter according to Scriptural Law, i.e., from the Courtyard of the Israelites and further inward, as the Talmud states in another source:<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 55b.</i> “Shmuel stated: ‘One is liable for [entering the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in a state of] ritual impurity only in the area that is 187 cubits long and 135 cubits wide,” i.e., the enclosure of the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. If so, the obligation to immerse applies only when one enters this place.
It is not appropriate to say that since there is a Rabbinic decree forbidding a person who is ritually impure because of contact with a human corpse from entering the Women’s Courtyard, our Sages required everyone to immerse before entering this courtyard. Among the explanations for this: Firstly, there is a Talmudic principle:<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shavous</i> 41a.</i> A Rabbinic safeguard<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this immersion, which is only a Rabbinic safeguard.</i> is not instituted to protect a Rabbinic safeguard.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The original Rabbinic safeguard is the prohibition against one who incurred ritual impurity because of contact with a corpse from entering this courtyard.</i> Furthermore, if there was a need to institute such a safeguard, it should have also been instituted for the <i>chayl,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See the end of sec. 12 for a discussion of the <i>chayl.</i></i> the area outside the Women’s Courtyard, for a person who contracted the impurity stemming from a human corpse is forbidden to enter there, as stated in Tractate <i>Keilim.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Keilim</i> 1:8.</i> Even were one to postulate that this chamber was not consecrated so that a person could immerse himself there before entering an area sanctified by Rabbinic decree, he would still be unable to enter this chamber, for he would have to pass through the <i>chayl!</i> Therefore, it would be necessary to say that the path to this chamber from the <i>chayl</i> was also not consecrated according to Rabbinic Law.
Furthermore, according to Rabbeinu Shemayah’s understanding, why does the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 30a.</i> state, “one who enters the Courtyard”? Seemingly, a person who has not immersed himself should also refrain from entering the <i>chayl.</i> In that place as well, this would be desirable so that he might recall any impurity of Rabbinic origin that he contracted, and purify himself from it. One might say that the <i>mishnah</i> does not mention such an immersion because it follows ben Zoma’s reasoning. If so, and ben Zoma’s reasoning is the opinion mentioned by Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi in the <i>mishnah</i> without associating it with his name, that is a sign that it is accepted as <i>halachah.</i> Why then does the <i>halachah</i> follow the other approach?<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rambam, Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim</i> 2:3.</i>
Instead, it is preferable to explain that even Rabbi Yehudah would not maintain that this chamber is not sanctified for the reason posited by ben Zoma. Rather, Rabbi Yehudah also maintains that it is not necessary to immerse unless one is entering a place that is consecrated by Scriptural Law. Therefore, all four of these chambers possess all the sanctity associated with the Women’s Courtyard. Nevertheless, a person who had been afflicted with <i>tzaraas</i> may immerse there as others may, since he already immersed on the previous evening, as Rabbeinu She­mayah himself said.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., Rabbeinu Shemayah himself agrees that a person who had been afflicted by <i>tzaraas</i> should immerse on the preceding night.</i> Perhaps because of Rabbeinu Shemayah’s opinion that this chamber was not sanctified, the illustration of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that accompanied the Manitoba edition of the <i>Mishnah</i> placed these chambers outside the courtyard. This is not correct, as demonstrated above.
Similarly, <i>Tosafos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 30b, <i>s.v. e lo tavil.</i></i> object to diminishing the status of this courtyard, stating: “In Tractate <i>Keilim,</i> it is stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Keilim</i> 1:8.</i> ‘The Women’s Courtyard was holier than it, as evidenced by the fact that a person who had immersed that day may not enter there.’ Now, the Chamber of those Afflicted by <i>Tzaraas</i> was located in the Women’s Courtyard.” The status of this chamber will, however, only be that of the Outer Courtyard. Even though this chamber will be positioned opposite the Sanctuary in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it will not be consecrated with the holiness of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> itself. This is reflected in its name, “the Outer Courtyard.”
There remains a matter that requires analysis: Since all the space included in the Outer Courtyard will not be consecrated with the holiness of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> itself, how will the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> contain a consecrated space measuring 187 cubits by 135 cubits, the area of the consecrated space in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Thus there will be a portion of the Courtyard of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> that will, on the surface, lack the holiness it had in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This presents a difficulty, as <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> proceeds to explain.</i> True, the Outer Courtyard’s length will exceed 187 cubits, for the Inner Courtyard will be 100 cubits long and the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> itself will be 100 cubits long. However, the Outer Courtyard’s width will, in some places, be less than 135 cubits.
There is no difficulty regarding the area opposite the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> on either side. As explicitly stated,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:3.</i> there were 20 cubits between the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and the chambers on the complex’s north and the south. The chambers themselves were 50 cubits wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:8.</i> Their entire area was consecrated, and the <i>kohanim</i> would eat the sacrifices of the highest order of holiness inside them.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:13.</i> Thus, the accumulated width of the consecrated area was divided as follows: 100 cubits for the holy chambers, evenly distributed with 50 cubits on each side; 40 cubits between the chambers and the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, evenly distributed with 20 cubits on each side; and the width of the Sanctuary and its side-chambers, which measured a total of 70 cubits. This adds up to a total of 210 cubits. Thus it is wider than the 135 cubits of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Nevertheless, the Inner Courtyard, which comprised the space in front of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> only measured 100 cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:23.</i>
If so, these concepts appear to support the approach of <i>Ra’avad,</i> who objects to <i>Rambam’s</i> ruling that the holiness of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and Jerusalem remain in the present age and indeed, will continue in the Ultimate Future.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchos Beis HaBechirah</i> 6:14.</i> <i>Ra’avad</i> continues: Furthermore, I maintain that even the statement of Rabbi Yossi, “The second consecration<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ra'avad</i> is referring to the consecration at the time of Ezra, when the Jews returned from Babylon.</i> sanctified it for the Ultimate Future,” applies only to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> as a whole, but not to Jerusalem and the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. For Ezra knew that [the dimensions of] the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and Jerusalem would change in the Ultimate Future, and [would] be sanctified again for all time…. This was revealed to me [as] the secrets of God are revealed to those who fear Him.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tehillim</i> 25:14.</i> Therefore, one who enters the site of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> at present is not liable for <i>kerais.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It must be emphasized that although <i>Ra'avad’s</i> opinion is respected, it is not accepted as <i>halachah.</i> Hence, no leniency may be taken to ascend the Mount of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> in our present state of ritual impurity. Indeed, even <i>Ra'avad</i> did not say that it is permitted to ascend the Mount, only that one is not liable for <i>kereis</i> for doing so. <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is stating that the fact that the Inner Courtyard will not encompass the full 135 cubits of width of the Courtyard in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> appears to support <i>Ra'avad’s</i> assertion that the boundaries of the sacred portions of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be redefined in the Ultimate Future.</i>
It appears that the difficulty with <i>Rambam’s</i> approach can be resolved as follows: In the Ultimate Future, the cells located near the Southern Gate of the Inner Courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See sections 19-21 for a description of the cells and the structures near them.</i> will be consecrated with the holiness of the Inner Courtyard. When the 6 cubits of the thickness of the wall of the courtyard, the 6 cubits of the cell, and the 5 cubits of the cell on both the northern and southern sides of the courtyard are added together, a total of 34 cubits will be reached.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., on each side of the Courtyard, there was 6+6+5 cubits, a total of 17. When the total of the two sides are added, the sum of 34 is reached.</i> When we add these 34 cubits to the 100 cubits occupied by the structure of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> there is only one cubit lacking from the 135 mentioned above. This one cubit distance will be made up – and indeed, exceeded – in the following manner: The <i>halachah</i> follows Rabbi Meir, who states:<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Keilim</i> 17:10.</i> “All of the cubits [mentioned in the measurements] of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> were [cubits of] average size.”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is understanding that <i>mishnah</i> as implying that these cubits were 5 handbreadths in measure. Others (Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, <i>et al.</i> to <i>Keilim, loc. cit.</i>) interpret those cubits as also equaling 6 handbreadths.</i> In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, by contrast, the measurements will be made with the angel’s measuring rod, and will thus be oversized cubits, a handbreadth larger than the average-sized cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See the Introduction at the beginning of the text, which explains that ordinary cubits equal 5 handbreadths and these oversized cubits will equal 6 handbreadths.</i> Thus, there will be 134 extra handbreadths for the 134 cubits in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This equals 26 ordinary cubits and 4 handbreadths.
Indeed, according to this reckoning, it would not have been necessary to include the entire area of the cells and their walls in the holiness of the Inner Courtyard. To explain: The Inner Courtyard was itself 100 cubits. In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> this area will be measured with oversized cubits. Thus, the Inner Courtyard will measure 600 handbreadths. When that figure is calculated in ordinary 5-handbreadth cubits, it equals 120 cubits. Thus only 15 cubits are missing from the 135 cubits of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. When they are divided in half, that measure will extend only a cubit and a half into the cells.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> deducted the width of the cell walls, which was 6 cubits each.</i> Nevertheless, there is an advantage to the previous explanation, because it provides a clear line of demarcation for the end of the consecrated portion. Therefore, we did not desire to exclude even the walls of the cells. To clarify the reason for that: At the end of the cells on the western flank of the gate, there is a four-and-a-half cubit length of wall without cells, as we explained in sec. 21. This area is included in 187 cubits of the courtyard’s length. Thus, there is another cubit and a half that the consecrated portion of the Courtyard encompassed. Without including the cells, there would be no clear demarcation for the end of the consecrated portion. Therefore, we stated that the consecrated portion would also include the walls of the cells. Thus, the consecrated area has a clearly marked border, i.e. the outer edge of the walls of the cells. Even though we have added a large portion, there is no difficulty. Indeed, additions have been made in the length and in the width of the area opposite the Sanctuary. This is possible because it is permitted to add to the dimensions of the courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">However, it is not permitted to subtract from the courtyard's dimensions.</i> as stated in Tractate <i>Sanhedrin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2a.</i> and Tractate <i>Shevuos.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shevuos</i> 14a.</i>
Nevertheless, Rabbi Yehudah differs with Rabbi Meir in Tractate <i>Keilim,</i> and maintains that even in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> all of the cubits were equal to six handbreadths. Thus according to Rabbi Yehudah, the lack of a cubit in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> presents a question. It is possible to say that Rabbi Yehudah follows the perspective of Rabbi Eliezer,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shevuos</i> 16a.</i> who maintains that when Ezra consecrated the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> he did not intend to include the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Alternatively, it is possible to say that there will be marking posts in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> compound to make a distinction, as the <i>Mishnah</i> teaches in Tractate <i>Middos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 1:6.</i> with regard to the Chamber of the Hearth: “There were marking posts that separated the consecrated [chambers] from those which were not consecrated.” Similarly, later in that tractate,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 4:5.</i> it mentions that there were marking posts in the second story of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to differentiate between the portion above the Sanctuary and that above the Holy of Holies. And the Sage who differs with Rabbi Eliezer ben Yaakov states<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 2:6.</i> that there were marking posts in the Courtyard of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to distinguish between the Courtyard of the <i>Kohanim</i> and the Courtyard of the Israelites.
According to this logic, it is possible to say that even according to Rabbi Meir it is not necessary to add further areas to the consecrated portion of the Courtyard, and there would be marking posts in the cells and outside, in the area where there were no cells. Nevertheless, the first explanation of Rabbi Meir’s approach appears preferable.

Chapter 72

“In front of the chambers, there was a passageway ten cubits wide leading to the inner area<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is referring to the Twenty Cubit Space between the building of the Sanctuary and the chambers mentioned in sec. 70.</i> via a space of one cubit.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:4.</i> To explain: it was impossible to enter the Twenty Cubit Space between the chambers and the cells, via the Inner Courtyard or via the Outer Courtyard. The Inner Courtyard measured 100 cubits by 100 cubits in front of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> to the east.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:47.</i> Now, it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 21:14.</i> “The width of the House to the east was 100 cubits.” Thus, the width of the building matches the width of the Courtyard. The 5 cubits of the Twenty Cubit Space which extended beyond the fifteen cubits of <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> on either side were closed off by the width of the wall – 6 cubits wide – that separated between the Outer Courtyard and the Inner Courtyard when coming from the east to the west. If so, there would not be an entrance to that Twenty Cubit Space unless there was a space for a slanted<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Here, the term מְשַׁפֵּעַ, translated as “slanted,” implies not only a slanted at an angle, but also inclined upward because the Twenty Cubit Space was on a higher portion of the Temple Mount.</i> passageway that entered the wall. This slanted passageway would be located where the wall between the Inner and the Outer Courtyard ended. The passageway would be located in the thickness of the wall, and would be a cubit wide, of sufficient width to enable a person to enter.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Sukkah</i> 7b states, an ordinary person is a cubit wide.</i> One would enter the 5-cubit space between the wall of <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> and the chambers, and proceed westward 10 cubits within that space. After 10 cubits, <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> ended. This is the meaning of the phrase, “a passageway 10 cubits wide.” From that point on, there was a twenty-cubit space between the chambers and the side-chambers adjacent to the Sanctuary. Why was this passageway described as 10 cubits wide? Seemingly, it would have been more proper to say, “ten cubits long.” Nevertheless, the term “wide” was used because the passageway ran from east to west, adjacent to <i>Beis HaChalifos. Beis HaChalifos</i> measured 15 cubits from north to south. Since the distance from north to south was longer than the distance from east to west, the 10 cubits that <i>Beis HaChalifos</i> ran from east to west is described as its width. Similarly, the distance 15-cubit distance from north to south is described as the length because it is a larger distance. This is <i>Rashi’s</i> understanding of the verse.
True, it is possible to access the Twenty Cubit Space from the area behind the Holy of Holies. Nevertheless, it was necessary that there be access from the eastern portion of the courtyard, for the overwhelming majority of use of the courtyard was from this direction. Moreover, according to our explanation in sec. 12, there is no gate through which to enter the courtyard from the west. Therefore, someone wanting to enter the Twenty Cubit Space from the courtyard would have to enter in the east, circle 50 cubits to the side, proceed 100 cubits for the length of the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, and then 50 cubits past the chamber until he could enter the Twenty Cubit Space. Therefore, it was necessary that there be a passageway from the east.
For this reason, the entrances from the chambers to the courtyard and to the Twenty Cubit Space described in the previous section are not sufficient. For even were one to use these entrances, he would have to make a circuit, walking the chamber’s width to the side, then, half its length, and then traverse the width from entrance to entrance. Moreover, it is not appropriate that these chambers serve as shortcuts providing access to the Twenty Cubit Space. For these chambers should be closed rooms, that are entered only at specific times. In contrast, the Twenty Cubit Space is open space like the remainder of the courtyard. Hence, it should be open like the courtyard allowing one to enter it at all times, in the same manner as he enters the entire courtyard.

Chapter 73

Lower [on the mountain] than these chambers, as one comes from the east, approaching from the Outer Courtyard, along the width of the eastern wall of the courtyard, facing the edifice and the building, there were chambers. There was a passageway before them whose appearance was like the appearance of the chambers on the north, resembling their length, their width, all their exits, their dimensions, and their entrances resembling the entrances of the chambers to the south. There was an entrance at the beginning of the passageway, the passageway before the platform of the musicians, on the east, as one approaches.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:9-12.</i>
<i>Rashi</i> comments on these verses: “<b>Lower [on the mountain] than these chambers</b>” – I do not know whether [the word mitachas] comes to teach that these chambers had underground passageways below them, or if it comes to teach that there were chambers on the eastern side of the courtyard. Since the mountain would slope downward on the eastern side, the verse describes them as “<b>lower than these chambers</b>,” i.e., the aforementioned chambers were on top of the mountain and these [chambers] were on its lower portion. It is as if the verse states, <b>“Lower [on the mountain]…, as one comes from east, approaching from the Outer Courtyard. Along the width… of the courtyard</b>” – [The verse is referring to] one who comes from the eastern portion of the Outer Courtyard, proceeding to the north via the northeast corner of the wall of the Inner Chamber on the outside. As he approaches these chambers on the north, he discovers along the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard, which is to the west of the Outer Courtyard, facing “<b>the edifice and the building,</b>” “<b>chambers</b>” that are next to that wall and located in the Outer Courtyard.
“<b>There was a passageway before them</b>,” – i.e., before these chambers, there was a passageway with a width of 50 cubits that resembled the passageway of the chambers on the north[west] described previously
“<b>Resembling their length</b>,” – i.e., the length of these chambers was the same as those on the north[west], and so too, “<b>their width.</b>” [Similarly,] “<b>their dimensions, and their entrances</b>,” – were like those of the northern chambers. “<b>Resembling the entrances to the chambers to the south</b>” – were the entrances to the chambers on the east. For in the south[west] as well, there were chambers… near the side-chambers, as explained above.
“<b>There was an entrance at the beginning of the passageway</b>” – The chambers on the east had an entrance at the beginning of the passageway. This passageway was located before הַגְּדֶרֶת הֲגִינָה, translated by <i>Targum Yonason</i> as “the platform of the Levites.”
The phrase, “along the width of the eastern wall of the courtyard,” i.e., next to the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard, seems to imply that the length of these chambers was built along the wall from north to south, and that their width ran from east to west. In actuality, this was not so. It is impossible that the structure was built in such a manner because there were cells with walls projecting 11 cubits into the Outer Courtyard from the eastern wall. If so, this courtyard will not have 100 cubits of open space, with the width of the chambers being 50 cubits, and with 50 cubits of open space in front of the chambers, like the chambers described previously. Also, it is difficult to understand via which path one would approach the gate of the courtyard. Surely, he would not approach the gate by passing through the chambers. Also, as explained in sec. 20, one of the attractive features of the gate to the Inner Courtyard was that the vestibule would project outward. Were these chambers to be constructed along the walls, the vestibule could not possibly be built in that manner.
Nor is it possible to explain that the chambers’ length ran from east to west and their width ran along the wall of the courtyard from north to south, because as mentioned above, there were cells located there. These cells will not leave space for the 100 cubits of the chambers’ length. There is another reason why the chambers could not be constructed in this manner, which we will give presently.
Similarly, it does not appear that the chambers are located at the corners of the Inner Courtyard, one at the northeast corner and one at the southeast corner, with their length running from west to east like those described above. For if so, it would not be possible to walk from corner to corner in the Outer Courtyard unless one entered these chambers, for these chambers would take up the entire length of the east side of the Outer Courtyard, that was situated in front of the Inner Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The length of the Outer Courtyard was 100 cubits and the length of these chambers was 100 cubits, so the chambers would occupy the entire length of the Outer Courtyard.</i> Furthermore, it is not logical to say that one who walks from one corner of the Outer Courtyard to the opposite corner would have to pass through the chambers, as explained in the previous section with regard to passing through the upper chambers in order to reach the Twenty Cubit Space.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In that section, it was explained that the chambers were intended to be closed rooms, entered only at specific times.</i>
Therefore, it appears that these chambers begin where the cells at the eastern flank of the northern gate to the Inner Courtyard end, and where the cells at the eastern flank of the southern gate end.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As explained previously, on the northern and southern sides of the Inner Courtyard, there were 11 cubits between the end of the cells and the end of the wall to the Inner Courtyard. These chambers would begin at this point.</i> The chambers run from east to west, similar to those on the higher portion of the mountain. Thus, there will be a way to proceed from corner to corner in the Outer Courtyard without passing through the chambers. For the space gained from the chambers being positioned to the west, paralleling the end of the Inner Courtyard, and the thickness of its walls, will create ample space between the end of the chambers and the wall of the Outer Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 74 where this calculation is explained.</i> For the length of the Outer Courtyard to the east is the same as that of the chambers, and the chambers begin some distance before the beginning of the courtyard. In this conception, there is also an open space measuring 50 cubits in front of the chambers, as is true of the upper chambers. The chambers are built at the corner of the eastern wall. Thus, one is on the northeast corner of the Inner Courtyard. Accordingly, the verse mentions coming from the east and turning to the north. Nevertheless, these chambers do not begin exactly at the corner of the courtyard, but rather, are set off slightly from the courtyard’s corner.
It was explained<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See sec. 71.</i> that the upper chambers were actually 51 cubits wide – i.e., an extra cubit was added to the width stated in the verse. Such an addition can also be made with regard to these chambers, by including one cubit of the wall of the thickness of the wall of the Inner Courtyard. Thus, at the end of the wall and the chambers, one would be facing the Outer Courtyard. The chambers in the north jut out a cubit southward into the courtyard, and the chambers in south jut out a cubit northward into the courtyard. Thus, the chambers are actually 51 cubits wide. There is a courtyard in front of the chambers measuring 50 cubits wide, i.e., there are 50 cubits between the chambers on the northern side and the northern wall, and 50 cubits between the chambers on the southern side and the southern wall. Thus, these chambers resemble the chambers positioned higher on the mountain.
On this basis, we can understand <i>Rashi’s</i> explanation of the phrase, “facing the edifice and the building.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:10.</i> As explained, these chambers are built alongside the wall of the Inner Courtyard, and do not begin at the corner of the courtyard. Instead, the chambers are positioned slightly northward. As stated in sec. 21, there are vestibules built there, on the inside, alongside that wall. Thus, the chambers are built opposite these vestibules. If, however, the chambers began at the corners or before the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard as was supposed, even though vestibules are also situated there, as explained in that section, the chambers themselves would not be facing the vestibules, only the end of their walls.
Following the conception above, however, a portion of the chambers is opposite “the building,” i.e., the vestibules, like the upper chambers that are built facing the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Alternatively, the term “building” can refer to a building in the inner chamber, i.e., the cells, for the chambers begin where the cells end. As we will explain in sec. 74, half a cubit of the wall of the outer cell enters the space of these chambers. Thus, the chambers can be described as being opposite the cells, because part of the cells’ area overlaps with the space designated for the chambers. All of this applies according to <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary.
Nevertheless, according to my humble understanding, it appears that the phrase, “facing the edifice and the building,” is intended to highlight the concept stated above: that these chambers are positioned westward, behind the eastern wall. Their length of 100 cubits does not run entirely parallel to the 100 cubit length of the courtyard in front of the eastern wall. True, they are introduced with the phrase, “as one comes from east.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:9.</i> Nevertheless, they do not begin at the easternmost point of that courtyard, occupying the entire length of that courtyard, but rather are set back a bit. Thus, they are positioned toward “the edifice,” i.e., the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> and “the structure,” i.e. the side-chambers that surround that building, as <i>Rashi</i> explains in his interpretation of the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid</i>. 41:12.</i> “the building that was on the outer side of the edifice,” as cited in sec. 64. Thus, the intent of the phrase here is that these chambers are positioned westward, toward the outer side of the edifice.
This interpretation of the phrase, “the edifice and the building,” is preferable to <i>Rashi’s</i> because elsewhere, that phrase is used only to refer to the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and the cells. Also, the term “edifice” is not appropriate for the structures in the courtyard. Furthermore, those structures should be described with one term, either “edifice” or “building,” but not with both terms, because there was only one type of structure in the courtyard.
The above also enables one to understand the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:12.</i> “There was an entrance at the beginning of the pathway, the pathway before the platform of the musicians.” <i>Rashi</i> interprets this phrase as meaning that these chambers had an entrance at the beginning of the pathway before the <i>Duchan,</i> the musicians’ platform. The place where the chambers begin is at the place where the cells end. This place is parallel to a point 4½ cubits from the beginning of the Inner Courtyard, as will be explained in sec. 74. The entrance leading from the chamber to the Inner Courtyard opens up at, “the pathway before the platform of the musicians,” i.e., the <i>Duchan.</i> The entire area through which the Israelites were allowed to enter leads to the <i>Duchan,</i> which is positioned beyond this area. As explained in sec. 26, there were 17 cubits from the entrance to the Inner Courtyard until the <i>Duchan.</i> Thus, the entrance that was within the first 4½ cubits was, “at the beginning of the pathway” leading to the <i>Duchan.</i>
Nevertheless, there is a difficulty regarding <i>Rashi’s</i> interpretation of the phrase: “<b>Resembling the entrances to the chambers to the south</b>” – [These] were the entrances to the chambers on the east. For in the south[west] as well, there were chambers… near the side-chambers, as explained above.
Why did Scripture describe the entrance to these chambers by comparing them to the chambers on south[west], which, unlike those in the north[west], were not discussed previously in the narrative? Indeed, in sec. 71, it was necessary to bring proofs that there were such chambers in the south[west].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See pp. 235-236.</i> Moreover, even when describing these lower chambers, the narrative refers to the northern chambers, stating,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 42:9.</i> “Lower [on the mountain] than these chambers.” The southern chambers were not mentioned or even alluded to until this verse. Only in the following verse<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:13.</i> does the narrative mention these chambers, speaking of “the northern chambers and the southern chambers.” Moreover, the fact that there is a courtyard 50 cubits long in front of this chamber is derived from the fact that there was such a space in the north, as the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 42:11.</i> “There was a passageway before them whose appearance was resembled that of the chambers on the north.” Why, then, weren’t the entrances also associated with those of the northern chambers?
Therefore, it appears that the verse should be understood according to <i>Targum Yonason’s</i> interpretation:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid,</i> 42:12.</i> “<b>Resembling the gates of the chambers that opened to the pathway on the south.</b>” The words “<b>on the south</b>” do not refer to the chambers but to the entrances. The intent is that the chamber’s entrances open to the south. Therefore, the chamber to which reference is being made is the northern chamber. It has entrances leading to the Twenty Cubit Space between it and the cells which are to its south, as explained in sec. 71. Similarly, the verse is teaching that the lower chambers also opened to the south. In that direction, they had “<b>an entrance at the beginning of the pathway</b>,” leading to the Inner Courtyard. Therefore, the previous mention of the entrances in the narrative is not sufficient, for the phrase in this verse is teaching that the chamber had an additional entrance at the beginning of the pathway. Thus, the verse is teaching that just as the upper northern chambers had an entrance to the south leading to the Twenty Cubit Space, as we explained, so too, the lower chambers had an entrance to the south. In addition, the lower chambers had another entrance “<b>at the beginning of the pathway</b>.”
The narrative begins by stating “Resembling the gates of the chambers that opened to the pathway on the south.” These chambers also have an opening to that direction. Moreover, they have another entrance “at the beginning of the pathway.” Alternatively, perhaps this is the only entrance these chambers had. Since this entrance also opened to the south, it could be the entrance referred to by the verse’s first clause. It is, however, more likely that there were two entrances, both opening to the south - one leading to the Inner Courtyard and one leading to the Outer Courtyard.
It is possible to explain <i>Rashi’s</i> reasoning as follows: The entrance of the upper northern chamber to the south opened to the Twenty Cubit Space. Since the opening of the lower northern chamber<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This explanation follows the conception that there were two openings to the lower chamber.</i> led to a far more spacious area, it is not comparable to the upper northern chamber. Therefore, <i>Rashi</i> compared it to the southern chambers, for their opening on the south also led to a more spacious area – in contrast to their opening to the north which led to the Twenty Cubit Space. Nevertheless, the interpretation of <i>Targum Yonason</i> seems more accurate.

Chapter 74

In sec. 71, we stated that the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will also have empty space behind the Holy of Holies. Tractate <i>Middos</i> states,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 5:1.</i> “There were 11 cubits behind the Holy of Holies.” It is possible to say that these 11 cubits correspond to the 11 cubits of the Courtyard of the Israelites, since according to <i>Rambam,</i> Israelites were also permitted to proceed behind the Holy of Holies.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The source in <i>Rambam’s</i> work has not been located. See <i>Yoma</i> 21a.</i> Following that logic, one can postulate that in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, there will be 17 cubits of empty space behind the Holy of Holies. This will correspond to the 17 cubits of space where Israelites will be allowed to proceed in the future structure’s Courtyard, as explained in sec. 26. It is true that the area behind the Holy of Holies in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be much longer than the corresponding area in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in the second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the courtyard was 135 cubits wide, while in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it will be 312 cubits wide.</i> The rationale is because the area behind the future structure’s Holy of Holies will extend across the entire length of the Outer Courtyard. Nevertheless, that is not problematic, because the part of this area that is on the northern and southern sides of the Outer Courtyard will be set off, and is thus not included when calculating the area where Israelites will be able to walk.
Even so, it seems that the dimensions of the past and the future complexes will be the same and, in the Ultimate Future, there will only be an 11 cubit space behind the Holy of Holies. The rationale for this statement is that in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, the area behind the Holy of Holies is part of the Inner Courtyard which encircled the building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> on its north and south, and behind it. Therefore, it is appropriate that the area behind the Holy of Holies where the Israelites could walk be equivalent to the area where they could walk at the beginning of this courtyard. In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> by contrast, as can be seen in the diagram that we drew,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A copy of that diagram is included as a foldout at the end of this volume.</i> the area behind the Holy of Holies will be part of the Outer Courtyard. It is not crucial whether or not the area behind the Holy of Holies will be sanctified with the holiness of the Courtyard, for the Twenty-Cubit Space and the chambers will be sanctified.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, providing an ample amount of space for the functions necessary to be performed there.</i> Thus, even without consecrating the area behind the Holy of Holies, there will be more than the 187 cubits of consecrated space that existed in the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> as explained in sec. 71. Thus, it can be concluded that the area behind the Holy of Holies is part of the outer portion of the Courtyard. If so, it is fitting that there be only 11 cubits behind the Holy of Holies to correspond to the 11 cubits that existed in the former structure. These 11 cubits will parallel the 11 cubits left at the end of the lower chambers in the eastern portion of the Outer Courtyard, as explained at the beginning of the previous section.
As stated previously, the Outer Courtyard in front of the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard was 100 cubits long. The lower chambers were also 100 cubits long. They were set back along the northern and southern walls of the Inner Courtyard, until the end of the cells on the flanks of the gates. After the end of the cells, there is 4 ½ cubits of empty space before the end of the wall. That figure is derived as follows: As explained in sec. 21, the cells on either side of the gate and the gate, take up 91 cubits. The Courtyard measured 100 cubits by 100 cubits. Thus there are 9 cubits of empty space along the walls. Since the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was built symmetrically, the 9 cubits half should be divided in half, leaving 4 ½ cubits on the west and the same amount on the east. As mentioned previously, the Inner Courtyard’s wall was 6 cubits thick. If we add these 6 cubits to the 4 1/2 cubits of extra space, the chambers will be built 10 ½ cubits west of the beginning of the Outer Courtyard. Thus, there will be 10 ½ cubits of empty space between the end of the chambers and the wall of the Outer Courtyard.
Since the builders of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> left 11 cubits of open space behind the Holy of Holies, it is appropriate to say that an equivalent space will be left there in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. Accordingly, there should also be an equivalent space left at the beginning of the Outer Courtyard. That measure can be achieved by having half a cubit of the last cell’s wall enter the wall of the chamber. Thus the chamber will be set back 11 cubits from the wall of the Inner Courtyard and there will be an equal amount of space left open at the beginning of the Outer Courtyard. Thus the beginning and end of the Outer Courtyard of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be symmetric. In the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, it was the beginning and end of the Inner Courtyard that resembled each other. In the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, this pattern will be followed in the Outer Courtyard.
Based on the above, it is possible to explain the calculation made in sec. 1, that the length of the Outer Courtyard from east to west is 317 cubits. Thus the end of my description of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will focus on the same subject as its beginning. To follow is the reckoning of that measure: There are 100 cubits in front of the beginning of the eastern wall of the Inner Courtyard, as explained in sec. 22. That wall is 6 cubits thick, as explained in sec. 23. The Inner Courtyard itself is 100 cubits 1ong, as stated in sec. 36. The building of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> was 100 cubits long, as stated in sec. 64. And then, there were these 11 cubits. When these dimensions are added together, a total of 317 cubits is reached.
In His great kindness and goodness until now, God has helped me explain the layout of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, its chambers, its structures, its cells, its exits, and its entrances. Everything is thoroughly explained, inspired by the generous hand of God.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>I Divrei HaYamim</i> 28:19.</i>
We are, however, obligated to complete our explanation of the area known as the Great <i>Terumah,</i> and discuss how its sides exceed the Temple Mount that is located within it, as we began explaining in our introductory words.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the Preface.</i> We hope to present an illustration of the Great <i>Terumah</i> at the conclusion of our description, along with numbers keyed to these explanations. The value of such a drawing is self-understood, and has been explained in the preface.
Thus, after we have keyed the portions of the drawing of the House until the number 74, we will begin keying the description of the Temple Mount from the number 75, and continue in ascending order until the description is completed.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">That drawing is found on page 274</i>

Chapter 75

“This is the instruction regarding the House: On top of the mountain, its entire border all around shall be most holy. This is the instruction regarding the House.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 43:12.</i> “It was 500 [measuring rods]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This verse does not mention measuring rods explicitly. Nevertheless, from <i>ibid.</i> 42:16-20, it is obvious that this is the intent here.</i> by 500 [measuring rods], a square all around.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 45:2.</i> We already discussed the circumference of the Temple Mount and its wall in the preface.
Tractate <i>Middos</i> teaches:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Middos</i> 1:3.</i> There were five gates to the Temple Mount: To the south were the two Chuldah Gates; they were used to enter and to depart. To the west was the Kaiphonus Gate; it was used to enter and to depart. The Tadi Gate was to the south; it was not used for any purpose. On the Eastern Gate was the emblem of the capital Shushan. Through it, the <i>kohen</i> who would burn the red heifer, the red heifer, and all those who help in its burning depart to the Mount of Olives.

Chapter 76

“There were 50 cubits of open space around it.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel,</i> 43:12<i>.</i> I.e., there were 50 cubits of empty space around the enclosure of the Temple Mount. The gloss of <i>Metzudos Tziyon</i> emphasizes that this land should not be planted with crops or trees.</i>

Chapter 77

The remainder of the sanctified area was the strip in the northern part of the Great <i>Terumah</i>. This strip was 25,000 measuring rods long and 10,000 measuring rods wide,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 45:1.</i> and was sanctified as most holy, as we mentioned in the preface to this work. The Temple Mount was located in the center of this portion. The narrative goes on to state that the portion around the Temple Mount will be 12,250 measuring rods to the east, and that same measure to the west. The length of the Temple Mount will be 500 measuring rods. Thus, the entire area will be 25,000 measuring rods long. The area to the north will be 4,750 measuring rods wide, and there will be the same measure to the south. The Temple Mount complex will be 500 measuring rods wide, and will be located in the center of this area. Thus, the area will be 10,000 measuring rods wide. This is <i>Rashi’s</i> calculation.
Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel writes, “In addition to all the above, there will be the 50 cubits of the empty space around the city.” This does not appear accurate, for if it were so, the three strips of land to be separated from the Great <i>Terumah</i> will not be the same size. Instead, the strip of land designated for the <i>kohanim,</i> in which the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> is located, will be 50 cubits longer on each of its four sides because of the extra empty space around the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. This is problematic because Scripture always describes the three strips as being equal, i.e., the strip for the <i>kohanim</i> and the Levites are of the same width and length,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 48:13.</i> and the strip of the city has the same length as the other two.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 48:10.</i> Similarly, the narrative later combines all three strips, and describes them as a square when discussing, “the entire <i>Terumah</i>,” as will be explained in sec. 83.
The reason <i>Rashi</i> did not subtract these 50 cubits of open space is because they were measured in cubits as stated in the narrative and not with the measuring rod. Indeed, they do not add up to an even number of rods, their total being approximately 8 ½ measuring rods. Since they do not reach a sum of 10 measuring rods, the narrative did not desire to subtract less than ten rods from the reckoning. This understanding is reflected in the commentary of <i>Radak</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Radak</i> to <i>ibid</i>. 45:4.</i> who writes that the 50 cubits of empty space are included in the area that remains outside the Temple Mount, as we explained in the preface. The remaining area was given to the <i>kohanim</i>, the servants of the Sanctuary, who draw close to serve God, providing them with a place for homes.

Chapter 78

<b>SECTIONS 78–79</b>
“Opposite the border of the <i>kohanim,</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:13.</i> to the south, was the portion granted to the Levites. Their portion was between the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> and the city. The city will be to the south, as the verse states,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 40:2.</i> “Near it, there were building[s] resembling a city.”
“[An area] 25,000 [measuring rods] long and 10,000 wide was granted to the Levites, the servants of the House, as a heritage; it had twenty chambers.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 45:5.</i> <i>Radak</i> interprets the words, “it had twenty chambers,” as meaning that the Levites should make twenty chambers in their portion close to the portion of the <i>kohanim.</i> According to <i>Radak,</i> these chambers would serve as dwellings for the gatekeepers of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, so that they will be close to the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. The Levites will use remainder of the area for their needs and private homes. Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel and <i>Rashi</i> also interpret the verse in that manner.

Chapter 79



Chapter 80

“The width of 5000 [measuring rods] that remains over a length of 25,000 [measuring rods shall be… for the city].”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 48:15.</i> The Great <i>Terumah</i> was 25,000 measuring rods long and 25,000 measuring rods wide. From it, on its northern side, two strips, each 10,000 measuring rods wide (from north to south) and 25,000 measuring rods long (from east to west), were given to the <i>kohanim</i> and the Levites respectively. Thus, there remained a strip 5000 measuring rods wide on the southern portion of the Great <i>Terumah</i>. The verse states that this portion “will be ordinary,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., not consecrated.</i> for a city,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is referring to Jerusalem of the Future. Although the site of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will not change, the location and dimensions of Jerusalem of the Future will not correspond exactly to those of present-day Jerusalem.</i> for dwellings, and for open space.”
The meaning of the phrases, “for a city” and, “for open space” should be understood simply. The phrase, “for dwellings” refers to the dwellings of the city’s workers. The narrative proceeds to explain the size of each of these three areas. Since all three are necessary for the city’s existence, all three are included in the collective term “city,” as in a previous verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 45:6.</i> “For the heritage of the city set aside [an area], 5,000 measuring rods wide and 25,000 measuring rods long, facing the sacred portion. It will be for the entire House of Israel.”
It is stated, “the city shall be in its midst,” i.e., in the center of this strip. “These are its measures: the northern side, 4500 [measuring rods], the southern side, 4500 [measuring rods], the eastern side, 4500 [measuring rods], and the western side, 4500 [measuring rods].”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 48:16.</i> “The perimeter [of the city] will be 18,000 measuring rods. And the name of the city from that day will be ‘God is there.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 48:35.</i>
Explanation is seemingly required regarding the following point: Scripture specifies the city’s dimensions on all four sides, and also mentions the sum of its perimeter. We do not find this type of redundancy for the other measurements mentioned in the narrative. Similarly, there is no such redundancy for the dimensions of the Great <i>Terumah,</i> nor for any of its sections, nor for the Temple Mount, nor for any other areas of the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> To offer a resolution: The text is informing the inhabitants of the exile that the city’s perimeter will be 18,000 measuring rods, which is equivalent to the number of worlds through which the Holy One, blessed be He, glides at night. As stated in Tractate <i>Avodah Zarah:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 3b.</i> “What does He do by night?... It may be said that He rides a light cherub, and glides in 18,000 worlds, as intimated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tehillim</i> 68:18.</i> ‘The chariot of God is twice (שִׁנְאָן) 10,000 times 2,000 angels.’ Do not read <i>shin’an,</i> ‘twice,’ [rather] read [it] <i>she’einan,</i> ‘that are not’ [i.e., twice 10,000 minus 2,000].” Thus, by mentioning the dimension 18,000, the verse is alluding to these worlds.
This enables us to understand the deeper meaning of the statement,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is the concluding phrase of <i>Yechezkel.</i></i> “And the name of the city from that day will be ‘God is there.’ ” The words, “from that day,” imply, “after the day has passed.” Thus, the verse should be understood as, “after the day has passed, God is there.” The word “day” refers to the era of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, for, as mentioned in the introduction,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">P. 10.</i> the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> resembles the world and its three divisions. This is where God is focused during the day, as our Sages explain, Even when the day has passed, God remains there. This is how this verse should be understood, in my humble opinion.
In the <i>Aggadah</i> in Tractate <i>Sanhedrin,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sanhedrin</i> 97b.</i> this concluding verse is interpreted as referring to the rows of righteous men surrounding the Divine presence, as Ravva states, “[The rows of the righteous] who abide before the Holy One, blessed be He, equal 18,000 <i>parsaos</i>, as intimated by the verse, ‘The perimeter… will be 18,000.’ ” <i>Rashi</i> comments on that passage: “The verse at the end of the Book of <i>Yechezkel</i> is referring to Jerusalem in the spiritual realms.”
It must be emphasized that when <i>Rashi</i> states that the verse refers to Jerusalem in the spiritual realms, he is not implying that the verse does not refer to the earthly city of Jerusalem. Heaven forbid that <i>Rashi’s</i> holy mouth would make such a statement, for a verse should never be taken entirely beyond its simple meaning.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Shabbos</i> 63a.</i> Instead, certainly, Yechezkel’s prophecy must be understood according to its simple meaning. Indeed, <i>Rashi</i> comments on the above verse in <i>Yechezkel</i>: “<b>The perimeter of the city will be 18,000 measuring rods.</b>This total can be reached by adding the lengths of its sides.” Thus, <i>Rashi</i> interprets the verse as referring to the earthly city of Jerusalem in accordance with the simple meaning of the verse. However, the Sages of the Talmud felt the verse had a deeper meaning because the city’s perimeter could be reached by adding together the lengths of its sides, which are clearly stated in the text. Furthermore, it is seemingly unnecessary to know the entire perimeter of the city. Therefore, the Sages explained that the text is teaching us that the city’s perimeter is equal in number to the rows of the righteous who abide in God’s presence. The term פַּרְסֵי used in that passage will be discussed below.
Although <i>Rashi</i> did not clarify the difference between the literal meaning and the <i>Aggadic</i> reading in his commentary to Tractate <i>Sanhedrin,</i> the same teaching of Ravva is cited in Tractate <i>Sukkah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sukkah</i> 45b.</i> In his commentary to that source, <i>Rashi</i> focuses on this matter, stating: “The perimeter… will be 18,000.” The end of the verse is “God is there.”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is because of these words that our Sages inferred that the verse could also refer to the rows of the righteous who abide in God’s presence.</i> Although the verse is speaking of Jerusalem of the Ultimate Future, it is also understood as teaching that in the abode of the Divine presence, there will be 18,000 rows surrounding the Divine presence. This [i.e. the 18,000 rows] refers to the righteous who are located closer to Him than the ministering angels, as implied by the verses, “At that time, it will be asked of Yaakov and of Israel, ‘What has God wrought?’,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bamidbar</i> 23:23. Note <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to that verse, which explains that the angels will ask this question of the Jews, because at that time, i.e., in the Ultimate Future, the Jews will be showered with greater Divine love.</i> “Her merchandise will be for those who sit before God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yeshayahu</i> 23:18.</i> and “Your eyes will behold your Master.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 30:20.</i>
Thus, <i>Rashi</i> clarifies the matter, noting that although the verse is describing Jerusalem of the Future, it also teaches this concept. It is possible that <i>Rashi</i> did not mention this concept in his commentary to <i>Sanhedrin,</i> because he relied on what he had written in his commentary to <i>Sukkah. Sukkah</i> is part of the Order of <i>Moed</i> and thus precedes the Order of <i>Nezikin</i> in which <i>Sanhedrin</i> appears. To explain: Our Sages teach<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shabbos</i> 31a.</i> that the Orders of the <i>Mishnah</i> follow the pattern alluded to in the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yeshayahu</i> 33:6.</i> “The faith of your times will provide the strength of your salvation….” In this verse, the phrase “your times” refers to the Order of <i>Moed,</i> and the word, “salvation,” refers to the Order of <i>Nezikin.</i> Since the phrase, “your times,” precedes the word, “salvation,” we know that the Order of <i>Moed</i> precedes the Order of <i>Nezikin</i>. Support for the concept that <i>Rashi</i> relied on his commentary to <i>Sukkah</i> when composing his commentary to <i>Sanhedrin</i> can be drawn from the fact that, in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> he did not cite the verses he cited in <i>Sukkah</i> as prooftexts in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> when explaining that there are righteous men who abide near the Divine presence. Rather than repeating himself, <i>Rashi</i> relied on his previous statements. Similarly, in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> he only mentioned the idea that the verse refers to the Jerusalem Above. Obviously, however, <i>Rashi</i> accepts the general principle that a verse should never be taken entirely beyond its simple meaning.
Moreover, it is possible to say that in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> it is not necessary for <i>Rashi</i> to explain that the verse should be understood according to its simple meaning, as well as according to its <i>Aggadic</i> interpretation<i>,</i> because Ravva’s words themselves indicate that the verse should be understood literally.
To explain: The fact that Ravva mentions 18,000 <i>parsaos</i> represents a difficulty, for the verse uses measuring rods as a measure of length, not <i>parsaos.</i> Therefore, Ravva should have mentioned measuring rods, not <i>parsaos.</i> This discrepancy can be resolved by explaining that the wording of the verse alludes to its twofold meaning: According to its literal meaning, the verse is discussing the city’s perimeter, stating that it will measure 18,000 measuring rods. Nevertheless, the verse does not explicitly mention measuring rods, enabling its meaning to be expanded to include its <i>Aggadic</i> interpretation: that the city will have a parallel in the spiritual realms, where the 18,000 rows of righteous men surround the Divine presence. These rows are measured in <i>parsaos,</i> and not in measuring rods, for in the spiritual realms, measurements are taken in <i>parsaos,</i> as is obvious from <i>Sefer Heichalos.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sefer Heichalos,</i> ch. 12. <i>Sefer Heichalos</i> is an early Kabbalistic text, dating from the Mishnaic period.</i> Measurements are taken in <i>parsaos</i> in the spiritual realms because they are the largest measure of length referred to in Talmudic literature. Thus, when the liturgical hymn for <i>Parshas Shekalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is referring to <i>Az Rayisi,</i> a hymn composed by Rav Elazar HaKalir.</i> mentions the different measures of length, it begins with <i>parsaos,</i> for they are the largest measure.
On this basis, it can be said that Ravva’s statement itself indicates that a distinction can be made between the verse’s simple meaning and its <i>Aggadic</i> interpretation, for the verse mentions measuring rods and Ravva mentions <i>parsaos.</i> Therefore, it is not necessary for <i>Rashi</i> to highlight this point. Tractate <i>Sukkah,</i> by contrast, does not quote Ravva as discussing <i>parsaos.</i> The rationale is that, in contrast to the passage in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> the passage in <i>Sukkah</i> is not a collection of <i>Aggadic</i> teachings. Ravva’s teaching is only cited there as a basis for a question concerning the lesson preceding it. True, in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> Ravva’s statement is also cited as a basis for a question. Nevertheless, that entire passage is of an <i>Aggadic</i> nature, and so the author brought the <i>Aggadic</i> text in full. Since <i>parsaos</i> are not mentioned in Tractate <i>Sukkah,</i> there <i>Rashi</i> felt it necessary to elaborate and explain that the verse’s <i>Aggadic</i> interpretation does not contradict its simple meaning.
On this basis, <i>Tosafos’</i> commentary to the passage in <i>Sukkah</i> should be discussed. <i>Tosafos</i> emphasizes that <i>parsaos</i> should not be mentioned in Ravva’s teaching, since <i>parsaos</i> are not mentioned in the verse. It would appear that <i>Tosafos’</i> intent is that the term <i>parsaos</i> should not be mentioned in <i>San­hedrin</i> either. <i>Tosafos</i> does not mention this point in <i>Sanhedrin.</i> However, this is not unusual, because there are few glosses from <i>Tosafos</i> for that chapter in <i>Sanhedrin.</i> According to our understanding, however, <i>parsaos</i> should be mentioned in Ravva’s teaching even though the verse mentions measuring rods. For Ravva is interpreting the verse in an <i>Aggadic</i> sense, as referring to the rows of the righteous surrounding the Divine presence. As such, we should follow the guidance in Tractate <i>Shevuos,</i> which instructs us to,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shevuos</i> 31a.</i> “Derive insight from one source and apply it in another context.” In our context, the insight from the verse can be applied to the rows of angels, where the wording <i>parsaos</i> is appropriate. In Tractate <i>Sukkah,</i> the term <i>parsaos</i> is not appropriate. Accordingly, it is not mentioned there. However, the term <i>parsaos</i> is mentioned in Tractate <i>Sanhedrin</i>, because that tractate is of an <i>Aggadic</i> nature.
According to our first explanation of why <i>Rashi</i> did not mention the simple meaning of the verse in his commentary to <i>Sanhedrin,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that <i>Rashi</i> relied on his commentary to the passage in <i>Sukkah.</i></i> it seems that the text in <i>Sukkah</i> should also mention <i>parsaos.</i> The rationale is that measurements in the spiritual realms are made in <i>parsaos</i>, as mentioned above. The reason this version is not found in the text of the Talmud, is that the scribe who composed the standard version saw <i>Tosafos’</i> statements and therefore omitted the word <i>parsaos.</i> In <i>Sanhedrin,</i> by contrast, there is no commentary by <i>Tosafos</i> on this passage. Hence, the word was not omitted. In truth, as <i>Tosafos</i> mentioned, the word <i>par­saos</i> was originally found in both passages, and – according to our understanding – belongs in both.
With the above interpretations, we have merited to explain that one should not err when studying <i>Rashi’s</i> words, as did some who wished to claim that Yechezkel’s prophecy should not be interpreted according to its simple meaning and sought to support their assertion by citing <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary in <i>Sanhedrin,</i> as stated by Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Abarbanel, Yechezkel</i> 45:1.</i> Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, however, refutes their claim, citing <i>Rashi’s</i> commentary to <i>Sukkah.</i> We elaborated, and also explained <i>Rashi’s</i> use of the term <i>parsaos,</i> and the difference between his commentary in <i>Sanhedrin</i> and in <i>Sukkah.</i> Nevertheless, fundamentally, we both shared the same intent: to emphasize that Yechezkel’s prophecy should be interpreted according to its simple meaning, as we explained in great detail. As a reader can see, the verse can be understood according to our interpretation, which is based on the words of the commentaries.
I do not assert that it is necessary to accept my entire explanation. Who knows whether I am correct? I am not a prophet, nor the son of a prophet. My words do not emanate from wisdom or from a special spirit; they are nothing more than what the God of heaven revealed to me in the understanding of the words of the classic commentaries and, in particularly, the words of <i>Rashi,</i> the foremost among them. Perhaps someone with a discerning heart will understand, comprehend, and conceptualize these ideas better than I do, and offer a different interpretation that will fit the meaning of the words more closely. After all, the three commentaries on whose work our work is based (i.e. <i>Rashi, Radak</i>, and Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel) do not always agree regarding every matter.
Nevertheless, since the commentary given does enable us to understand Yechezkel’s prophecy according to its simple meaning, there is no reason to reject it. It is possible that it is true. If, however, someone else will offer a different explanation of the verses that presents their simple meaning more effectively, I will happily bow my head in acceptance of it. However, it is impossible to say that the verses can be understood only in an abstract, metaphoric sense, when there is a way to understand them according to their simple meaning.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">From the words of Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel (in his commentary to <i>Yechezkel</i> 45:1, portions of which are cited below), it is possible to surmise why <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> elaborates at such great length regarding the necessity to understand Yechezkel’s prophecy according to its simple meaning. In that source, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel rebuts the argument advanced by Christian scholars who maintained that the prophecy should only be interpreted in a metaphoric, spiritual context, but that it was never meant to be understood in physical terms. Seemingly, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> also felt it necessary to negate that interpretation.</i>
There will be three portions of the <i>Terumah Gedolah:</i> a) <i>terumas hakohanim,</i> in which the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> will be located, b) <i>terumas haleviim,</i> and c) the <i>terumah</i> of the city of Jerusalem. Each area will be a square, measuring 25,000 measuring rods by 25,000 measuring rods. As explained in the preface,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">P. 20.</i> the angel’s measuring rod was 6 cubits long.
