Thus, the area of each of these sections will only measure 150,000 cubits by 150,000 cubits. Now, a <i>mil</i> equals 2000 cubits. Thus, these areas will measure 75 <i>mil</i> by 75 <i>mil,</i> as we stated at the outset. Thus, since a <i>parsa</i> is equal to 4 <i>mil,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The hymn entitled <i>Az Rayisi</i> composed by Rav Elazar HaKalir.</i> each of the three parts of the <i>Terumah Gedolah</i> will be a square, which is just a bit less than 19 <i>parsaos,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">18.75 <i>parsaos</i> to be precise.</i> as stated in the <i>piyyutim.</i>
This reckoning concurs precisely with the <i>mishnah</i> in <i>Taanis</i>, ch. 1,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taanis</i> 10a.</i> which states that the prayers for rain were delayed 15 days after the conclusion of the Sukkos festival, to enable the last of the pilgrims to reach his home on the Euphrates River. The Euphrates is in the north of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, and serves as a border between <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and Babylonia, as stated in <i>Kiddushin,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Kiddushin</i> 71b.</i> and as stated by <i>Rashi</i> in his commentary to the verse in the beginning of <i>Yehoshua:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yehoshua</i> 1:4.</i> “From the desert… until the great river, the Euphrates.” There are seven tribes whose portions are north of the Great <i>Terumah.</i> Thus, a festive pilgrim who sojourns in the city,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., Jerusalem of the Future. See the map of the future division of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> above.</i> which is in the southern portion of the Great <i>Terumah,</i> will have to pass through eight portions of land on his journey home. Each of these portions is 75 <i>mil</i> wide. If we round up the amount of <i>parsaos</i> in each area, the pilgrim will have to travel through 8 * 19 <i>parsaos,</i> or 152 <i>parsaos,</i> on his way home. However, as we explained above, each <i>mil</i> only equals 18.75 <i>parsaos.</i> Therefore, we now have to deduct the extra .25 <i>parsaos</i> we added to each <i>mil.</i> If we multiply .25 <i>parsaos</i> by the 8 <i>mil</i> the pilgrim is traveling through, we see that we have to deduct 2 <i>parsaos</i> from the 152 <i>parsaos</i> arrived at above, leaving us with a total of 150 <i>parsaos</i>. Our Sages calculated that a person walks 40 <i>mil,</i> i.e., 10 <i>parsaos,</i> a day.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Pesachim</i> 94a.</i> Thus, it will be a 15 day journey from Jerusalem to the Euphrates.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahadura Basra. Tosafos Yom Tov</i> elaborates on this concept in his commentary to <i>Taanis</i> 1:3. In that source, he also explains that there are <i>halachic</i> rationales that would allow a person to continue walking on <i>Shabbos.</i> Thus, the <i>Mishnah’s</i> words “a fifteen-day journey” could be understood literally.</i>
The narrative in <i>Yechezkel</i> states that Jerusalem will occupy a square measuring 4500 measuring rods by 4500 measuring rods (i.e. 27,000 cubits by 27,000 cubits, or 13 ½ <i>mil</i> by 13 ½ <i>mil</i>), a distance that a person could walk in about a third of a day. It is not at all far-fetched to say that the city will be that large. Indeed, Nineveh was,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yonah</i> 3:3.</i> “a three-day walk [wide].” How much more so can this be said of Jerusalem in the time of the Redemption, when many nations will stream to her. Certainly, it could be a ninth of that size. Indeed, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Abarbanal, Yechezkel</i> 45:1.</i> states that the city of Egypt was larger than that in his day.
To emphasize that the prophecies should be understood according to their simple meaning, Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel also cites Yirmeyahu’s prophecy concerning Jerusalem in the Ultimate Future, saying:<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yirmeyahu</i> 31:37-39.</i> “ ‘Behold, days are coming,’ says God, ‘when the city shall be built… from the Tower of Chananel to the Corner Gate… and the entire Valley of the Corpses… as far as the brook of Kidron, to the corner of the Horse Gate toward the east, it shall be consecrated to God.’ ” Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel asks: The names of these places are well-known landmarks in the surroundings of Jerusalem. Can it be said that the names of these landmarks should be understood in any way other than their simple meaning?
Moreover, in this prophecy, Yechezkel speaks of the order of the sacrifices and how they will be offered, the dedication of the Altar, the service of the <i>kohanim</i> and the Levites, the portion to be given to the <i>Nasi,</i> his offering of his sacrifices, the presents he will give to his sons and servants, the division of the land into tribal portions and the gates of Jerusalem, mentioning them by name. All this clearly indicates that the prophecies are meant to be understood literally. Moreover, many of the matters described here by the prophet existed in the First and Second <i>Batei HaMikdash.</i> If then, those matters existed in a physical sense, why not say that the same applies to the Ultimate Future as well? Why say, that the prophecies must be understood only abstractly and metaphorically? Instead, “we will go forth in the name of God, our Lord,”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Michah</i> 4:5.</i> maintaining the faith that this prophecy is to be understood simply, and that Yechezkel delivered it in anticipation of the Ultimate Future. The above are the words of Rav Yitzchak Abarbanel, who elaborated at even greater length to demonstrate that this prophecy should be understood in simple, physical terms. We have condensed his words.
Since the prophecy can be understood in a simple way, there is no need to seek abstract, spiritual interpretations. Instead, we should believe in its simple meaning. Certainly, the prophecy contains a deeper, spiritual interpretation. Nevertheless, it will unquestionably be fulfilled in a physical sense, like all the other prophecies that were delivered since the beginning of human history. All of these matters have a deeper, spiritual meaning, even though they were also actualized in a physical sense, as the <i>Zohar,</i> states in <i>Parshas Behaaloscha;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Zohar,</i> Vol. III, p. 152a.</i> consult that source. First and foremost, we must understand a verse simply; afterwards, we may contemplate its inner, spiritual meaning.
Look at God’s Sanctuary which Moshe fashioned in the desert, and God’s <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> which Shlomoh built. Did they not have an inner dimension that paralleled the Sanctuary in the spiritual realms? Our Sages’ interpret<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Taanis</i> 5a.</i> the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tehillim</i> 122:3.</i> “Like a city in which all joined hands,” as meaning that Jerusalem is a joined city. Jerusalem above, in the spiritual realms, is linked to its material counterpart, the earthly city of Jerusalem. Nevertheless, when the Torah and the prophets described the city and the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, they were focusing on its existence in actual fact, in this material world. The same applies to Yechezkel’s prophecy. There will come a time when this prophecy will be fulfilled on the material plane. Since it has not yet happened, we must conclude that it is a prophecy for the future. It cannot be said that Yechezkel’s prophecy was fulfilled with the building of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, because as we have demonstrated, this prophecy differs from that structure in almost every aspect. Because of those differences, it cannot be said that this prophecy is describing the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.
Since this prophecy has not yet been fulfilled, and like all the other prophecies, the intent is that it will be fulfilled on the material plane, we are therefore obligated to thank God for the miracles and wonders He wrought for us and our ancestors and trust that, “the vision is yet [intended] for an appointed time; it speaks concerning the end and will not fail to come true; though its [fulfillment] delays, wait for it; because it will surely come.”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chabakuk</i> 2:3.</i> Our city will be rebuilt, our Temple will be constructed, and God will cause His presence to dwell among us eternally. Yechezkel relates in his prophecy, that after the angel departed the Holy Chamber, he measured the Temple Mount from the outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:15.</i> The prophecy continues:<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid,</i> 43:1-9.</i>
He led me to the gate that looks towards the east. Behold, the glory of the God of Israel came from the way of the east…. A spirit took me up, and brought me into the inner court; and, behold, the glory of God filled the house. I heard Him speaking to me from the house…. He said to me, “Son of man, this is the place of My throne, and the resting place for the soles of My feet, where I will dwell in the midst of the people of Israel forever. The House of Israel will no longer defile My holy name…. by placing their threshold by My thresholds…. Now, let them put away their harlotry… and I will dwell in their midst forever.”
Yechezkel is prophesying about a building that was measured, and which he saw with its design, its exits, and its entrances. He related that the word of God came to him and a voice told him: “This is the place of My throne, and the resting place for the soles of My feet.” The basis of this translation is that the word <i>es</i> in the phrase <i>es makom kisi,</i> shares the meaning of the term <i>zeh,</i> “this is.” This translation is also reflected in the <i>Targum Yonason.</i> Thus, this verse is teaching that the physical <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> is aligned opposite God’s throne in the spiritual realms, and that the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> serves as His footstool. This is similar to Yeshayahu’s statement:<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yeshayahu</i> 66:1.</i> “Thus says God, ‘The heavens are My throne and the earth, My footstool.’ ” The <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> is the place where God will dwell among the Jewish people. In that future era, they will no longer desecrate His holy name. Therefore God states, “I will dwell in their midst forever.”
Yechezkel relates similar prophecies in ch. 37.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 37:26-28.</i> I will discuss this matter further in the conclusion of this text.
“The gates of the city will be named after the tribes of Israel. There will be three gates northward; one gate of Reuven, one gate of Yehudah, one gate of Levi. At the east, there will be… three gates; and one gate of Yosef, one gate of Benyamin, one gate of Dan. At the south, there will be… three gates; one gate of Shimon, one gate of Yissachar, one gate of Zevulun. At the west, there will be… three gates; one gate of Gad, one gate of Asher, one gate of Naftali.”<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:21-34.</i>

Chapter 81

“And the open space of the city will be 250 [measuring rods] in the north, 250 [measuring rods] in the south, 250 [measuring rods] in the east, and 250 [measuring rods] in the west.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:17. The additions are taken from <i>Ras</i>hi’s commentary.</i> The city itself covers an area of 4500 measuring rods by 5000 measuring rods. With its empty space, the city is a square, 5000 measuring rods by 5000 measuring rods.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For the addition of 250 measuring rods on the east and the west creates a square of 5000 measuring rods.</i> The open space fills the width of the strip of land in which the city is located.

Chapter 82

“The remainder [of the 25,000 measuring rod] length along the sanctified [portion of] the <i>Terumah...</i>” – of the 25,000 measuring rods of length, the city occupied only 5,000 – “10,000 to the east and 10,000 to the west” – should be used for fields. “Its produce will serve as food for the workers of the city.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:18.</i>

Chapter 83

“The entire <i>Terumah</i> 25,000 [measuring rods] by 25,000 [measuring rods] square shall be set aside as a sanctified portion”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:20.</i> – for the <i>kohanim,</i> the Levites – “and the property of the city.” These areas were already discussed, each in its place. “The remaining portion [of the land] shall be for the <i>Nasi,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the High Priest; alternatively, the king, the <i>Mashiach</i> (<i>Rashi</i>)<i>.</i></i> [his portion being] on either side of the sanctified portion of the <i>Terumah</i> and the property of the city along its width of 25,000 [measuring rods] of the <i>Terumah</i> to the eastern and western borders.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned, the <i>Terumah</i> was a square, 25,000 measuring rods by 25,000 measuring rods. It was located in the center of one of the 13 strips of land into which <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> will be divided. The portions of land to the east and the west will be granted to the <i>Nasi.</i></i> [The area] on the western border parallel to the portions [allocated to the tribes] shall be for the <i>Nasi.</i> The consecrated <i>Terumah</i> and the Sanctuary of the House will be in it. The property of the Levites and the property of the city will be in the portion that will be for the <i>Nasi.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechez­kel</i> 28:21-22.</i>
The prophet is explaining that the three areas, i.e. the portion of the <i>kohanim,</i> the portion of the Levites, and the portion of the city, will fill the 25,000 measuring rod width of the Great <i>Terumah.</i> However, the Great <i>Terumah</i> runs across the entire width of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> until the sea. Although these three areas filled the width of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, they did not fill its length, as we wrote at the outset.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">P. 21ff.</i> What remains on the eastern and western sides of the consecrated <i>Terumah</i> will be given to the <i>Nasi.</i> Thus, these three portions will be in the middle of the portion allocated to the <i>Nasi.</i>
This is also reflected in the prophet’s previous statements:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 45:7.</i> “The portion to be given to the <i>Nasi</i> will be on either side of the consecrated <i>Terumah</i> and the property of the city, facing the consecrated <i>Terumah</i> and the property of the city, from the western border, extending westward, and from the eastern border, eastward, its length corresponding to one of the portions [allocated to the tribes] from the western border to the eastern border.”
עה – The Temple Mount is 500 measuring rods by 500 measuring rods. It has five gates: two in the south, one in the west, one in the north, and one gate in the east.
עו – The 50 cubits of open space around the city.
עז – The portion from the east of the Temple Mount to the eastern border of the consecrated <i>Terumah</i>, an area of 12,250 measuring rods by 10,000 measuring rods. There was a similar portion extending from the west of the Temple Mount to the western border. From the north of the Temple Mount to the northern border of the consecrated <i>Terumah,</i> there were 4,750 measuring rods. There was a similar portion extending from the south of the Temple Mount to the southern border. All of this land was given to the <i>kohanim,</i> the servants of the Sanctuary who draw close to serve God. It is for their homes.
עח – The portion of the Levites. This area corresponded to that of the <i>kohanim.</i> It occupied a strip of land 10,000 measuring rods wide and 25,000 measuring rods long. It was given to the Levites for their needs and for their homes.
עט – The 20 chambers of the Levites, located next to the <i>kohanim’s</i> property. The gatekeepers lived in these chambers.
פ – The square of the city, 4,500 measuring rods by 4,500 measuring rods. The gates of the city: three to the north, three to the east, three to the south, and three to the west.
פא – The open space around the city, 250 measuring rods in all four directions.
פב – The area to the east of the open space around the city until the eastern border of this portion, 10,000 measuring rods by 5,000 measuring rods; a similar area to the west of the open space around the city until the western border of this portion. In it were fields, “whose produce served as food for the workers of the city.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:18.</i>
פג – The <i>Nasi’s</i> portion on either side of the consecrated <i>Terumah</i> that was given to the <i>kohanim,</i> the Levites, and designated for the property of the city, from the western border westward and from the eastern border eastward. Its length is equivalent to the length of one of the tribal portions of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, from the western border of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to the eastern border of <i>Eretz Yisrael.</i>

Concluding Statements

“[The portions of] the remaining tribes: From the eastern edge to the western edge, Benyamin…, Shimon…, Yissachar…, Zevulun…, Gad.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:23-27. In the preface, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> had mentioned the seven tribes that will be given a portion to the north of the <i>Terumah.</i> Here, he mentions those whose portion will be to the south of it.</i> These tribes were given their portions south of the Great <i>Terumah.</i>
See our opening statements.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">P. 21.</i> There it was stated that seven tribes received their ancestral portions north of the Great <i>Terumah.</i> This can possibly clarify the version of the <i>Sifri</i> favored by Rabbi Klonymus, and cited by <i>Rashi</i> in his commentary to <i>Parshas Shoftim:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, Devarim</i> 18:2.</i>
<b>He </b>(the tribe of Levi) <b>will not receive an ancestral heritage – </b>This refers to the ancestral heritage granted to the five tribes; <b>in the midst of his brethren – </b>This refers to the ancestral heritage granted to the seven tribes.” The preceding verse states that the <i>kohanim</i> and the Levites will not receive a portion of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> like the other tribes. The intent of the <i>Midrash</i> is that this will also apply in the Ultimate Future, when the tribes will receive equal ancestral portions: five [tribes will have their portions] on one side of the Great <i>Terumah</i> and seven [tribes will have their portions] on the other side [of the Great <i>Terumah</i>]. Even in that future time, the <i>kohanim</i> and the Levites will not receive a tribal portion. Instead, “God will be their portion.” They will be given the portion in the <i>Terumah</i> to be dedicated to God, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Sections 77-79.</i>
“ ‘This is the land that you will allot as an inheritance to the tribes of Israel and these are their divisions,’ spoke God, the Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 48:29.</i> The prophecy that the land will be divided equally among the twelve tribes, with each tribe receiving an equal portion, like rows in a vineyard was never realized from the time God’s people entered the land onward. Therefore, Yechezkel’s prophecy serves as proof that ultimately our people who have been dispersed in exile will return to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> from the four corners of the earth.
Rabbi Akiva stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sanhedrin</i> 110b.</i> that in the Ultimate Future, the Ten Tribes will not return to <i>Eretz Yisrael.</i> This does not contradict the prophecy mentioned above, for as <i>Rashi</i> explains,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, Sanhedrin</i> 110b.</i> Rabbi Akiva was only referring to the generation that was exiled: “This does not refer to their children and their descendants, but only to those who were exiled. [The exiled] do not possess a portion [in the land],<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i> is teaching that even at the time of the Resurrection of the Dead, those members of the Ten Tribes who were exiled will not merit to arise and receive a portion of <i>Eretz Yisrael,</i> as is evident from the discussion in <i>Sanhedrin, loc. cit.</i></i> because they were utterly wicked men. Their children and their coming generations, by contrast, merit a portion in the land, and convey that right [to their descendants].” [The Talmud’s continuation of this discussion proves that Rabbi Akiva did not mean that the descendants of the Ten Tribes would not receive a portion in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>:]
Ravva bar bar Chanah said in the name of Rabbi Yochanan: Rabbi Akiva departed from his ways of piety, as indicated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yirmeyahu</i> 3:12.</i> “Go and call out these words to the north,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The words, "to the north," are referring to the descendants of the Ten Tribes, who were exiled to the north (See <i>Rashi, Yirmeyahu,</i> 3:12.).</i> saying, ‘Return O straying Israel, says God, and I will not visit My anger upon you, for I am pious, says God, and I will not bear a grudge forever.’ ”
The Sages of the Talmud did not question Rabbi Akiva’s statements based on the passage in <i>Yechezkel,</i> which discusses the division of the land into equal portions among the twelve tribes. Thus, it is evident that all the tribes will return in their entirety. Each tribe will receive an equal portion, taking their place as an independent tribe. It would not be just that only a few of one tribe return, like those of the Ten Tribes who returned on Yirmeyahu’s urging, and yet receive an equal portion of the land as a tribe with a large population. Instead, the intent is that all the members of the tribes will return, and each tribe will receive an equal portion of <i>Eretz Yisrael.</i> Nevertheless, the Talmud does not quote Yechezkel’s prophecy as proof that the Ten Tribes will return, for [even according to Rabbi Akiva] the prophecy is speaking about righteous men, not those who were exiled. Therefore, the passage cites the verse from Yirmeyahu, “Return O straying Israel,” that indicates that even the wayward and wicked among Israel will return.
The words, “I will not have My anger fall upon you, for I am pious,” indicate that according to the attribute of judgment, it could be expected that God would have His anger be visited upon the Jews. It is only as a result of His piety that God says, “I will not bear a grudge forever.” Instead, after a long exile, the Jews will be returned to <i>Eretz Yisrael.</i> The words, “I will not bear a grudge forever,” indicate that God’s anger will persist for a long time. It will not, however, last forever, and we can look forward to the time when the prophecy will be fulfilled.
One should not interpret the Talmud as posing a question from the prophecy of Yirmeyahu – who preceded Yechezkel – because Yirmehahu’s prophecy was directed to the members of the Ten Tribes who themselves were exiled from the land. However, that is impossible, for Yirmeyahu did not begin his prophecy until many years after the generation that had been exiled passed on. To explain: The Ten Tribes were exiled in the sixth year of King Chizkiyahu’s reign.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>II Melachim</i> 18:10.</i> King Chizkiyahu’s reign continued for 23 years after the Ten Tribes were exiled.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This can be deduced from <i>II Melachim</i> 18:13.</i> Menashe, Chizkiyahu’s son, ruled for 52 years.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>II Melachim</i> 21:1.</i> Menashe’s son, Amon, ruled for 2 years.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:19.</i> Amon was succeeded by his son, Yoshiyahu. Yirmeyahu’s prophecy began in the 13th year of King Yoshiyahu’s reign.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yirmeyahu</i> 1:2.</i> Thus, there were 90 years between the time the Ten Tribes were exiled and Yirmeyahu’s prophecy. Since, “the days of our lifespan are 70 years,”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tehillim</i> 90:10.</i> it can be assumed that the members of the Ten Tribes who were themselves exiled had already passed on. Moreover, certainly only those members of the Ten Tribes who had reached the age for which they would be considered liable for punishment before they were exiled would not be included in the Resurrection of the Dead. Thus, the Talmud is not using chronological evidence as its argument. Rather, by basing its statement on the phrase, “Return, O straying Israel,” the Talmud is inferring that even the wayward and wicked among the Jews will return and, hence, is questioning Rabbi Akiva’s statement.
This is also implied by <i>Rashi</i> who, in his commentary to that passage, states that the verse implies that, “all Jews will return.” The word “all” implies even the “straying,” and certainly, the righteous. Even Rabbi Akiva does not differ. He never entertained the thought that the descendants of the Ten Tribes would not return, for it is not logical to assume that their descendants, who did not sin, will perish because of their ancestors’ sins, and not return to their land.
For this reason, the Talmud does not challenge Rabbi Akiva’s statements by citing this prophecy, which indicates that the Ten Tribes will return to <i>Eretz Yisrael,</i> or by citing a previous prophecy of Yechezkel:<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 37:19.</i> “Behold, I will take the staff of Yosef, that is in the hand of Ephraim and the tribes of Israel, his companions, and I will place them together with the staff of Yehudah, and I will make them one staff.” This prophecy implies that the Ten Tribes will return to the kingdom of Yehudah, and become one with the other tribes. In this verse, the word עץ, is translated as “staff,” and refers to a staff of leadership. The verse implies that all the people will return, and thus the Jewish nation will be fit to accept the sovereignty of the House of David. This concept is also reflected in a later verse in that same prophecy:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:21-22.</i> “Behold, I will take the Children of Israel from among the nations to which they have sojourned…. I will make them into one nation in the land with one king…. They will no longer be two nations… two kingdoms.” This prophecy was not fulfilled in the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> when many rebelled against their rulers and sought the kingship for themselves. True, the Jews were not divided into two nations, but they were divided into many factions.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">By citing these verses, <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> is emphasizing that this prophecy was not fulfilled in the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, but rather will be fulfilled in the future with the coming of <i>Mashiach</i>.</i>
The prophecy continues:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 37:23-24.</i> “Nor shall they defile themselves any more with their idols, nor with their detestable things, nor with any of their transgressions; I will save them… David… shall be king… they will follow My judgments” – i.e., the judgments that they despised previously and exchanged for idol worship – “and perform them,” observing the judgments, the statutes of our holy Torah, that they despised in the days of Yechezkel. This prophecy thus serves as testimony to the eternal nature of the Torah’s laws, indicating that they will not change. “They will dwell in the land…, they and their children, and their descendants forever.”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:25.</i> We were exiled from <i>Eretz Yisrael,</i> and have not yet dwelt in it. Thus, this prophecy has not yet come to fruition. Ultimately, it will. We will return and dwell in our land for all time.
The prophecy continues: “My servant David shall be their prince forever.” This prophecy was not fulfilled in the era of the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash,</i> when the kings were the descendants of the Chashmonaim, who were <i>kohanim.</i> True, initially, there was a governor from the tribe of Yehudah, as mentioned in the words of Ezra,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ezra</i> 6:7.</i> Chaggai,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Chaggai</i> 1:1, 2:2.</i> and Zechariah,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Zechariah</i> 4:6-10.</i> but his rule was not perpetuated.
The prophecy further states:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 37:26.</i> “I will establish a covenant of peace with them; it will be an eternal covenant with them.” This is yet to be fulfilled. On the contrary, due to our sins, our people do not enjoy peace. That verse continues, “I will multiply you.” This prophecy also has not yet been fulfilled, as we are the smallest among the nations.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Devarim</i> 7:7.</i> Yechezkel’s prophecy continues with the promises:<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 37:26-27.</i> “I will place My Sanctuary in their midst forever. My presence will rest upon them; I will be their God, and they shall be My people.” Similarly, this promise has yet to be fulfilled. On the contrary, in our era we ask, “Why is it said among the nations, ‘Where is their God?”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tehillim</i> 115:2.</i> Certainly, we are His nation and He is our God, but the point of the prophecy is that the relationship will be obvious, that Divine favor will be manifest upon us to the extent that the nations of the world will recognize that the God of Israel is the true God, and that Israel is His nation, as the prophecy concludes,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yechezkel</i> 37:28.</i> “The nations shall know that I, God, sanctify Israel, since My sanctuary will be in their midst forever.” The implication is that the nations of the world will realize and acknowledge that, “I will be their God.” The nations’ realization will stem from the awareness that, “My sanctuary will be in their midst forever.”
Where is the House of our holiness and glory? Can it be said that this prophecy was fulfilled with the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>? Did the Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> last forever? On the contrary, it has been destroyed for more than twice as long as the time when it stood.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Second <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> stood for 420 years, and <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> composed his text in 5362 (1602), 1532 years after its destruction.</i> Instead, this prophecy concerns the Ultimate Future, when God will dwell among us for all eternity, and all the nations will recognize that God is our God and we are His nation.
This prophecy indicates that the Ten Tribes will return, that God will dwell among Israel forever, and that the people of Israel will no longer make God’s Sanctuary impure. [The two prophecies are interrelated:]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See the Introduction, page 12.</i> because the people of Israel will no longer make His Sanctuary impure, God will dwell among them forever. In that future era, the land will be divided among all twelve tribes of Israel, as explained in sec. 80 above.
With these words, this holy task has been completed,
our explanation of the design of God’s new House.
As we have merited to arrange it,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i>‘s words recall the <i>Chasal Siddur Pesach</i> hymn that concludes the Pesach <i>Seder</i>.</i>
passage after passage, whether small or large,
so too, may we merit to build it.
And [may we merit] to sing in it with thanksgiving and praise
accompanied by the instruments of David the man of God
and we will fill our mouths with song
in the service unique to our tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yom Tov Lipmann Heller, author of <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> , was a Levite. With these words, he expresses his prayer to resume the function his tribe performed in the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i>
speedily, in the near future.

Explanations of Problematic Passages in I Melachim

To Fulfill the Promise Made in the Introduction to Explain what is Enigmatic in King Shlomoh’s Building
“He built the House of the Forest of Lebanon.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This building was a royal palace built by King Shlomoh, and was not part of the <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>. True, our Sages (<i>Yoma</i> 39b; <i>Pirkei d’Rabbi Eliezer,</i> ch. 49) used the term “the House of the Forest of Lebanon” to refer to the <i>Beis HaMikdash.</i> Nevertheless, the building itself was a palace used by the king.</i> It was one hundred cubits long, fifty cubits wide, and thirty cubits high, [supported by] four rows of cedar columns.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:2.</i> Each row of cedar columns was 100 cubits long, and there were 50 cubits between the first row and the last one, this being the width of the palace. “There were cedar beams (כרתות) above the pillars.” The pillars had openings between them like exedras do. Thus, the beams were like lintels over the door posts.
These beams also served as support for the boards, the צְּלָעוֹת mentioned in the following verse:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:3.</i> “the boards that were on the pillars.” [This phrase teaches numerous details about the building’s structure.] The boards are described as being, “on the pillars,” and not on the beams, because the pillars served as the main supports for the building. The beams merely strengthened the structure by joining one pillar to another so that the structure would be a single entity. The beams lay on their sides and did not stand upright. Thus, the phrase, “the boards that were on the pillars,” should be understood as meaning “the boards were on the beams that were on the pillars.” The beams were not mentioned in this verse because they were secondary.
Alternatively, it is possible to say that the beams were not positioned above the pillars, and did not lie on the pillars. Instead, they were connected to the tops of the pillars, and were on the same level as the pillars. Rather than serve as a lintel atop a doorpost, the beams were like long planks that joined one pillar to another. They were thick, of the same breadth as the pillars, joining the pillars to create a level surface on top of them. According to this interpretation, the phrase, “the boards that were on the pillars,” can be understood simply: The boards rested on the pillars, because the beams were on the same level as the pillars. If that is true, the phrase “There were cedar beams above the pillars” must be interpreted as “on the higher portion of the pillars” and not as explained above.
The צלעות, the boards, on top of the pillars, were fashioned from the same wood as were the pillars. Thus, it was as if there were pillars lying on the beams and pillars. This is indicated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:6.</i> “And pillars and timber on top of them,” which <i>Rashi</i> interprets as referring to the wood that served as the boards.
The palace had a ceiling made of cedar wood. The ceiling contained 45 cedar planks, which were placed on these boards. This ceiling was divided into three portions for the three spaces between the four rows of pillars.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashi, I Melachim</i> 7:4.</i> Each of these spaces contained fifteen cedar planks, their length extending over the width of the structure, from one row of pillars to the next. The width of the cedar planks, as they were placed one next to the other, ran across the length of the structure. The fifteen planks were not long enough to extend over the entire 100-cubit length of the structure. Therefore, shorter cedar beams were placed at the end of the structure. These beams were placed lengthwise, along the length of the structure. They were fashioned like a lintel at their ends so that they could be attached to the beams fashioned for them on either side.
<b>“This is the craftsmanship of the stands”</b> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:28. This verse is referring to the stands for the basins. In the First <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>, Shlomoh had ten basins made. The basins rested on these stands. The stands had wheels so that they could be moved easily.</i>
He made “four copper wheels for each stand.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:30.</i> Each stand resembled a large wagon.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the stands resemble carriages in which the basins were placed.</i> “Each wheel was one-and-one-half cubits high. The wheels were fashioned like chariot wheels.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:32-33.</i> Each wheel was made up of two wheels, one inside the other,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi, I Melachim</i> 7:33. <i>Rashi</i> based his commentary on this verse on <i>Yechezkel</i> 1:16.</i> in a crisscross. Thus, the person moving the stand could easily turn it in any direction without having to maneuver it extensively.
There were 4 cubits between the wheel on the east and the one on the west, and the wheel on the north and the one on the south. Thus, the bases of the stands were square, not rectangular like our wagons. This is intimated by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:27.</i> “four cubits was the length of one base and four cubits its breadth.”
A long, thick rod was inserted in both of the northern wheels, running from east to west, to serve as the wheels’ axle. A similar axle was inserted in the southern wheels. The inner wheels also had an axle inserted in the eastern and western wheels, running from north to south. These axles are referred to as יְדוֹתָם in the narrative.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>33.</i>
That narrative also mentions ְגַבֵּיהֶם, “their hubs,” referring to the center of these wheels. The axles revolved in these hubs. The word ְגַבֵּיהֶם derives from the word <i>govah,</i> which literally means “height.” The hubs are referred to as ְגַבֵּיהֶם because they are the highest part of a wheel when the wheel lies on the ground. Another reason why the text uses the term ְגַבֵּיהֶם to refer to the wheels’ hubs is because the center of a circle can be called its height, for all of its sides originate from it.
The verse uses the word ְחִשֻּׁקֵיהֶם to refer to the wheels’ rims. The rims connect the spokes, which are referred to as חִשֻּׁרֵיהֶם. The term חִשֻּׁקֵיהֶם is also used when the Torah discusses the <i>Mishkan:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shmos</i> 27:10.</i> “Their bands (חִשֻּׁקֵיהֶם) were silver.” The rim of a wheel is the part of the wheel that revolves and is in contact with the earth. Usually, wheels have an iron rim. Thus, the <i>mishnah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Keilim</i> 11:3.</i> mentions “an iron rim.” In this instance, it was not necessary for the wheels to have an iron rim, because the wheel was made entirely of copper.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Copper is both ductile and tough, making it easy to shape into wheels and sturdy enough not to need an outer iron rim.</i>
The verse uses the term <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:28.</i>וּמִסְגְּרֹת to refer to the frames that were attached to the axles on all four sides. These frames formed a square, as stated,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:31.</i> “Their frames were square, not round.” These appendages are called frames because they enclose – or frame – the stand’s axles on all four sides.
Copper planks were attached to these frames. Scripture<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.:</i>30.</i> refers to these copper planks as סַרְנֵי נְחֹשֶׁת. The root of the word סַרְנֵי is סרן, which is made up of the same letters as the word נסר, “board.” Hence, the commentaries explain that the term סַרְנֵי נְחֹשֶׁת should be understood as meaning “planks of copper.” Attached to these planks was a façade, resembling the rungs of a ladder. The rungs were attached to the surface of the planks, and were not suspended above the planks. Between each rung was a frame on which there were forms of oxen, lions, and cherubs.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the frames and the planks served as decorative coverings, hiding the wheels from view.</i> The planks reached above the frames, all the way to the wheels. They ascended on an angle, appearing like a cap, or a covering for wagons, until they reached the level of the wheels. The planks continued to be extended until they covered the wheels. However, the two sides did not meet in the center, and they were not covered from above. There, they had a round hole, a cubit and a half in diameter, over the center of the wheels. This covering was called<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>I Melachim</i> 7:31.</i> a כּוֹתֶרֶת, “a coronet.” The hole in the coronet was referred to as פי כותרת, “the opening of the coronet.” Although the word פי is normally translated as “mouth”, it can also be translated as “opening” as in the phrase, ופיה עגול מעשה כן אמה וחצי האמה, “its opening was round, like the craftsmanship of the stands, a cubit and a half in diameter.”
This hole was surrounded by a shield that rose half a cubit high. This is derived from the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:35.</i> “At the top of the stand, there was a round shield half a cubit high.” The base would be inserted into this shield. The base was a receptacle in which the basin was held, i.e., the basin would be inserted into the base, which in turn was within the shield above the round hole in the higher portion of the stand. The base would rise a cubit above this shield. This is implied by the phrase,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:31.</i> “Its opening extended a cubit from the inside of the coronet.”
Thus, each stand was three cubits high: The wheel was a cubit and a half high. The round shield was half a cubit high, and the base itself was a cubit high; it emerged from the shield. The cubit-high base was square, and it had four shafts, one on each of its four corners. This is reflected by the verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:34.</i> “There were four shafts to… each stand. The shafts emerged from the stands.” The rationale is that the bottoms of the basins narrowed so they could be inserted into the bases. To prevent them from slipping down lower than the desired height, the shafts were constructed around the bases as support.

The Closing Ode

The charm of the original ode lies in how <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> skillfully evoked subtle poetic allusions to well-loved Biblical verses and Midrashic phrases, whose pictorial and spiritual associations cannot be conjured up by their equivalents in any other language. In the tradition of the early medieval <i>paytanim,</i> or liturgical poets, our author’s contrived verbal riddles challenge the reader to navigate his way by following the above-described clues to his classical sources. Interestingly, the initial Hebrew letters of all the stanzas together spell the author’s name. In addition, every line ends with the letter <i>yud</i> – not to signify the first person, but to create a mesmerizing rhyme scheme.
My hands trust Him Who responds when I call upon Him,
When I beg and request and plead that He forgive in abundance,
For I am abashed by my transgressions: I have sinned, and am guilty of numerous iniquities.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 43:10, which associates the feelings of shame for one’s sins with knowledge of the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i>.</i>
Even before I called and opened my lips, He answered me;<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Yeshayahu</i> 65:24.</i>
The God of my father stood by me for my heart’s requests;
He graced me with understanding, granted me knowledge, and made me wise.
A man who loved the Temple Mount stood here and measured its bounds.
He shone like copper, and his hand held a flaxen cord and a measuring rod.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:3.</i>
God called the prophet Yechezkel: “Behold, O venerable one! I have brought you here
To measure the bounds of My home and abode, to banish impurity
So that I may dwell here forever – here, where they began to fulfill My commands.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 43:9. As <i>Tosafos Yom Tov</i> explains in the introduction, the repentance of the Jewish people and their observance of God’s commands will cause the Divine presence to rest in the Future <i>Beis HaMikdash</i> forever.</i>
The prophet first came to the Gate of Eison<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:15 and sec. 2 above.</i> and to the twelve stairs,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechez­kel</i> 40:6 and sec. 2 above.</i>
And God showed him the entrance hall and the cells and the pillars and the narrow windows.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:6-11, 14-16, and sections 4-7 above.</i>
Gates were positioned throughout the northern and southern facades.
He measured them, and they were like the one at the first entrance,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:23-24 and sections 9-10 above.</i>
With thirty chambers rising fifty cubits from the floor.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:17 and sec. 12 above.</i>
Smoke rose from the courtyard’s four corners, where God’s sacrifices were prepared,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 46:22 and sec. 13 above.</i>
Opposite the inner gates that were located as the prophet had been shown.
Before the Divine presence he saw a platform for the conductor of His musical praises,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:12 and sec. 26 above.</i>
And a high altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:47 and sec. 37 above.</i> and carving space for the sacrifices that were offered;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:39 and sec. 31 above.</i>
He saw chambers aloft, and compartments for those who serve in this House.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 42:13 and sec. 71 above.</i>
Facing them, he saw the walkways of the Entrance Hall, with its corridors<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:15 and sec. 50 above.</i> and two mighty columns:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 40:48-49 and sections 47-48 above.</i>
The entire structure of the Sanctuary, with its wicket entrances<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 44:1-2 and sec. 52 above.</i> for the men in charge, engraved like sapphire.
Here the High Priest stepped whispering into the Holy of Holies,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:4 and sec. 59 above.</i> the chamber of my birth.
Its gold-paneled walls were ornamented with cherubs;
its doors were engraved with young lions and palm trees.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:18-20.</i>
On the three sides of the Sanctuary were side-chambers,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:5-7 and sec. 65 above.</i> affixed in it like the curtains of a tent.
And facing them twenty cubits away were chambers;<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yechezkel</i> 41:10 and sec. 70 above.</i> and to the east of the courtyard,
Extending to the west, the <i>kohanim</i> prepared the sin-offerings.
These sights will always remain my evidence of the Holy Temple
Until it is rebuilt and the Divine presence will forever abide in our midst,
Hastening the arrival of <i>Mashiach</i>, and of the messenger Eliyahu, who will proclaim our Redemption,
with the Resurrection of which the prophet speaks, when the dead bodies will arise.
